THE RED PAGE.
Two Voices.
WILLIAM BLAKE. WALT WHITMAN.
BLAKE is damned handy to steal from.”
So said Fuseli in Blake’s own time-
which time was ’twixt 1757 and 1827.
~ . ._Whitman was born in 1819, and
died m 1892. He, soul son and heir of Blake, took
up his inheritance in another land, in happier
times.
To the world Blake was lost ’till in the fifties of
the last century. Alexander Gilchrist began, the
Rossettis continued, and Swinburne completed a
work of resurrection and interpretation which
makes plain a man, who in art and letters was
chief of his age; who saw, understood, and
imagined more than any of that age ; who reached
° U j uiore boldly and farther into the actual verities
and infinities than any who have been since.
Son of a hosier in London, Blake, apprenticed
to an engraver, painted strange, and wrote mad
things, none too modest or seemly. Used to sit
with his wife in the costume of Adam and Eve
(before the fall), claimed that the prophets Isaiah
and Ezekiel dined with him, that the Ancient of
Days looked in through his chamber window ;
came near being hanged for sedition, behaved dis-
respectfully to the patron who saved him ; roared
fir Liberty lustily and always. S° the world
spoke—often lying; and why should the world
hold such a one in any tender regard ?
But Fuseli knew and used him in life, Flaxinan
and Stoddart also. Later on, the Rossettis, both
as painters and poets. Gustave Dore took more
from him than he himself could give again to
the world. Watts sat at his feet ere he painted that
great picture of “ Mammon ” ; Carlyle struck in
one deep root : Emerson gained assurance and
bj-eadth ; and a half-score of German philosophers,
ISeitzsche the latest, found stuff for their webs in
scraps of his visions. And, there is Whitman.
V. i thout Blake, Whitman had not been.
Blake was a heretic, utter and complete as
Marcion or Arius or Manes. Any inquisitor, any
Chri V ian unrestrained, would have burned, as a
g’ood Tory Government tried to hang him. He.
pulled down worshipful virtues one after another,
stripped them of their masks and disguises, and
set up their naked, often ghastly, realities, to
shock and scandalise unnumbered congregations.
Modesty was to him a “timid and tacit pru-
rience” ; humility a “ mistrustful and mend ir-'o.is
condition.” Thankfulness, begotten of humility,
liked him no better.
Since all the riches of the world
Mxv bs gifts from the Devil or earthly kings,
I should suspect that I worshipped the Devil
If I thanked my God for worldly things.
For himself he knew “no other Christianity
and no other Gospel than the liberty of both mind
and body to exercise the Divine art of imagina-
tion.” His was a complex, seismic temperament,
and yet his life, as now bared in all its details, was
pure, laborious, honorable. But for his heresies
he might have had an early fame equal to that of
Oowper, the “teapot pietist,” or of Paine, the
dealer out of tape-yard infidelities.” In the
beginning his verse had the sweetness of Herrick,
the felicity of Keats—
Smile on our love and while thou drn west round
The sky’s blue, curtain, scatter silver dew
On every flower that closest its sweet eyes
l.n fiinely sleep. • Let the west wind sleep on
The lake. Speak silence with thy glimmering eyes
And wash the dusk with silver.
No possibility of mistaking quality there. It
shines true gold in every aspect But, *alas, there
were other things even so early :
A little black thing amongst the snow,
Crying weep ! weep ! in tones of woe.
Where are your father and mother say?
“They are gone up to the church to pray.”
At a very early date distressing complexities of
that sort intruded on his visions, though years
passed before in his strongest verse he put the
final question:
Tiger! tiger ! burning bright
In the forests of the night,
What Irtimortal hand and eye,
Framed thy fearful symmetry?
In what distant depths or skies
Burned that fire within thine eyes?
On what wings dared He aspire?
What the hand dared seize that fire ?
When the stars flung down their spears
And watered Heaven with their tears,
Did He smile His work to see ?
Did He who made the lamb make thee?”
Never since the voice out of the Whirlwind
came to that fearfully bedevilled squatter of
Chaldea have more pregnant words than these
been spoken. The question involved is not of the
origin of evil, but of power-force, living energy
unrestrained and reckless; of that “bit of the
devil,” lacking which no man or woman makes
much in the world.
In the “ Marriage of Heaven and Hell,” Blake
sets himself to answer and expound. First, he
has to state his case, and in that statement are
many amazing things
Better murder an infant in its cradle than nurse unacted
desire.
Prisons are built with stones of law ; brothels with
bricks of religion.
The reason and interpretation of which is, that
the' nursing of unacted desire is the stifling of
! >eific impulse ; the need of prisons is chiefly made
by unnatural law, and the brothel is fed by the
timid and tacit prurience of what is known as
modesty. Blake makes this last matter plainer,
according to his own light, in “ The Everlasting
Gospel.”
In the case of the Woman Taken in Adultery, a
dialogue is imagined. “What was thy love?”
asks the Forgiver. The woman answers—
Love too long from me has fled.
’Twas dark deceit to earn my bread ;
’Twas covet, or ’twas custom, or
Some trifle not worth caring for.
But thin, 0 Lord, this was my sin,
When first I let those Devils in,
in dark pretence to chastity —
Blaspheming love, blaspheming thee.
Thence rose secret adulteries,
And thence did covet also rise;
My sin thou hast forgiven me,
Canst thou forgive my blasphemy ?
The Hell Blake knows and desires to wed with
his Heaven is of active human will, desire, energy,
unrestrained by any “dark pretence.” He deemed
that he beard the banns of that marriage published,
firstly in the tumult of the uprising of the French
nation, secondly the successful revolt of the
American people. A good deal of the confusion
of his later work came through the bitterness born
of the smother of the suppression of the French
upheaval. Thence arose the bewildering riot of
thought and revelation in the prophetic books.
“ Thel ” is plain enough to all who do not hanker
after such “simplicity” as frequently makes
Wo-Is worth and Tennyson puerile. But as to
“Albion,” “Jerusalem,” “ America,” and “Uri-
zen,” it is better for most people to lean oh
Swinburne’s shoulder, as Dante on Virgil’s, and
overlook them. But there is purpose in them all,
and that purpose is the elevation and emancipa-
tion of man. The"e is nothing higher and nothing
lower in this world or in space, in time or eternity.
A God of vengeance or of sacrifice is an inven-
tion. All asceticism is “dark pretence.” Humility
is a trick of “ the ancient elf ” conceived for man’s
degradation. Sweep all away, for by all does the
priest rule, and the king rule, and the law ter-
rorise. Stand upright, assertive beyond all com-
monplace or priest-bouud conception—and the
better day dawns. Such was Blake’s creed, ex-
pressed through “The Everlasting Gospel” and
“ The Marriage of Heaven and Hell.” Only by
the work of his hands could he get a hearing. He
etched his own plates, letterpress and illustration,
printed and bound his own books, and sold them
for daily bread. But in mind he was always
serene, and strong, and brave, and when at length
death tanned, he stretched out his hand joyfully
as did old Coplas de Maurique.
He was accessible enough to steal from through
all those years, and the stealing went on without
much acknowledgment. But about the year 1850
an effort was made by the most active and loyal
spirits of the Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood to bring
the man and his work out to the light. Alexander
Gilchrist was their medium. And almost exactly
co-incident was the advent of Walt Whitman in
America. The old message was given to the new
world through new lips. It was not well
received. The critics and the purists would
have handed Whitman over to the Holy Office.
But Whitman found a cause well worthy to live
and to die for. He fought, laboured, suffered
through the war, which at least established on a
basis of liberty the Republic, one and indivisible.
And he could see in that establishment full scope
for the realisation of all his vision. Hence all his
channts are jovous, robust, actual, present. He
had not to build up a New Jerusalem. He did
no” dream a dream, and ask, “Shall it be?” or
“ Mxv it be ? ” His assurance is buoyant;—
Come, I will make the continent indissoluble ;
I will m ike the most splendid race the sun has ever yet
shone upon:
I will make divine, magnetic lands.
Whitman knows no evil save of man’s making
or imagining. There is no vice and no vicious
opnortunity where there is no lying nor pretence.
“When man understands himself, he has the
highest possible conception of God.”
What do you suppose will satisfy the soul, except to
will free and own no superior ?
What do yon suppose I would intimate to you in a hun-
dred ways, but that man or woman is as good as God ?
And that there is no God anv more divine than your-
self?
¥ * * *
Oh, the joy of manly selfhood ! Personally to be ser-
vile to none—to defer to nor.e--not to any tyrant, known
or unknown.
• « * *
To confront with your own personality all other per-
sonalities on earth.
That is mental freedom: Bodily liberty is not
less or less plainly expressed. The body can only
be free as it opposes unnatural restrictions and
acts out desire. It is deadly every line of it to
the “gospel ” of modesty, of chastity, of humility,
of thankfulness.
Blake made for himself a hearing, but found in
his own time few if any listeners. Whitman
announced himself, springing a mine under all
conventionalities, and had the satisfaction, after
the shrieks of horror had ceased, of hearing the
question raised: “What does the man mean?” lie
answered, kept on answering, and, as every
succeeding note declared, with unchanging faith
and hope. It is well that he died in 1892, for ere
the last decade of his century closed such things
came to the United States as must have frozen
some at least of the founts of his farthest reach-
ing hopes. No more did the native genius assert :
“Lo I will make a continent one and indissoluble,
divine, magnetic land.” No more was comrade-
ship a universal, all-embracing - word. The
divine magnetic influences were despised ;
suokered tentacles were thrust out to
grasp and to strangle. The very basis of
national unity was suborted and all the maxims of
the fathers of the States despised. In the face of
the suppression of the Filipinos the soul of Whit-
man would stand aghast.
And if Blake should rise again or look out from
the company of the elect, to witness the begin-
nings of another earth century, what would he
behold ? What in England and throughout the
British Empire, but a recrudescence of all the
throne-worship and consequent tyrannoUvS sup-
press ion of human right and national spirit which
bore him down, too, in his own time ?
Easy to imagine the two hoary earth prophets
transformed into mighty Genii of the Infinities’
scattering the dust of which worlds are made on
their heads, rending their garments as the cloud
wrack of a storm is rent, and weeping as the
storm weeps. For must it not appear to them
that their earth teaching was wholly in vain ?
They sought to uplift man and woman as the most
perfect images of God this planet has brought
forth ; to teach them trust in themselves, natural,
beautiful, strong, naked, unashamed. And to
what effect ? The dark pretence still stalks the
world, and sits on the high places of the world.
Ihe social order festers and -rots with the
pruriences bred of the many inventions, the affec-
tations, the artificial restraints. The wheels of
Empire grind over true national spirit in every
land and no voice of Liberty makes protest. But
is all hope lost ?
F. M.
Froissart in 1902.
FCARRIJTHERS GOULD, caricaturist,
is also a writer of some distinction. In
• T roissart’s Modern Chronicles ”he has
, .. , , Siven. in picture and letterpress, a
dehghtfully humorous epitome of two decades of
English history. Here in Australia the book will
be, probably, condemned by Jingoes as vilely
Pro-Boer, because it is truthful in regard to the
S’African war; in England it has been received
with glad
acceptanc e
everywhere.

throughout.

CLERGYMAN DEFYING

(From the Modern Froissart.)
of a truth it behoveth you nob to be so reproachful, for
it is your apple-cart as well as mine that hath been put
in peril, and we be both in the same plight, seeing that
neither you nor X had foreknowledge of the enterprise.
Let us rather have pity for each, for we both are in the
same boat.”
Then these two looked one at the other, and sorely
mused, for neither knew how much the other did not
know.
The author covers the whole range of politics in
his 100 pages—Home Rule, the War, the question
of Ritualism—and always with the same light
touch: it is one of the most entertaining books of
the year.
* _
Some correspondents—
P.F.R. gives the other view : I am an Imperialist, but
no Jingo. F. R. is right about the little Greek States,
cradled into originality by impassable mountains. But
perhaps he hasn’t noticed that the electric telegraph has
been invented since then. Also newspapers, railways,
trade-unions, etc. He also carefully forgets that the
Greek democracies were founded on slavery. Easy
enough to have a cultured ruling class, if you can get
work done bv six times as many subject “living tools.”
Our problem is to govern so that there shall be no human
Waste, but all find fit scope for their faculties. This we
are more likely to get as part of a big nation, so
it seems to me. If you can convince me on this
point I will turn Separatist to-morrow. As to
the happiness argument, the first , two' centuries
of the Roman empire are generally conceded to have been,
on the whole, happier for Europe than any two centuries
(of which we have record) preceding it. My case is simply
this : Australia proved she thought life as a part of a big
thing is better than a little life “on one’s own” hy
federating. Why stop there ? We are still English
people. It is better ■politically, socially, mentally, to be
in intimate communion with a great nation, than'to lead
a cloistered, narrow-minded life, “ taking in each other’s
washing.!’,. Why stop there? Ultimately, in the fulness
of time, I look to the “Federation of the World ” of the
poet. That is why I and many others are Imperialists :
we hate the war as much as you do ; we hate English
(and Australian) snobbery as much as you do ; we are as
keen upholders of Australian individuality in art and.
literature. But if Maoriland was wrong in refusing to
federate, how will Australia be right in separating from
England ?
•A*
Henri Pene Du Buis, described by a correspon-
dent to The B. as “ the Buskin of America ” (the
blasphemy !), prints every syllable of that terrible
name in large caps over and beneath his articles.
Crushing over Victorian painter Meyer Blashki,
H.P.D.B. drivels :
The landscape that captivates me is in the symphony of
half-tints of a sky so delicate that it seems to be made of
evaporated azure. There are mists flaked with pink and
at the lilac line of the horizon white clouds forming an
aerial .exhedra. The. angels that are pitiful sit there, I
imagine. ...It is not even a picture. It is simply an
expression of the beautiful ephemeral as, the dust on' the.
wings of a butterfly. A breath would, efface it. . . . An’
artist must guard himself, belong to himself, remain
stable in the instability of things, realise himself obsti-
nately amid the diversity of phenomena.
And that is “fine writing” to the American
public and its wooden-nutmeg Ruskin !
&
It has now been discovered that Shakespeare
wrote the Psalms usually ascribed to King David.
Cut the final “e” from the poet’s name—no one
cared how it was spelt in the old days. Thus you
get 4 and 6—four vowels and six consonants.
Turn up the 46th Psalm, count 46 words from the
beginning, and you crane to the word “Shake.”
Count 46 words from the end, and you have
bpear. In the first line of the.second verse is
the word Will.” In the first line of the second
ver.-,e from the end is “Earn.” There you have
the whole, ‘ William Shakespear.” That is con-
clusive enough for anyone.

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE
The health of women is of supreme im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CURE —a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.

General Debilitation.
A CASE IN PYRMONT.
A Permanent Recuperation.
The Case of Mr. W. H. ATKINS.
(by our reporter.)
What an easy matter it is for a benevo-
lent being to diffuse pleasure and gladness
around him. In order to do so it is only
necessary to consider what means are pos-
sessed to lighten the burdens of our fellow-
men, and to help in whatever direction the
conscience may dictate. It is evident that
Mr. William Henry Atkins, of No. 211
Crown-road, Pyrmont, shares in this
opinion, for he said in reply to a question
put by our reporter :
“Of course, I will give you any informa-
tion you require, as the knowledge I gained
may be turned to good account by some
poor sufferer in need of good advice. It
has always been my motto to help those in
distress, and I think I can do so by letting
humanity know about the remarkable ex-
perience I had. I think that worry was at
the back of all my troubles, for after an in-
cident which grieved me greatly, I fell into
bad health. I did not care about anything
to eat, simply because I had no appetite.
The most tasty morsels imaginable failed to
tempt me as a rule, although I had to force
myself to take some nourishment when I
felt that I could not hold out any longer.
Not only was my appetite bad, but I ab-
stained from eating because of the pains
which afterwards appeared. My stomach
always got full of wind, which shifted about
from side to side, and left cramping pains
in all directions, and heavy pains affected
my chest. Sometimes I felt as if the
breath was being crushed out of me, the
oppression was so great, and I naturally felt
deeply concerned about it, as chest com-
plaints are so serious in their consequences.
As my food was not doing me any good I
lost strength rapidly, and soon I dropped
into that languid state of indifference to
everything which is so disastrous to every
businessman’s career. I felt as if I did'
not care how things went, and the morbid-
ness of my spirits quite precluded me from
thinking about anything pleasant. Haras-
sed as I was by depressing headaches, and
with my eyes dim and feeling as if they
were swollen, no wonder that I lost all in-
terest in life, for the average man does not
wish to live unless he is contented and at
ease.”
“But your lot was not so hard to bear
when night came, for then, I suppose, you
obtained relief in sleep ? ” remarked Mr.
Atkins’ interrogator.
“ Don’t you believe it; if anything, my I
nights were more intolerable than the days,
as I could not succeed in getting anything.
like a night’s rest. Certainly, I used to \
doze off from time to time, but the sleep
was not refreshing, because of the intrusion
of repulsive dreams, and when I got up I
felt as weary as if I had 1 een awake all
night. I could hardly stand the bitter taste
which was in my mouth every morning, and
the unhealthy condition of my stomach
could be detected by the fur on my tongue;
but what I regretted more than all else was
the quick and painful throbbing of my heart.
When I laid down I felt that organ shaking
inside in a way that caused me much alarm,
so that, with every particle of strength
being gradually eaten away by my com-
plaints, there was nothing in my condition
to assist me in entertaining hopes of a re-
covery. The least exertion brought on a
feeling of faintness, and when 1 stood up I
used to get quite giddy, as if everything
was on the move. All this I put down to
my extreme weakness, which was also re-
sponsible for the annoying aches in my legs
and loins.' From some cause or other my
arms were also intensely painful, and if ever
a nervous system was wrecked it was mine
On the least provocation I became excited
and irritable, while the trembling of my
nerves served to increase the general misery
of my existence. My body was greatly de
nuded of flesh, and I can tell you in all
seriousness that my life was a burden which
I would willingly have east off at any
moment.”
“I believe you suffered in this way
several years,” interjected the scribe.
“ During the period of your illness did yon
ever enlist the services of a medical man ? ”
“ Considering that I was lingering, wait
ing, and hoping for years, I should rather
think I did,” replied Mr. Atkins. “ Why,
I consulted dozens of doctors, yet my suffer-
ings grew worse, and I may also state that
I have taken scores of medicines which were
procured at stores and chemists’ shops. It
would have been far better if I had kept
the money in my pocket, for all but one of the
medicines were of no service to me. The
only remedy which suited my complaints
was Clements Tonic, and this was recom-
mended to me by a man living in Harris-
street, who had used it, and knew what it
could do. After taking two bottles of
Clements Tonic I felt much better, and was
able to get about with much more freedom.
I went to a chemist to get some more, but
he put me off by saying he coidd give me
something better, and pointed out the
difference in the size of the bottles, so,
foolishly enough, I bought his medicine in-
stead of Clements Tonic, and in a few days
I was as bad as ever. Then I insisted on
having Clements Tonic, and again I im-
proved under its influence, so that there was
no doubt about its being the best medicine
in the market. I would rather have one
dose of Clements Tonic than a barrelful of
any other medicine, for it proved its supe-
riority in every possible way. The first
thing it did was to make me eat and sleep,
and I was glad of the respite from my
afflictions which I had when resting. Be-
fore long a feeling of animation spread right
through my system, and I then shook oil
the yoke of depression which had clouded
my past few years, as I felt assured that
Clements Tonic would pull me through. In
course of time the pains arising from indi-
gestion were quelled; the nauseous taste
left my mouth, and my head was free from
aches. Some of my doctors said my liver
was diseased, while others said my stomach
was extremely weak; but if they had seen
me after a few weeks’ use of Clements
Tonic they would not have known me. My
stomach could then digest and retain any
kind of food, and my liver symptoms were
non-existent. The shaking of my nerves
was promptly checked by Clements Tonic,
and the regulating and strengthening effects
of that medicine on my system generally
were really extraordinary. Every ache and
pain yielded to the pacifying influences of
Clements Tonic, and I am heartily thank-
ful to say that by its exclusive use my
health was re-established. I have lived in
this house for the past 17 years, and all the
neighbours know that Clements Tonic cured
me ; but for the benefit of those who are
unaware of these facts you may publish
them in any form you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Hisnry Atkins, of 211 Crown-road,
Pyrmont, in the State of New South Wales, Com-
monwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four
folios and consecutively numbered from one
to four, and that it contains and is a true and
faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish
in any way my statements —which 1 give volun-
tarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney, tbit
twentieth day of February, one thousand nine hun-
dred and two, before me,
JNO. C. HALLAM, J. 9

Sickly Children
‘Her own dear little self again,
thanks entirely to Scott s
Emulsion”
In these trying winter weeks one hears
everywhere the cry of the children, disease
so easily breaks down their tender strength.
Every mother should answer that cry in
this way. w e stberry Avenue,
* “ Green Lanes, London, England,
“ April 22nd, 1901.
it Dear Sirs,— My little daughter, two
years of age, had a very bad attack of
whooping cough last autumn I tried
everything I could, for her, but could not
got her well, and she fell away to almost
nothing. Reading of many who had bene-
fited by Scott’s Emulsion, I decided to
try the preparation for my child, and
lam very thankful I did so. In a few
weeks, after taking Scott’s Emulsion, there
was a very material improvement in her
condition, and the cough was not nearly
so harsh or distressing. What rather sur-
prised me was the quickness with which she
PHYLIS AUDRY WILLS.
(From a photograph.)
acquired a taste for Scott’s Emulsion; she
really wanted more than was good for her.
I soon felt we were on the right track.
You may be interested to know my wife
procured a bottle of emulsion from a
chemist, stated to be ‘ theirj own make, but
it did not have the least effect upon baby,
and after that we would have nothing but
‘Scott’s.’ Now when I look at my child
and remember what she was a few months
ago, I can scarcely believe she is the same
little one. She is thoroughly well, strong,
and healthy, and her own dear little self
again, thanks entirely to Scott’s Emulsion.
“ Yours very sincerely,
“ (Signed) Edward Wills.”
Whooping cough is, of course, only one of
the many children's illnesses flying about
just now. Measles, mumps, bronchitis,
rickets, marasmus, poor digestion, anaemia,
scarlet fever, these are some of the un-
pleasant words one hears in all directions.
In every case of these diseases Scott’s Emul-
sion should at one stage or another be
given—Scott’s Emulsion will cure. There
is nothing to be obtained anywhere that is
half so good for sickly children. Your own
doctor will agree to this.
And your own sense will agree that you
cannot hope for the striking results pro-
duced by Scott’s Emulsion if you buy
something else. You may be offered what
is said to be “ just as good,” but the seller
of such imitation or substitute has no
evidence of what he says, and can produce
none, for the simple reason that Scott's
Emulsion is the standard children’s remedy
on which all others are modelled. Mr.
Wills’s experience with one of these “just
as good” preparations shows you exactly
what to expect from them. No imitation
is ever as good as the original.
Mr. Wills emphasises another striking
point about Scott’s Emulsion—how nice it
is to take: “she really wanted more than
was good for her.” This is the great ad-
vantage Scott’s Emulsion has over common
cod-liver oil, which no one ever wants to
take! And then, medicinally, Scott’s Emul-
sion is, according to the comparative tests
of a distinguished physician, “ three times
as efficacious as the same amount of
common cod-liver oil.”
For these reasons see that the picture cf
a man carrying a big fish is on the wrapper
of every bottle; if they are there you have
Scott’s Emulsion.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

Various Verses.
A Millionaire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I walk into town,
Having no tramway-fare,
My heart is not cast down,
I do not fret or frown,—
I am a Millionaire.
The way, indeed, is long,
And longer still might seem,
But, midst the moving throng,
I hear a golden song,
And dream a golden dream.
The dream is brave and bold :
A noble legacy—
Ten thousand pounds in gold—
Left by an uncle old,
Has found its way to me.
I am a Friend of Art,
And haunt the studios;
I play the Patron’s part
In princely style: my heart
It opens like a rose.
There is no painter poor,
Whose works I do not buy,
And make his fame secure,
And board and lodging sure—
With beer when he is dry.
The Poets, most of all,
Are patronised by me.
I build for them a tall,
Fine oimmunistic hall—
A grand phalanstery.
A garden, large and fair,
That stately home surrounds;
And scent of blossoms rare
Perfumes the pleasant air
Within those pleasant grounds.
And glasses gaily clink,
Each filled with liquor prime,
Whose bubbles brightly wink ;
And he who wants a drink
Need only write a rhyme.
In my phalanstery
The bards find noble cheer,
And live like lords, rent-free,
And merely pay to me
A poem once a year.
I look around and sigh;
The trams rush up and down.
How money seems to fly !
My fortune’s spent, and I
Am still a mile from town.
I should have made my cake
Much bigger at the start;
It is the great mistake
That most day-dreamers make—
To play the miser’s part.
But what means that to me ?
Merely a moment’s thought.
And with a smile of glee,
Unto my legacy
I simply add a nought.
Then watch me sweeping by
With golden-crowned Romance !
My head it strikes the sky;
I am King Midas —I
And Lord of High Finance.
Bohemian friends of mine,
The men I knew of old,
I ask them all to dine,
And fill them up with wine,
And cram their hats with gold.
I give a dinner great
To creditors done-brown,
And place beneath each plate
A cheque for debts to date
I wake, and am in town !
N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.
Fishing Lines.
Many there be whose balladry be-rhymes
The Race from fallen flag to whacking whips,
And many they that ring Sb. Cupid’s chimes,
For that love turns to lutes and lyres their lips ;
And some they sing of Stock, and some of Ships,
And some the swirling of the miner’s dish,
But strike we here the string for him that trips
Forth with the patient push whose single wish
It is to fish and fish, and then again to fish.
These be the folk love well the River G-od,
His ruffled ripple and his rattling reed,
And eke his wealth of bream and weight of cod
Beyond where waits the woven water-weed,
To snag the hook, therefrom not easy freed ;
These love the river-wind (so oft has spoke
To singers stretched where banks be shadow-
treed) ; .
They love to hear it yarning to the oak
What time the hours serene serener make a
smoke.
Ay, seems it for this sort is joy enow
Where, on his wallaby, their River God
Smiles to the rushes which, replying, bow
And greets the grasses that responsive nod :
Here on a forky stick they fix the rod
To learn that the expected ne’er may come,
At least till twilight brings with it that odd
Jeer of the Jackass from the patriarch gum,
Blest hour of bites! for now mosquitos ’gin to hum.
But, sure, to us beyond all bends and birds
Our craft’s own notes: —the sinker as it drops—
Yea, e’en the dreadful din of Jack’s wild words
Finding once more he hath forgot the chops,
Or tangleth tight his tackle in tree-tops,
Or when our last match dies and disappears,
Or on the strand the hated cat-fish flops,
Or, as when prey more prized the margin nears,
The traitorous hook betrays and straight the great
cod clears.
O, sweet the shadows when the dark, deep drifts
At the strange stillness of the sunset dream,
Sweet when the amorous moon flings lavish gifts
Of gold and silver to her love, the stream;
Yet, sweetest of riparian hours, I deem
That one wherein the tell-tale float makes tracks,
And strained the lines that long so loose did seem,
And mine a big’un hauls to land, but Jack’s
—The spoil a foot from shore—all on a sudden
slacks!

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia >

prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

postage — lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—note
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

SATURDAY, MAY 17, 1902.
The Real Reform Movement and the
Bogus One.
The cry for reform of the State Parliaments,
and the State finances, and the State politics
generally, grows louder in the land, and
N.S. Wales, Victoria, and Tasmania have all
got the disease. South Australia is peaceful.
It has had its reform, in a sense ; that is to
say, it lias cut down its Parliament a little,
and left things at that, with all the really
large evils untouched. The reform move-
ment is almost wholly a financial movement,
and the reduction of Parliament is advocated
mainly on the ground that it will save a cer-
tain small amount of money—in N.S. W.
per inhabitant per annum. Therefore the
reduction is, in every case, aimed principally
at the Lower House, which has some brains
and a very moderate salary. The Upper
House, which has less brains, and is utterly
stagnant, and, in the three leading States
at all events, receives no salary, arouses no
serious hostility. The financial discussion has
enabled a large number of alleged reformers,
among whom the Melbourne Age is pro-
minent, to exhibit a pitiable ignorance as to
the real condition of affairs, the usual pro-
cess of argument being to ask wildly and
generally why it should require, say, £7 per
inhabitant ''annually to run this country
when £3 per inhabitant is sufficient in
the United Kingdom? The difference
arises mainly through railway fares and
freights being called public revenue in Aus-
tralia and not in England, and through cost
of working the railways, interest on money
spent on their construction, cost of police,
education, and charities being called public
expenditure in Australia, while in England
the first two are not at all, and the other
three only in part, so described. This is sim-
ple enough, but it is apparently outside the
knowledge of most of the agitators. The
general tendency is to rush wildly at that £7
or so, and cut off the tail of it with an axe
regardless of circumstances.
* * • •
The first essential of financial reform is to
get a distinct idea what are the finances
which it is proposed to reform. To make a
beginning of this, the railway accounts
should be taken out of the national balance-
sheet altogether. The railways should be
•debited with their working expenses and the
interest upon the loan money owing on their
account, and credited with their own
revenue; and when the balance is struck
•only the amount, if any, which the general
revenue has to contribute to makegood their
shortage should appear in the general
balance-sheet. Then Victoria would realise
that its real expenditure, including educa-
tion, old age pensions and the railway deficit,
is now only about £4,000,000 a year, and
N.S.W.’s would stand at about £7,000,000,
and a great deal of the sensational and bogus
element would vanish out of the public
finances. This would help to abolish the
crowd of excited but useless persons whose
one idea is to fix a bloodshot and unreason-
ing eye upon the big totals in the public
accounts, and then rush at them with a
lethal weapon. And having got rid of these
persons there might be a chance of real
reform.
» * * *
The public works expenditure should also
constitute a separate account. The present
system puts a premium on fraud and dis-
honesty, and makes lying a profitable under-
taking. The public only looks at totals, and
anyone who can fake the total can please the
public—when such financial authorities as
the Age and George D. Meudell know no
better the general public can’t be expected
to know any better either. The Treasurer
who spends out of revenue £6,000,000 on
the ordinary business of government, and
£2,000,000 on necessary public works, is a
spendthrift who “ ble’ws ” £8,000,000 of
public income in one year. His successor,
who charges all these works to loans, and
yet spends £1,000,000 more than the other
man on the ordinary business of government,
stands forth brilliantly as the economist who
runs the country on £7,000,000. The na-
tural result is that the economist, or fakir,
or public fraud and swindle, who keeps
down the ordinary expenditure by charging
all manner of things to loans, is exceedingly
common. _ Except in rare instances, is
the only kind of politician that it pays to be.
He gets the approbation of the Age, and, in
a less degree, of George D. Meudell, and
of all the other people who look at the totals
and then yell; and that reward keeps him
up.
• • » *
Further, it is highly advisable that the
Public Works Fund should not only be kept
separate from the general accounts, like the
railway funds, but that the proceeds of cer-
tain taxes and other revenues should be paid
directly into that fund, without going
through the ordinary revenue at all, and
should be so strictly set apart for Public
Works purposes that they can be diverted
under no circumstances whatever. The
reform parties in N.S.W., Victoria, and
Tasmania all demand that borrowing for un-
productive works shall cease ; and not only
this but all borrowing should cease—it
can’t cease too soon. There has already, in
various States and in times of great stress,
been a temporary or partial cessation of bor-
rowing for non-revenue-yielding works, but
always with the same unsatisfactory result.
The necessary works that used to be pro-
vided for out of loans were not provided for
out of revenue—they were stopped altoge-
ther. Ministers were afraid to pay for
them out of revenue, for under a system
where money spent on making new roads
and docks, afid money spent on a Coronation
Drunk, are all lumped together, they would
simply have been remembered as the per-
sons who made a lamentable increase of
expenditure, and the Age and George
D. Meudell would have denounced them
accordingly. So when there was unusual
economy in the way of charging public works
to loans it simply meant that new schools
were not built where they were wanted, that
buildings were allowed to fall into disrepair,
that valuable works were left half-finished,
and so on. And when the country got tired
of this state of things, the loan leprosy
returned. There is no permanent reform to
be made in this way.
• » • *
Experience has shown that New South
Wales can get through with £9,400,000 of
revenue and £600,000 of loan moneys im-
properly applied to revenue purposes—total,
£10,000,000. It has also shown that
N.S.W. can spend nearly £11,000,000 of
revenue and steal over £1,000,000 of loan
money for revenue purposes—total, over
£l2,ooo,ooo—without very much more to
show for it* And in a less degree the same
thing has happened in other States. If
N.S.W. enacted that borrowing should cease
and that the whole land revenue, of nearly
£2,000,000 a year, should be paid into a pub-
lic works fund to take the place of loans, it
would possibly be found that the State could
get through on the revenue that was left to
it. If nothing of the kind is enacted, then
the expenditure, without any special provision
for public works, will continue to swell up
and consume the income as usual. It is no
use trusting to the surplus to pay for unpro-
ductive but necessary works—there never is
any surplus, and there is never likely to be
under the present easy-going conditions. So
long as the Governor’s salary, and the interest
on the public debt, and the civil service
wage-sheet, and the grants to country schools-
of-arts and hog shows, and all the sundries and
miscellaneous expenditure have first, call on
the revenue, and the public works vote gets
only what is left, the public works vote will
mostly depend on loans, and all the pro-
grammes of all the reform leagues that ever
were or will be, will make no difference.
But if loans are prohibited by Act of Parlia-
ment, and the land revenue in N.S.NV.
(equal to about £2,000,000 a year)
and the land revenue and land and
income-tax receipts in Victoria (equal
to about £700,000 a year) are paid
into a separate fund to supply their place,
and the trust funds are put in the hands of
trustees, absolutely beyond the Treasurer’s
reach, then the Government will have to
make the remainder of the revenue cover the
ordinary costs of administration, or else find
more revenue. In New South Wales, it
might be possible to fill the gap by economy.
In Victoria and Tasmania, which are already
run more economically than even England is,
it would almost certainly be necessary to tax
David Syme and George D. Meudell some-
thing extra to make the accounts balance.
♦ • * #
There is no way, apart from this cast-iron
process of law, by which the necessary reform
is likely to be effected. If there is merely a
vague, general instruction to economise, a
Treasurer may abstain from borrowing for
unproductive works for a year, or possibly
two, or perhaps only six months. Then the
popular impulse will have died away, like
the stamp-collecting disease, or the habit of
having surprise-parties, or the ping-pong
mania, or the Simultaneous Mission, and
the man who promises to restore the
endowments to schools of arts and the grants
to cattle shows, and to make up for the extra
outlay by “ borrowing in moderation in
order to judiciously develop the re-
sources of the country,” will outbid the
economical Treasurer and get his office.
It would be difficult, at least it would be
a matter of unpleasant publicity, to secure
the repeal of the anti-borrowing law
and the law assigning certain revenues
for public works purposes ; but in the
absence of such laws then the conscientious
Treasurer who broke his heart trying to put
things right would get neither gratitude nor
reward. After a few months his attempts at
economy and his demands for more revenue
would be met on all sides by the suggestion
that a little temporary raising of money
would do no harm, and wouldn’t be noticed.
He would lose the support of the country
member whose hog-show had been docked
of its subsidy. He would be denounced by
the Age for increasing David Syme’s taxes.
He would be called a cheese-parer and a
skinflint and a man devoid of broad ideas.
The “ broad-minded person ” who believed in
a policy of “ developing the resources of the
country by a judicious expenditure of bor-
rowed money ” would breathe in the ear of
his party and win it away. And he would
go out of office a poor, worn-out man, and
the papers would ever after speak of him in
the highest terms—but he would remain out
of office all the same.
• • • *
Take Premier Peacock as a case in point,
a rather weak man of good intentions, yet
of considerable intelligence. It is absurd to
hope that Peacock, by himself, would,
especially for his paltry salary, keep up a
long-continued struggle for the repair of
things financial. The Age and G. D. Meu-
dell and the Kyabram League would pre-
sently give up supporting him actively and
go about their other business. The House
would support most cordially every abstract
proposition re the saving of money, but
every member whose job or steal or per-
quisite was cut off in the effort at reducing
the proposition to practice, and every man
whose interests were touched by the new
tax proposals, would be a lost vote, so long
as there was a Tory leader with a fine, large
lavish policy to give the vote to. But if
loans were legally prohibited, and trust-
funds couldn’t be got at, and the £700,000 a
year of land revenue and land and income-tax
money had to be paid, by the operation of
a boiler-plated statute, into the Public
Works Fund, then even a Premier of infirm
purpose ivould be master of the situation.
He could inform the country member that
he might or might not consent that his
school of arts, or agricultural show subsidy
be retrenched, but there wasn’t the least
hope of there being any money to pay it
with, even supposing it was nominally voted.
He could resist the importunate member
who wanted his relative appointed to the
public service by the simple intimation that
the relative could come in if he liked, but
there was no money to pay him with. If
new taxation became really necessary it
would be forthcoming when the alternative
lay in stopping payment. And he could
fight this battle without losing votes, for the
sufficient reason that not even a Duncan
Gillies, or an O’Sullivan, with the same
laws to restrict him, could offer any better
terms.
• • • «
If the Reform Party, while its enthusiasm
is still at white heat, can force the State
Parliaments to pass the two statutes here
suggested—an anti-borrowing law and a law
setting aside sufficient sources of revenue
(say £2,000,000 a year in N.S.W., and
£700,000 in Victoria) for the supply of a
Public Works Fund—then the other
reforms and economies will take care of
themselves. But if it expects a Treasurer,
for the sake of the miserable pittance
which is called a Ministerial salary, to adhere
persistently by his own efforts to a policy
which the country itself will have practically
forgotten in six months—to be abused for
his parsimony as the Age abused Turner in
the latter days of his Victorian premiership,
and as the Argus and Paunch abused him
almost all the time—to sacrifice his political
career by offending with his integrity
members whom his opponent is willing to
placate with loan funds and extravagances—
then it expects too much. The popular
Reform Movement is as ephemeral as a
religious revival. The only way to give it
some reasonable show of permanence is
to fix its policy by Act of Parliament, so
that the old easy-going ways of sin can’t be
slid into again without the formal and public
repeal of that Act.
The Tory Sydney University.
Sydney University Senate, Thb Bulletin hears
on good authority, has been seriously considering
question of ‘Mealing with ” Mr. G. A. Wood,
Chains Professorof History, fordaring tohold views
different from those of Dr. Maclaurin and Dr.
Ken wick on the question of the Boer war, and
approximating his opinion to that of the late Mr.
Gladstone ; also for daring to address a Labor
party meeting on the subject. From what one
hears, this Senate has been more scandalised by
Prof. Wood dragging the culture of the Univer-
sity down to the depths of a Trades and Labor
Council than even by his daring to share the
opinions of Herbert Spencer, John Ruskin,
Bryce, George Meredith, Froude, Glad-
stone, Hori Grey, and a few other people of low
opinions, but of well-cut coats, as to the situation
in South Africa. The Trades and Labor Council
delegate usually combines low democratic views
with unfashionable clothing, and that union is
irremediably hateful to the fatted Tories of
the university Senate. This intention of the
Senate to either gag or bludgeon Professor Wood
has been freely canvassed by the Labor party
during the last few days. The issue was brought
before them as affecting, not the rights or wrongs
of the Boer war—that is an issue on which the
Party has agreed to differ—but the right of
freedom of speech in a democratic community.
Subsequently the outside organisation of the
political Labor Party discussed the matter and
came, we understand, to an almost unanimous
agreement that any such outrage on civil liberty
as the University Senate contemplated would
have to be combatted from the first, and, if carried
into effect, made the subject of as effective a
remonstrance as possible.
• * * •
With that decision every democrat must agree,
whatever his views on the South African war. If,
for exercising his rights as a free citizen, a servant
of the University is to be victimised through the
ignorant, illiberal tyranny of that institution’s
governors, the conclusion must be come to that the
University is a danger to the democracy of this
state, and must be treated as such in the future.
There already exists good cause for resentment
against the. Tory tendencies of Sydney Univer-
sity. It is in the position of having received from
the State a magnificent tract of land, frequent
subventions for its building funds, and an annual
subsidy now standing at £14,700. Without the
help it received from the general taxpayer it could
never have come into existence, and could now not
continue in existence. Yet it has always shaped
its policy so as to make its classes as close a pre-
serve as possible for the sons of the rich, and to bar
from them the sons of the poor (who yet have to
dive deeply into their pockets to help pay for its
up-keep). It further seeks to confine its scope so
as to be as much as possible a merely Sydney
institution. In the election of its Senate, for
Distance, practically only Sydney members of
Convocation can vote, as the franchise rnay only
be exercised in Sydney, and graduates living in
the country cannot, as a rule, afford a lengthy jour-
ney to the capital to participate in a senatorial
election. The duty of an institution which is
in large part maintained by the general tax-
payer—with money drawn from Bathurst, Bourke,
Goulburn, and Newcastle, as well as Sydney—to
give country students some share in its benefits by
extension lectures, is almost entirely neglected.
It is a weighty impeachment, therefore, which can
already be urged against this institution. If, in
addition, its governing body assumes the functions
of a Censor of opinions, and seeks to crush out all
expression of Democratic thought on the part of
University professors, it will become a necessary
duty of Liberals to take means to crush it utterly,
if necessary ; otherwise, to reform it root and
branch.
* •** • •
The power at the disposal of Parliament, if ever
exercised to the full, would be undoubtedly effi-
cacious. What free grants of money have been
made to the University of Sydney in the past
cannot, of course, be revoked. But the land on
which the University buildings stand was not
actually given to the institution, but conferred on
a “peppercorn rental”—that is to say the State
can demand yearly one peppercorn as rent: and
it can raise that rent to two peppercorns, or to
£20,000, as representing the full rental value of
the property, if it pleases. That would be an
extreme step, but quite a feasible one. A yet
more simple means of dealing with Toryism in its
Gothic entrenchments at Newtown would be the
withdrawal of its subsidies. For 1901-2 the Uni-
versity is estimated to get from the State of
N.S.W. :
By statutory endowments ... .. £5,500
By votes on the Estimates .. .. 9’200
Total £14,700
A mere vote on the Estimates could lop off the
£92oo—an Act of Parliament the £5500. The
withdrawal even of £9200 would probably make
the carrying on of the University impossible; if
in addition the statutory grant were annulled, and
a heavy ground rent were imposed, Dr. Maclaurin
and Co. would certainly have to close down ; they
would not be likely to seek to make up the deficit
out of their own pockets. And if the University
is to show itself an ignorantly Tory institution,
devoted to the suppression rather than the culture
of thought, Liberals in Parliament will certainly
work to that end, and work with the full approval
of the constituencies. We can better afford to do
without a higher knowledge of Latin than without
the democratic right of Freedom of Speech.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Peacock’s Tale.
Much will be forgiven and many things will be
forgotten in the case of Victoria’s Premier
Peacock if he only adheres to the constitutional
reform ideas which he has outlined. His pro-


gramme is not quite an ideal one, but it
than was commonly expected, it is
ahead of the Tory and fraudulent
policy,” and it is a larger measure of re ' or ffi , tl f
most countries get in one instalment without
revolution. The principal suggestions are .
m Assembly to be reduced from 95 members to 60
returned by *6O constituencies and elected by adu t
BU (2) a House of 48 Rich Landlords to have its membership
reduced to 30, returned by 15 constituencies. .
/o\ aii ratepayers to have votes for the House of Land-
loSs, which the number of voters from
133 |?a£ Severson who has a vote for the House of
Landlords to be elioible to be a member of that boot.
(5) In case of deadlock, both Houses to be dissolvable
Incase of further deadlock the matter at issue to be
settled by a vote of the two Houses sitting as one.
(6) Ministers to be allowed to speak in either House.
The great reform lies in clause 4. Hitherto, by
reason of the property qualification, only 2000
persons in all Victoria were capable of being
elected to the House of Landlords, and when
from this number were deducted those who were
too old, too busy, too vacant in their minds, who
were absent, who didn’t care for politics, and
those who were in the Assembly, it was seldom
possible to get two candidates together. Ihe 48
Rich Landlords were allowed to insolently dis-
qualify 131,000 out of their 133,000 electors, and to
treat them as political lepers and outcasts, unfit to
sit in the House which they were supposed to create,
and which was supposed to represent them. Bra-
inier Peacock’s reform policy, if it is earned out,
will lift Victoria, in a constitutional sense, from
its antiquated position on the extreme back seats
of civilisation to a front place. If it is carried out
—that is the question ! All the Kyabram crowd
of boodlers and frauds and Tories will howl
bitterly about their magnificent scheme of saving
2id. per inhabitant annually (a whole complete
2VI ) bycutting down the membership of the Demo-
cratic branch of the Legislature, being mixed up
with a small matter like decent and progressive
government, and thereby put to hazard. Llie
Assembly isn’t likely to give a large majority to
a measure for sacking 35 of its members. Ihe
House of Landlords will almost certainly cut out
the reform clauses, and offer to pass the measure
as a reduction measure alone. All the Kyabram
push, and the G. D. Meudell crowd, and the
Argus will urge the Government not to sacrifice
the chance of saving 21yd. per inhabitant annually
by its blind obstinacy in attempting to furnish
Victoria with a decent Legislature. Peacock came
back from the last election pledged to call together a
popularly elected Convention to draft a new Con-
stitution. He had the people with him, and he
had a small majority in the Assembly, and yet he
weakened miserably and let the thing drop. Now
the question is: Has Peacock got more courage
and more fighting capacity than he had a year or
two ago ? Everything depends on that. A man
who can’t fight is of no use in the present
emergency. If Peacock sees this matter through
to the bitter end The Bulletin will never call
him Pecksniff again.
A White Man’s Exclusion Act.
The Commonwealth law, which was framed for
the exclusion of the Asiatic, is apparently being
administered by the Barton Government as a
law for keeping out white men and letting in
every possible nigger who applies. The unfor-
tunate circumstance about the Aliens Exclusion
law is that its efficiency depends wholly on the
common sense and good faith of its administrators.
In this matter, as in many others, Australia came
to grief over the British connection. The irres-
ponsible despot at the other end—the man whom
Australia has no voice in electing to his Parlia-
mentary position, and who stands towards us in
the same position as a Turkish sultan or other
violent character —insisted that there should be
no direct exclusion, only an educational test. The
applicant at our front door may, or may not,
as the Federal Government feels inc’ined, be re-
quired to write out 50 words in any European
language, and be shut out if he fails to do so.
It rests with the Government to decide: (1)
to whom the test shall be applied and to whom it
shall not; (2) what language shall be used ; (3)
what degree of accuracy in writing out the 50
words shall constitute passing the test. The
Act may be made to exclude every, nigger,
and to let in every white man, or to ex-
clude everybody or to admit everybody—in
fact, Joseph Chamberlain’s foolishness gives the
Federal Government almost unlimited power in
this respect. And the use to which the Barton
Ministry has put that power so far has been a
shameful treachery to the White Australia ideal
and a disreputable crawling to the nigger-loving
Salisbury-Chamberlain Government. Every
Hindu who pounds at our gate is tested in the
nearest approach to his own language that the
law will admit of. These attractive brothers
and friends and descendants of the butchers
of Cawnpore are required to write out a
simple passage in English, practically the
only European language current m India, and
are thus given every possible opportunity to get
through. But the other day at Newcastle (N.S. W.)
the law fell savagely on a Portuguese sailor who
had deserted his ship, and who knew no more of
the Aliens Restriction Law than he did of
Andree’s lost balloon. If it had given him the
same chance that it so anxiously gives to the nigger,
and tested him in the European language with
which he was sure to be most familiar —Portuguese
—he might have passed, but, according to the
newspaper report, it tested him in English, and
then fell on him with a savage sentence of six
months’ gaol for not being able to write the tongue
of Shakespeare. A Portuguese, even a Portu-
guese common sailor, is not an undesirable
immigrant in the same sense that an Asiatic,
whether prince or coolie, is. He is a white man,
with the white man’s possibilities, and can inter-
marry with us without causing any deterioration
of the race. The law does not require that he
should be put to the test at all—it is optional, not
compulsory. It does not specify that the test
shall be in English—that was a disreputable
stretching of the law to oppress the white stranger,
while the way of the undesirable nigger is smoothed
in every possible manner. When two niggers re-
cently broke away from a ship in a Queensland port
they were simply brought back and put on board
again— they didn’t get six months as the white
man does, for being illegally at large in the Com-
monwealth. The Sydney Telegraph struck a
great truth when it suggested that it would be a
fair thing if the Portuguese Government cap-
tured Premier Barton in its territory, and gave
him six months in gaol for not knowing the Por-
tuguese language, and for being unable to write
out a passage from Camoens to dictation. There
is an urgent necessity for the Labor party in the
Federal Parliament to make it clear to the Barton
Ministry that the Act is not to be applied to. the
white stranger within our gates, that no civilised
country sends a white man to gaol for not know-
ing foreign languages, and that the law is an anti-
nigger law, and is to be used as such.
The Usualness of being 1 a Murderer.
An old Bulletin contributor, who se honesty and
truth this paper can voucii lor to as great an
extent as it would care to back the moral P.JN.
of any man, and who was 13 months at the
S’African war as a non-commissioned officer, tells
this:
Every soldier who has been much in the field and has
talked much with Tommies and fellow colonials has heard
dozens of stories of atrocities as bad as those related of
the Bushveldters —or nearly so. I do not think I am far
wrong in saying that Boers are murdered every week.
There are columns, such as the Canadian Scouts, in which
it is understood right through that prisoners are not to
be taken, but are to be shot right off. The commanding
officer does not line his men up and say, “ I, , hereby
give orders,” etc. But there are other ways of achieving
the same result. The Canadians are far the worst in this
respect, and they will tell you with much satisfaction of
scalpings and “ necktie-parties.”
I never saw any of these atrocities committed. But
out there you can get plenty of first-hand evidence. It
is told to you always, as the story of a meritorious deed,
to one who should, as a matter of course, appreciate it.
The idea that the killing of a surrendered Boer will not
be regarded as a great deed never seems to be thought of
out there. I have a vivid recollection of the look of
astonishment and disgust on the face of a Tommy when I
described, as a set of savages, the participants in a parti-
cularly revolting piece of Boer-killing which he had
narrated. He told me how a soldier had found an
unwounded Boer hiding down an ant-bear hole, and,
instead of making him come out as a prisoner, bayonetted
him by degrees down the hole, which he then filled up.
These yarns are often told with such corroborative
detail that, even bearing in mind the great anxiety
of Tommy to tell something particularly atrocious,
go as to earn the approbation of colonial
listeners, it is impossible not to believe them.
A Maorilander once described to me the catching and
shooting of a sniper on the Barberton trip towards the
end of 1900. ‘‘Where was the officer?” “Oh, the
officer partly suggested it, and got away out of sight.
The sniper begged for his life.” “ What did they kill him
for ?” “ Why, because he was a sniper, and had been
annoying them for a long time before he was caught.”
A sniper (one man fighting on his own) is by some per-
version of ideas supposed to be breaking the rules of war
—if he is a Boer, that is.
The hanging by the Canadians in American fashion
(that is with revolver accompaniment) of four Boer sur-
renderors because of an alleged white flag incident some
time before, I have seen indignantly denied by a Canadian
colonel in Engiand. If that colonel did not know it was
quite true, he ought to have known, because nine
Canadians out of ten can give him all information about
the story.
I have come back with some well-defined ideas.
First that the Boer is on the average a much
more humane and chivalrous man than the average
Tommy (who mostly approximates to the city
larrikin); or than the average colonial, who is a
man of much more excited and excitable passions
than the Boer ; or than the average Canadian,
who is simply a white Red Indian, and should be
absolutely prohibited from taking part in civilised
warfare. It is also my painful fate to believe,
since I know it for a positive fact and cannot get out of it,
that there are more “ white flag incidents,” more
atrocities and barbarities, and less chivalry on the English
than on the Boer side. Also that warfare, so far from
developing the nobler and more chivalrous passions, does
just the opposite. I am sorry. I would have had my old
faiths remain. But I can never look at my fellow man
now, even if he be merely engaged in the ferocious occu-
pation of measuring ribbon in a draper’s shop, but I think
of the cold-blooded savage concealed beneath. I ask you
not to print my name, since the time has not yet come
when the truth is permitted.

The Man Who Looks Wise.
The correspondence that has lately been raging
between Premier Barton, of Australia, and Pre-
mier Lewis, of Tasmania, in re “Tattersall,”
has left the Flyspeck C.M.G. with a considerable
pain in his reputation. Somehow or other Mr.
Lewis has managed, in the course of years, to
work up some sort of local reputation as the pos-
sessor of more than ordinary brains. He is a
solemn, humorless person who has lived a respect-
able life, and is gifted with the faculty of looking
wise. He has never offended a political oppon-
ent, hardly ever had an opponent to offend had
he wanted a battle ever so much. While Brad-
don was boss of the Land of Dreams Lewis
was nominal leader of the nominal Opposition,
and filled the position with such decorum and
with so earnest a desire to “assist the Govern-
ment ” that Braddon grew to love him as a sick
younger brother, and took every opportunity of
pleasing him and his numerous relatives by judi-
cious compliments on his learnedness as a lawyer,
his kindliness as a critic of the Government,
and his high-standing as a statesman. The wily
Braddon’s compliments had probably as great an
influence in building up the unoffending Lewis’
reputation for wisdom as the Honorable Neil’s
own talent for looking wise. Throughout the
island there has been for sometime a vague, elusive,
half-expressed notion, hardening to a belief, that
some day Lewis would distinguish himself before
the nations ; it was felt that no man could possibly
continue to wear such a sagacious expression of
countenance and to preserve such a solemn
demeanor unless there was genius behind,
patiently awaiting an outlet. It seemed that the
opportunity had arrived for Lewis when this
“Tattersall” dispute cropped up, and many
prophets arose to predict the triumph of the
“brainy” State Premier. There is reason to
believe that the gentle Lewis himself was per-
suaded that the Hour had arrived and that Neil
Elliott Lewis, C.M.G. was the Man. He
entered his protest against the Federal Govern-
ment’s action in refusing to allow “TattersaLl”
letters to be carried by mail. In the course of a
carefully-edited epistle to Premier Barton it was
politely urged: (1) That “Tatt.” should be
allowed to carry on his sweeps until the High
Court could rule for or against the legality of the
Federal stoppage of letters ; (2) that the Federal
Government was infringing the spirit of Federation
by its rash interference with a State-sanctioned
institution; and (3) that Tasmania would lose
£ 20,000 a year by the proposed abolition of
“ Tattersall’s ” sweep business. This last point
was stressed as the most important. Barton sent
a cordial reply stating that he was glad to hear
that nothing more formidable than a loss of
revenue to Tasmania was the cause of his learned
friend’s distress, and assuring him that the
Federal Government would find a means of
making that prospective loss good. Then the fat
was in the fire, and Lewis is said to have come
nearer to saying a naughty word than ever in his
life before. He had either to stand forth as the
champion of a gambling monopoly, which would
be too shocking a thing for his respectability;
or he had to tell Barton that Tasmania wasn’t a
mendicant and that his money might perish with
him, which was foreign to Lewis’s colorless habit
of mind ; or to reply that the assurance offered
was too vague, and that he had heard of Barton’s
promises before —which would have been a suit-
able reply, only Lewis hadn’t the fighting capacity
to say things like that; or he had to meander
vaguely about State rights, which is a large, pale,
uninteresting subject that convinces nobody and
interests hardly anyone. So Lewis comes out
badly in the fight. He has hit his antagonist
severely with a feather and made no impression.

The Superfluous Upper House.
“ Kiwi ” contributes his mite to the discussion :
Your comment as to the superfluity of a Second Legis-
lative Chamber (B. 12 3/’O2) is well illustrated by the posi-
tion of affairs here (Maoriland). In the grand old days of
squatterdom and plutocracy the Leg. Council was just
such an excrescence as the present Vic. House of 48 Rich
Landlords. The Ballancb-Seddon Govt, mended matters
somewhat by the infusion of a fairly strong democratic
element. But at the same time it reduced the term of
membership from life to seven years. Now what is the
position? The present party in power has made about
30 appointments out of a chamber of 45 members. The
“lifers”— i.e.. those appointed before’92, and to whom
the septennial term does not apply—are rapidly becoming
less already, and the Council possesses a majority of
Govt, appointees, so that it is merely a duplicate of the
Seddonian majority in the Lower House. Of what
logical use, therefore, is a second Chamber which, instead
of being a Constitutional check on rash legislation
is a mere automaton of its maker? About the only purpose
our Second Chamber has now is that of a place of reward
for political friends, and the mode of securing appoint-
ment to it is to toady in excelsis to our “ Uncrowned
King.” This would not be so bad if Richard selected the
best men among his friends, but he often stuffs into the
Council some village Hampden who has never sat in the
Lower House and has had no further training than the
chairmanship of some obscure local body. Now the coming
power in this and every country is the Labor Party—if it
doesn’t allow itself to be fooled by appointments to offices
of emolument. But our Labor Party, instead of demand-
ing the abolition of the Second Chamber, is fishing after
more appointments thereto of Labor members. No
clean-handed party can afford to handle the pitch of
patronage and the working men must realise this if
they are to do any real good. If the Seddon Govt, could
last another 10 years the Council would be practically all
Seddonite— there might be a small or large Opposition in
the Reps., but there would be none in the Council. And
the Council would consist largely of men that the people
wouldn’t dream of electing to the Lower House—not
intelligent democrats, but the parasites who fawned on
Seddon and breathed only by his permission. A Council
which rejects the Assembly’s measures must be suppressed,
because it can’t be tolerated that any mere nominee or
property suffrage body shall reverse the will of the people.
And a Council that 'merely says ditto to the Assembly
ought to be suppressed. It is uselesss and its erawlsome-
ness is unwholesome. There is no room for an Upper
House in any well-devised Legislature.

Westralia’s Railway Troubles.
The action of the Westralian Government in
raising its railway freights by 25 per cent, has
created a shriek of rage and consternation which
appears to be much louder than the case calls for.
The old rates were much below the Australian
average, and the increased ones are certainly not
above the average. It is pointed out that the
Victorian lines cost over £12,000 and the N.S.W.
ones over £13,000 per mile, while the Westralian
railways—being mostly built over State lands in-
stead of over private property resumed at twice
its value, and being of rather cheap and shoddy
construction at best, and being mainly in level
country—cost only a shade over £5OOO per mile.
With less than half the interest burden to carry,
therefore, they should be able to charge lower
rates. But on the other hand N.S.W. and Vic-
toria have accumulated over £8,000,000 of railway
deficit apiece through carrying goods and pas-
sengers below paying rates, and it would be sheer
folly for Westralia to go and do likewise. That
State is the only one which hasn’t a great
railway deficit. It did most of its rail-
way building after the gold and loan boom
set in, and it enjoyed such a run of luck that it
has, so far, realised a net profit of some £600,000
over and above working expenses and interest.
If its Government had had sufficient sense to
devote these profits to reducing the railway debt
then the system would have had a smaller burden
to carry in the lean years which are certain to
arrive sooner or later, but it hadn’t—things like
that are never done in Australia. In the glorious
times of 1896 and 1897 and 1900, Westralia’s rail-
ways and rolling-stock were, to a great extent,
new and required little repair, and if the profits
of these and adjacent years had been applied in
reduction of the railway debt it would merely
have been the ordinary, necessary, business-like
process of writing off depreciation— but that also
is never done in Australia. Now things are at
sixes and sevens. Of the 262 locomotives it is
frankly admitted by the Minister that 154 are
cripples, and the supply of goods waggons has run
so low, mainly through decay and lack of repair,
that there are not nearly enough available
to do the work. Also, the repairing
shops are inadequate, and there is a
congestion of halt, maimed and blind roll-
ing stock waiting for attention. If the £600,000
or so of profits made in the good days had been
used to reduce the railway debt there would be
£20,000 a year saved in interest and available for
repairs ; but these things didn’t happen. In the
good days, when the boom was at its height,
Westralia had cheap freights, and ran its rolling
stock and its cheap, new, shoddy railways to
death, and made no allowance out of the profits
for the old age that was creeping over its assets.
In 1901 there was no more railway than in 1900, and
the number of miles run was actually less, but
expenses were more, and the year’s profit
fell from £162,000 to £65,000, and for this the
general wearing out of things was at least partly
responsible. Now it is reckoned that the current
year will show a loss of £53,000, and sundry con-
cessions in the way of shorter hours promised to
the employes are expected soon to make another
£BO,OOO a year loss. By reason, of all this, the
increased rates are probably inevitable, but Wes-
tralia apparently doesn’t understand it, and it sits
among its 154 crippled locomotives and asks what
the trouble is all about.
4
Taking Steps.
Melbourne Age publishes an article on the
“continuous dribble” of population out of Vic-,
toria, and asks what is to be done. The popula-
tion, of course, continues to increase, for the births
largely exceed both the deaths and the emigra-
tion, but still the fact that there are more depar-
tures than arrivals is a depressing circumstance.
It isn’t a novel circumstance. There are regions
in the British isles that are now. more thinly
peopled than Victoria, and from which the popu-
lation is still going out, but that fact makes the
Victorian situation no better. The Age’s remedy,
as usual, is something loose, vague, and general,
after this fashion :
A Government which knows its duty would take instant
steps with the object of preventing other dissatisfied
farmers from abandoning their holdings. This and future
Governments will also have to be wary and cautious in
their treatment of capital. The greater the extent to
which capitalists invest in Victorian interests the greater
the amount of work there will be for residents in the
country. To make the moneyed class suspicious and to
hamper and restrict it unnecessarily i 3 nothing short of
suicidal.
Whether the Government should chain these men
to a gum-tree, or in what other way it should pre-
vent them going, is left unexplained. Nor is any
definite suggestion offered as to why the small
Victorian farmer is thus clearing out. Probably
there are at least six reasons for it—at least this
writer thinks he can recognise six : (1) There
is a tendency to go to countries like
Westralia and New South Wales where the
Government is still splashing about borrowed
millions at a glorious rate. While the splashing
process lasts there is a fictitious prosperity with
which a sober-minded State can’t compete. (2)
The small fertile area of Victoria is already about
four times as crowded as any other agricultural
district of the same size in Australia. (3) In this
area the price of land is absurdly high, and the
rents demanded by a bowelless landlord class are
grossly exorbitant. Also, even in this narrow
district settlement is retarded in places by the
existence of big estates held by unprogressive
monopolists. A very weighty progressive land-
tax on large estates, on estates owned by absentees,
and on estates which are not worked by their
owners but are sub-let, together with an energetic
system of compulsory repurchase by the State,
would make room for a lot more population.
(4) The eastern part of the State, mostly mountain
and forest, is worth something, though not very
much, but it is almost without roads. The western
part, which is none too good but is also worth
something, is without water. The land-tax above
proposed might bring in another £150,000 a year
with which East Gippsland might be opened up
with roads and the mallee country irrigated.
Then there would be room for a vast amount more
settlement than there is. (5) More railways are
wanted to carry the settlers’ produce to the coast.
Railways are expensive largely by reason of the
ridiculous sums demanded by unscrupulous land-
lords for compensation. A more honest class of
landlords would be beneficial. (6) Lower railway
freights are urgently wanted to make farming
profitable, but these are hardly possible until part
of the railway debt is paid off. A lavish passing
of the hat among the rich men of Victoria,
and the raising of a million as the nucleus of a
railway sinking fund would be a good start
in the direction of this most urgent reform, and
The Bulletin suggests that a subscription be
opened immediately. These proposals may be
regarded as crude and violent, but they at least
embody a definite scheme for doing something.
Anybody can talk in a vague, spectral fashion
about “ taking steps ” to prevent rack-rented
farmers leaving their holdings, and about the
necassity of the Government speaking kindly to
“ capital ” as if capital was a lost cat out on a wet
night, but there is nothing definite about such
matters. The world is full of people whose
remedy for everything is that somebody should
“ take steps,” and the world is full of step?
4
From Arthur Griffith, M.L.A. :
The agitation for the reduction of the ' nembershfp of
the'popular House in the State Parliaments developes
more and more clearly into an attempt by the wealthy
classes to destroy the democratic legislation of the last
few years, and to substitute for the men who brought it
about, what the Arohs calls “ a more desirable class of
public man.” In New South Wales it is primarily a land-
owners’ movement against the Land Tax. Look at the
personnel! The principal speakers at the inaugural
meeting (at Oamden) were : Onslow, of Camden Park (a
vast area of our best agricultural land, stretching from
Menangle to Camden, which one of our Governors jobbed
away to Onslow’s grandfather in the corrupt days ere we
had a Parliament) ; Antill, of Picton, the owner
of a similarly acquired estate ; Downs, of Brownlow
Hill Estate, and Moors, of “ The Oaks.” All worthy
men as private citizens, but representative only of a
small class whose grandfathers got hold of the best
of our lands when there was no Legislative Assembly,
and who, not unnaturally, hate the institution that has
dared to tax it. They would propose—not the reduction
—but the abolition of the Lower House, if they thought
they had a hope in that direction. If the men of this
class and their allies in the press can succeed in weaken-
ing or destroying the democratic element in our Assem-
bly, they may also succeed in obtaining a repeal of the
land and income taxes. As a matter of fact, it would
not need a very serious readjustment in the personnel of
the Assembly to accomplish this, and these people know
it. We cannot, surely, be expected to believe that men
of wealth and influence, who hitherto have taken no part
in public matters (except to oppose the land and income
taxes), have been suddenly smitten with a patriotic
enthusiasm for an economy of some 2£d. per inhabitant
annually—the amount to be saved by abolishing the
salaries* of 30 or 40 members of Parliament. No 1 they
have a much bigger thing on 1
