Prahran (Melb. ) City Council has rejected, by
seven votes to four, a proposal to open the local
public library for a few hours on Sundays. Never-
theless, the defeat in this case gives promise of
victory all along the Melbourne line. Sab-
batarian arguments against the opening of libraries
and picture-galleries on the one day of the week
when the general public want them opened have
only to be published from time to time in cold
print to work their own undoing. When the em-
ployment of Sunday labor was referred to as an
“ evil ” inseparable from the proposed innovation
three of the Prahran councillors volunteered to
employ themselves in taking charge of the little
library on the “Day of Rest,” which is a day of
work for many people, particularly parsons. So
the humanitarian “gag” was silenced. “The
thin end of the wedge for the introduction of a
Russia, 1902.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Peasants raided the State granary at Turkalinsk, in the
province of Tomsk, Sibera. One hundred peasants died
of famine prior to the revolt.
Five hundred infantry in Poltava declined to fire on a
crowd of people, which included women and girls. The
soldiers declared they would not shoot women. All the
soldiers were tried bv court-martial, and every tenth man
shot. The remainder were degraded and sentenced to
penal servitude.
Peasants arrested for rioting have been knouted—in
many cases to death.—Gable News.
The hissing tongue of horse-hide black,
Swung by the Cossack’s hand :
The dark blood from the peasant’s back
Congealing on the sand.
With bubbling breath, to brutal scoff,
Life quits the writhing lips,
And thy life’s sun, O Romanoff !
Veers to its last eclipse.
The twice ten thousand royal spies,
The million butcher braves,
Siberia’s mines, Saghalien’s sties,
The white Steppes’ untold graves—
The knout, the rod, the axe, the cord,
The battue on the plain,
The priest’s own God, the Czar’s own Lord,
Are impotent and vain.
The tabernacled eikons quake,
The palace beauties pale,
The loosened knees of Order shake
Though Law girds on its mail.
For through flogged women’s wailing breath,
And groans of knouted men,
And sobs of childhood clemmed to death
The Terror speaks again.
Hail! to the Terror! Hail! All hail!
The dagger, bomb, and brand :
Hail Hell itself —if Hell avail —
To purge the accursed land.
Knouted to death ! Fate says not so.
Knouted to life at last,
To render back, straight, blow for blow,
With interest on the past.
F.M.

FACILE PRINCEPS.
SEMPER IDEM.
BUCHANAN WHISKY.
Continental Sunday ” is another aged British
“gag” that doesn’t seem to meet the
situation in Melbourne. For there the Saw-
bathian street corner is apt to present a
frivolous appearance, inasmuch as numbers
of young men in ungodly felt, hats are fooling
around, with nothing but girl and blasphemy to
engage their thoughts, what time their distant
English relatives are having a comparatively con-
tinental time at the public-house. In a country
whose pet vice is street-loafing, the Sunday library
and picture-gallery would have the unanimous
support and all-round blessing of the police, and
yet the Mayor of Prahran assured his four fiery
radical councillors that the opening of any insti-
tution, save church, during church hours would
be a Disgrace. Also, the majority of seven
accepted his worship’s utterances as an everlasting
truth. But the voice of the average mayor is not
always going to be the voice of God in these
matters.

In general dramatic interest “ Boy Jim ”
is not on a par with “The Gates of Bondage,”
previously staged by the Woods-Williamson co.
at Sydney Criterion. The drama looks as if it
were merely hung round the great fight for the
championship in Act IV. The man who enacts
the Gloucester champion is a professional pugilist
and Alf. Woods a good amateur, so it is not to be
wondered at that some man in the audience lost
their heads on the opening night and betted on
one or other of them, forgetting that the real pug.
has to be knocked out and that backing him is
utter waste. One man realised this when it was
too late, and his cries about what he called “me
splosh ” were heartrending. Ted Wilson falls with
reluctance, while Alf. Woods’ stage mother breaks
into the ring, and, having concealed Alf's sonship
for years for no reason at all, proclaims it publicly
from the same motive. The rest of the play con-
sists mostly of modern rifle and old-fashioned
overcoat —also modern overcoat —with allusions to
co-operative stores that didn’t exist in the days of
Rodney Stone. Rodney himself is a dreadful
sketch, which mercifully fades away as the
drama progresses. Somebody called Dorothy
Clyde appears from nowhere, in order to be res-
cued from a badly-dressed and starved-looking
nobleman. There is a great deal of explanation in
this piece, about a ghost which is not produced,
about a crime which is not seen, and about a
hidden lord, who is brought forward at the last
moment in a dressing-gown and slippers, in which
costume he is understood to have glode about a
certain house for many to escape justice.
» * * *
The Bulletin is confirmed in its good
opinion of Alf. Woods, whose rendering of an
adventurous youth is artistic and free from
romantic exaggeration. The manner in which he
depicts that youth in a sulky humor, with a dis-
inclination to honest toil (this is a sign of noble
blood on the stage), has many clever touches
about it. Miss Williamson, as his actress-ma,
looks imposing in black velvet. The part is about
as melancholy as the sea on a wet day, but no
fault can be found with Miss Williamson’s render-
ing of it. Harry Douglas is a nice, good-
humored baronet, but does not give the necessary
impression of a middle-aged dandy, the set of
whose necktie is a matter of life or death.
The village blacksmith is neatly acted by John
Hesford, and various nice girls romp on and off in
early nineteenth century costumes for purposes
that are mostly ornamental. The referee at the
championship fight on Saturday night found the
publicity too much for him and gibbered through
the only sentence he had to say, but he refereed
with promptness and decision which, after all,
is the main thing.
** * «
No new ideas of any importance concern-
ing the “Runaway Girl” have occurred to The
Bulletin since last week. Further study of that
cheerful nothing makes the indefinite lay brother
with the shorn and shaven head retreat further
into the background, and the large fat British
tourist appear a person of diminishing importance,
while the whirl of skirts and stockings in the
“Man from Cook’s” hurdle race comes further to
the front as the p. incipal episode of the play. So
far all the ladies have jumped beautifully, and
none of them have fallen, and there is no sign of
any one running stiff. It seems to be the only
race in Sydney which is absolutely above suspicion.
The city has now made up its mind definitely as
to which lady shows the most alarming amount of
stocking as she jumps. It is the same lady every
night, and the public has ascertained, by inquiry
at all the leading drapers, that stockings like these
can’t be purchased for less than 10s. fid. a pair.
** * *
At Sydney Palace, the World’s Enter-
tainers are entertaining in about the same way as
usual, and comedian Platt still relates how, as a
doctor, he cured a baby (“ given up ” by the whole
faculty) so thoroughly that it got to a weight of
b., simply by prescribing elephants’ milk as
a diet—it was an elephant’s baby, by the way.
“The Merry World ” has entered upon a second
edition, which is as merry as the first. Bunth and
Rudd have withdrawn the foolish Chinese act and
returned to something’ funny.
** * *
“In Sight of St. Paul’s ” has negotiated
its second week at Sydney Lyceum, where the
great fire and the rest of the realism
make the audience forget that the hero is
an imbecile and Mrs. Bland Holt a lodg-
ing-house slavey too self-sacrificing for this
world. The Cathedral scene in the last act
is as impressive as ever, and calculated to send the
audience home in a religious frame of mind. St.
Paul’s has no special bearing on the drama, and,
as almost every story happens in view of some
large building or mountain or other landmark,
there is no great relevancy about the title of the
play. But it is a title that sounds well and is easy
to invent.
** * *
Harris and Walters, new-comers at Sydney
Tivoli on Saturday, had a cordial welcome from
the audience but did not impress the writer as
more than a comedian who jokes with difficulty and
a lady who sings with the same substance. Peggy
Pryde is still the prime favorite, and doesn’t sing
the “ Honeysuckle and the Bee ” now; so she
deserves to be. Callahan and Mack come a good
second in public favor.
** * *
At the Newbury-Spada concerts the popu-
lace has been recuperating its jaded sense of hear-
ing, every Wednesday and Saturday night. The
ear that has been long afflicted by such discords as
street bands, ’buses racing over blocks, trams and
the Australian accent, takes considerable healing,
and the Newbury-Spada pair pour in the balm
with musicianly skill. Next Saturday night' will
be a great one for Scotland, the pop on that
occasion being a Scotch pop.
** * *
“ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” was staged at the
Sydney Empire on Saturday, while The Bulle-
tin’s representative was at another theatre look-
ing at another Shock. When said representative
called at the venerable little house on Monday
it was closed, owing, it is said, to the illness of
Mr. John Cosgrove. John was billed for the villain
Marks, and brother Tom as his namesake of the
Cabin. Thomas, who is rather a comedian than
anything else, probably ascended to Heaven on
strictly humorous principles ; but all these things
are now guesswork. A large number of ‘new
names appeared on the bill, and the remark that
the negro slaves would be personated by a host of
auxiliaries sounded large and satisfying, but
whether the host were three or five this writer
cannot say. It is alleged that the Empire will
re-open on Saturday.
#* * *
If Melbourne felt a violent admiration for
Miss Janet Waldorf, the present “ Romeo and
Juliet” show, at Her Majesty’s, would perhaps be
better liked than a more massive and “ reverent ”
staging of that corpse-strewn romance, such as the
public suffered cheerfully for the sake of Mrs.
Potter-oum-Bellew, or Miss Jenyns, who had no
Bellow to help her. As far as a tearful five-act
Shakespearian drama, with five dead bodies in it,
can be made quick and interesting, this perform-
ance supplies the want. The Bard’s dialogue has
been judiciously pruned. One misses some of the
original superfluous characters, and doesn’t mourn
their absence. The general working of the business
seems to study economy in time, as well as in
scenery. Better for the spectacular effects to be
underdone than overdone, seeing that “Romeo
and Juliet ” offers no opportunities for gorgeous
frivolity. Parts of the performance are good, the
Mercutio (H. H. Vincent), and Friar Lawrence
(Atholwood) being very good. Edmund Gwenn—
the clown in “ As You Like It ’’—makes a quaint,
incisive Peter, whilst Mrs. Hill, as the old nurse,
and Melford and Miss Lane, as the parental
Capulets, put in careful work. Elsewhere feeble-
ness is the complaint. Some of the young men
of Verona who have little to do are not equal to
doing it for all it is worth, although they carry
their clothes confidently.
* * * •
Of Miss Waldorf and Norval McGregor in
the name parts, the present scribe would say that
they are overweighted most of the time. They
fail respectably. They arouse no enthusiasm and
excite no scorn in the audience. Romeo shows a
youthful figure and a neat style, and Miss Waldorf
is a rather pretty Juliet with a considerable talent
for acting. But her husky elocution, which has
the mannerisms of Mrs. Potter and Miss Duggan,
is so indistinct that it grows wearisome in long
speech. Next Saturday (17th) “Twelfth Night”
will be played for the first time since the Essie
Jenyns period.
** * *
At the Royal a tall medical gentleman
continues to disguise himself in a huge false nose
for obscure purposes of crime. Also a street arab
enters the service of a horse trainer, and is
allowed to wear his golden hair in a chignon at
the back, out of respect for the ’orses, or because
he is the missing child of a widow who runs the
racing stable and loves the trainer. But as the
boy’s relation to the sporting wid. is not suspected
until he has pulled her out of a cistern, she can
hardly be the reason why he doesn’t get his hair
cut. “ The Favorite ” threatens to win the Leger
for another two or three weeks, anyhow, and in
the meantime the mystery of Willard’s fictitious
nose will be further investigated, together with
the sad case of Stirling Whyte, a bookmaker who
says he loves to lose money in the interests of
sport.
** * *
Frank Thornton and co., at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, are still engaged upon the case of Mrs.
Smith, the parson’s wife, who goes to roost in the
right Mr. Smith’s bedroom, which opens out of
that unpractical citizen’s sitting-room and leads
to a long string of complications. “Facing the
Music ” is a sufficiently brisk and ingenious farcical
comedy of the old type. When the wrong Mrs.
Smith meets her lawful parson in the other Smith’s
apartment, the gentle shepherd doesn’t explain
the situation to her, and although the other
Smith could squelch the misunderstanding at one
verbal blow as soon as he arrives upon the scene,
he prefers to go mad every time the female in-
truder crosses his domestic path. Also the wrong
Mrs. Smith is persuaded by a casual detective to
play the part of the right Smith’s housekeeper
and serve his wife with breakfast, whilst the real
housekeeper, who presumably makes the coffee,
stands around in the kitchen, or somewhere, and
says nothing. The little leaven of probability
that leavens the whole lump of extrava-
?ance_ in some farces is not discernible
in ‘Facmg the Music,” nevertheless Thornton
a nd 00. make it travel. And the curtain-raiser,
thanks to Joseph Wilson’s clever character acting,
is a bright streak of eccentricity. Thusfar the
ladies of the co. have not improved oh acquaint-
ance, with the exception of Miss Katie Lee. This
trim little body would be a striking reminder of
her late sister Ada if she were more exactly like
the original Jennie, and she seems to have come
provided with a fair share of the family talent.
** * *
In “Why Smith Left Home” the new
cook, the different count, and other members of
a reconstructed cast were quite at home after the
first performance or two, and the angel-cake
would, doubtless, have been hovering around
Smith's auntie all this week if the Bijou manage-
ment were not pressed for time. “The Wrong
Mr. Wright ” was due for revival on Wednesday,
at the instigation of Special Request, who is first
u? US j n to ®' enera .l Desire. The millionaire in the
blood-red vest will bloom again for three nights
only, after which “ What Happened to Jones ” is
going to *ry the effect of new faces in the old
parts. Then the Willoughby Comedy Co. will
tear itself away in the direction of Adelaide.
** * *
At the Opera House all goes well with
Wootwell. Also the charming little lady accom-
plice of the gentleman in the violet clothes is pro-
gressing favorably, and the programme includes
some items which were not there last week.
** * *
Adelaide Royal has been doing record
business with “Sweet Nell.” House full every
night for two weeks, with one piece, and that not
an opera. It has now declined upon the “ Sorrows
of Satan” and other people, without Nellie Stewart.
The Tivoli is closing down for a month or so,
after two years uninterrupted vaudeville (another
record for Adelaide.) Rickards proposes to lease
the house occasionally, while transferring his co.
to Broken Hill and elsewhere. “ The Wrong Mr.
Wright” accordingly appears at this house.

State revenue returns:

Things, however, are made to look a little better in the
Government’s statement of accounts by reckoning cer-
tain trust funds—the rents derived from other people’s
property in the resumed area of Sydney—as revenue,
after this fashion :
Revenue (Rents of
Resumed Area of Expenditure. Deficit.
Sydney included.)
1901 .. £8,765,689 £8,943,346 £177,657
1902 .. 9,136,552 9,522,271 385,719
Increase.. £370,863 £578,925 £208,062
The large increase of revenue is more than swallowed up
by the still larger increase of expenditure. Last year
ended with a heavy deficit, and so far as the current year
has gone things are much worse than they were last year.
As regards the loan expenditure it also increases daily :
New South Wales Loan Expenditure.

to to Increase.

£2,287,155 £4,072,464 £1,785,309
As all the loan money already raised has been spent, and
over £3,340,000 has been temporarily borrowed from
trust funds and other sources to keep the loan account
going, another large raising of money should be about
due in New South Wales.
During the last ten months N.S.W. has had
really £BlO,OOO more revenue to deal with than it had
last year. The loss of £570,000 postal receipts was coun-
terbalanced by the fact that the Federal Government
also took over the expenditure of this wholly unprofitable
department, therefore this can’t be reckoned a decrease.
So without including the rents of the resumed area,
which are not revenue in any fair or justifiable
sense of the term, the See-O’Suilivan Government is
getting extra revenue at the rate of very nearly £1,000,000
a year. Old-age pensions absorb fully half of that, leaving
about £500,000 a year to deal with. Increased road votes
and increased wages and other expenses on the railways
and tramways, have swallowed up part of this—perhaps
£300,000. The increased interest bill, that ever-growing
octopus which gets larger with every loan that is floated,
probably accounts for the rest. In addition, £338,000 has
been spent beyond the increased revenue. Before the
Commonwealth started there was great argument about
“ What N.S.W. would do with its surplus.” The surplus
has been done with all right. The problem of what
N.S.W. will do with its deficit is the real trouble now.
* * * *
Queensland shows up a little better now than it
did at this time last year :
Queensland (10 months to 30th April.)
Revenue. Expenditure. Surplus.
1901 .. £3,503,294 £3,358,095 £145,199
1902 .. 3,017,608 2,804,780 212,828
Decrease £485,686 £553,315
Increase _ £67,629
Queensland has about £737,000 to pay in interest at the
end of June (every year that devouring monster, the
interest-bill, grows £40,000 to £60,000 larger by reason of
fresh borrowings), so it looks as if the deficit’would not
be far short of £500,000 after all. Yet (allowing for the
fact that the postal revenue does not appear now in the
State balance-sheet), the revenue for the year ended June,
1902, promises to be far larger than it was in 1898 or any
previous year—and in these days Queensland paid its
way, and had something over. There is almost sufficient
increase since 1898 to cover the extra interest payable
through increase in the public debt. The trouble arises
solely out of the fact that the Philp Government (allowing
for the fact that postal, telegraph, customs, and excise
expenditure no longer appear in the State balance-
sheet) is spending at the rate of nearly £650,000
a year more than the Bvrnes and Nelson

Affairs in South Australia are thus £181,140 worse than
they were at this date last year, and last year ended with
a small deficit, which was only turned into a small sur-
plus by treating certain trust funds as revenue. S.A.’s
situation is about the worst of the lot.
3tralia ’ s revenue for April (£342,718) was
±89,066 more than the revenue for April, 1901. It was
also £10,793 more than the largest previous month’s
revenue c ver collected in the State The fact that Groper-
land gets the full benefit of the Federal tariff, and is also
allowed (as no other State is) to collect its inter-State
duties just as before (a privilege of which it avails itself to
“ e u ™ e rmost farthing) is responsible for this (the fact
that the first 6d. received as revenue from the Ooolgardie
water scheme came in last month, when the supply
reached Northam, has nothing to do with the case, for the
coin was embezzled out of the consolidated revenue and
jpven to Bigjohn to hang on his watch-chain). Yet the
Westralian Federal members mostly preach Freetrade,
and, instead of urging their own State Government to get
a large measure of Freetrade by abolishing its border
duties, they demand further reductions of the Federal
tariff. Westralia, with its border duties, can do with a
lower tariff than the other States which have no border
duties, and the W.A. Freetrade members have as much
regard for the other States as a rhinoceros might have.
Next October, the first reduction of 20 per cent, in the
Westralian inter-State tariff falls due, and, after that, it
is to be reduced by 20 per cent, annually till it wholly
disappears, so the present boom is only temporary. Mean-
while, there is already a surplus of £259,775, and out of
that Premier Leake proposes to build the Port Hedland
to Marble Bar railway and a section of the Maloolm-
Laverton railway, also to purchase the Oanning-Jarrah
line. The proposal to build even one or two small lines
out of revenue is a shock to the old borrowing tradition
that is calculated to make somebody fall down dead from
sheer astonishment.

• » • •
The great law case, Wadey v. Mort’s Dock and
Engineering Co. (Sydney), ended with a verdict against
the co. for £11,139. Costs are said to be about £lO,OOO
which makes roughly £21,000. Also, it is said that both
parties intend applying for a new trial. It is a case of
such dimensions that it takes 35 days to explain it, and
the possibilities in the way of getting new trials (at
several thousand pounds each) are very large. Mort’s
Dock Co. has £30,570 of reserves, and the Wadey case
looks quite capable of swallowing them all.
* • • *
Melbourne stockbroker Meudell, who seems to
have bought a calculating machine at a bargain sale, and
is heaving vast masses of figures about, made some quaint
observations in Melb. Age the other day. Among other
things he threw these figures at an unprepared public :
Victoria. France.
Persons per Member of Parliament 7000 43,000
Debt per head £54 2 0 £3l 3 0
Total trade per head .. .. 29 18 9 92 0
Revenue per head 6 14 9 8 13 0
Expenditure per head .. .. 613 2 313 0
Interest per head 1 18 6 0 16 11
This, of course, is the same old swindle by which the
lightly-taxed rich men of Victoria are trying to strike
public sympathy as an oppressed and down-trodden race.
The affair is worked by comparing the French national
revenue (which does not include any railway receipts,
these going to private companies) with the national
revenue and the railway receipts of Victoria. The Vic-
torian pays annually £2 15s. 3d. in railway fares, which
are not taxation, seeing that he gets better value for them
than the Frenchman does for the fares he pays to private
companies. He pays ss. to make up the railway deficit,
which arises from the fact that, in proportion to popula-
tion, he gets four times the railway accommodation that
the Frenchman does. And he pays about £3 14s. 6d. in
ordinary revenue, against the Frenchman’s alleged £3 13s.
—it should be £3lss. 2d. And as both Victoria and France
are getting deeper in debt through insufficiency of
revenue the sooner Peacock increases the above taxes the
better. If Meudell doesn’t like it he can become a
Frenchman and call himself “ Mossoo.”

Inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted

Vanning Tables. —The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesle.v-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*
• ♦ • *
Electricity in mining is being more extensively
adopted every day. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, the Australian representa-
tives of Ganz and Co., have just installed at the Great
Southern ' onsols mine, Rutherglen, a complete plant for
the underground haulage, doing away with the old costly
system of trucking. The work is now done by electric
locomotives at a much reduced cost, the whole of the
drivers and surface works is also lighted from the same
installation. This company has also supplied electric
pumping plants to the Sons of Gwalia mine, and probably
the largest electric pumps in the world for the Charlotte
Plains and New Hapilah mines at Maldon. Having
secured the services of a well known European expert,
they are now prepared to advise, and contract for any
electrical service such as lighting, pumping, haulage,
traction, rock drilling, or the generation and transmission
of power on the latest and most approved methods, and
invite inquiries.*

- Insurance policies are in most of the States pro-
tected by statute against the creditors of the assured, but
the protection has been rendered worthless, in the many
cases in which the assured leaves a will containing the
words, “ after payment of my just debts,” by the con-
struction the courts have put upon those words. After a
big struggle with the Leg. Council, the law was recently
altered by a cumbrous section which protected policies
even although these words occurred in the will, except in
certain cases. But the good old coach-and-four has
already found a metalled road through this merciful little
charter of the widow and orphan. Some of the most im-
portant safeguards in the section merely apply, it is being
found, to insurance policies payable only on death. See-
ing that, nowadays, a large proportion, perhaps the
majoritv, of policies are payable on the attaining of a
certain age as well as on death, a speedy amendment of
the law is desirable—until which will-makers who do not
want their wives and children to starve, can be recom-
mended to make their policies safe by simply omitting
the quite unnecessary words “upon payment of my just
debts ” from their wiil9.

The co. appears to be highly prosperous, hut its balance-
sheets are a bewildering aggravation. They lump to-
gether interest on deposits, which is a fixed charge, and
dividends to shareholders, in one lump sum. The balance
at the beginning of the year is shown, also the balance at
the end after paying these amounts, but it being im-
possible to accurately disentangle the dividend the exact
profits can’t be got at. Sometimes the report states that
the dividend is 8 and 9 per cent. Sometimes it merely
savs the usual dividend is paid. And now and then it is
accidentally mentioned in the report—not in the balance-
sheet—how much the profits come to and the sum
absorbed in dividends. This habit appears to become
more frequent of late. The capital now amounts to
£7O 425, so the total shareholders funds are 1 j8,d70.
Deposits are £95,906, and the institution holds cash and
liquid securities, £BO4O. The dividend is unusually mag-
nificent for these rather unprofitable times.

The contest between the English Jews and the
American Jews for the monopoly of the British tobacco
trade is hot. The English Jews having offered a big bonus
to retailers if they would handle their goods only and
boycott the American people’s stuff, the latter have
replied offering to give up their entire net profits
in England for the next four years, and also
£BOO,OOO in cold cash, as bonuses to those retailers who
stand by them ; and they ask for no boycott of the other
man’s goods, condemning that as “ un-English ” ! So far
the American Jews seem to have the best of it; they’ve
got the money and got the tactics too.

Some recent figures relating to the Northern
Steamship Co. of Auckland Maoriland : >
, Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves
March, 1900 7 per cent. = £3589 £37,825
„ 1901 £7380 7 „ „ 4967 40,238
„ 1902 4797 7 „ „ 4875 40,160
Probably the reserves will prove £4OO less than this, the
annual meeting having a habit of voting the directors that
sum as honorarium after the balance-sheet is published.
The earnings are steadily increasing, but an unusual
expenditure in repairs causes an apparent falling off.
Paid up capital is £69,642, so by comparison the reserves
are very solid. The one mysterious and unpromising
item in the balance-sheet of the Northern Steamship Co.
is the very large liability described as “ Accounts owing
by Company.” In 1900, this stood at 133,712 ; in 1901 it
was £31,520 ; by the present year it had risen to 136,118.
This is an enormous sum, equal to more than half the
paid-up capital, and whether it includes bank overdraft,
mortgages or open accounts, or all three of them, it
suggests that the co. is trading on very insufficient
capital and is consequently' in difficulties. The entire
shareholders’ capital and reserves only amount to
£109,802, and as the co. has £131,268 invested in
ships alone, to say nothing of the money locked up in
premises, it seems that being deeply' in debt must be its
chronic state. It has practically no liquid assets, and
apparently no loose cash to pay its dividend with ; and
at the present rate of progress the process of accumulating
funds with which to pay off the liabilities promises to be
a work of many years. The balance-sheet does not show
how much this state of things costs the co. in interest,
but the situation suggests that it would be highly advis-
able to reduce the dividend to 5 per cent, and keep it at
that till the co. is able t» trade with its own funds
exclusively.

The Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative Society of
Sydney and Richmond River, N.S.W., sends along its
balance-sheets since its formation two years ago :
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
March, 1901 - £3Ol 6 per cent. = £45 £256
f 6 „ „ 1041
„ 1902 - 1528 -! 20 p.c. bonus to V 418
( consignors = 1262 J
The Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative Society looks like a
very prosperous and promising concern which might
easily come to a bad end, though there is very little posi-
tive evidence to go upon. It has £1753 paid-up capital,
so the total shareholders’ funds are £2171. It owes
£11,896, and it has owing to it “ outstanding accounts
due to society, £12,849.” The business is growing very-
fast, and this item is growing along with it. Not
being a coastal farmer, writer _ has no clear idea
by whom these amounts are owing or how they are
secured, but it certainly looks as if the failure of
one debtor might pretty well wipe out the society,
and as if it would be advisable to accumulate some
solid reserves as a protection. The private co., or
private dealer, doesn’t give any bonus to consignors ;
this is bad for consignors, but, in the same circumstances,
he would be £1262 further ahead as a guarantee against
accidents. If consignors didn’t reach out for more than
15 per cent, at first, the institution might get more
securely on its legs.

The Lion Brewery and Malting Co. (Adelaide)
concluded a rather desolate half-year on 31st January,
1902. The last two years’ record has been as follows :
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
January, 1900 £226
July, 1900 £2217 6 per cent. = £lBlB 625
January, 1901 1416 5 „ „ 1818 223
July, 1901 2494 6 „ „ 1818 899
January, 1902 976 6 „ „ 1818 67
This is not a promising position for a co. which has kept
up the beer-struggle for 14 years. The Lion Brewery was
floated in 1888, the vendors guaranteeing 10 per cent,
dividends till 1892. Since then the dividends have
never risen above 5 per cent. The co. has a paid-
up capital of £74,240. A small amount of forfeited
capital has apparently gone into the business and been
absorbed—certainly it doesn’t appear in the form of any
reserve fund. Within the last two years the liability on
mortgages, loans, and bank overdraft has been reduced
from £26,638 to £25,053, but this has only been done by
reducing the stock. The Lion Co. might as well face the
fact that it isn’t earning 5 per cent, just at present, and
cut down its dividend to 4 per cent. Also, for a co.
which is so heavily burdened, there are good points about
3 per cent.

Broken Hill Proprietary pays a div. (£48,000),
the first this year. Last half year the co. made a profit
of £56,293 without allowances for depreciation ; it never
reports what its metals realise, but apparently the prices
were £ll lls. 6d. for lead and 2s. o£d. for silver. These
times a Id. per oz. movement in silver is, so far as the
B.H. Prop, is concerned, equal to an Bs. per ton movement
in lead. In regard to the other B.H. cos. a movement
in silver is less important. On the figures quoted the big
co. can’t be making quite a shilling div. (£48,000) per half
year, even allowing nothing off for depreciation. Of course,
iittle economies are still being introduced, but all the big
schemes one hears of hang fire somehow. There is
snarling and snapping again between the B.H. Prop, and
the Silverton Tramway Co. The former says the latter is
not taking its fair share of slump-results. The big mining
co. says that it paid it to build a line of its own to the
Iron Knob, and it might pay to build another cheap 30
miles from the mine to the S.A. line at Cockburn. More
will be heard of this. ’JJhe B.H. Prop. Co. is actually
putting up at its own expense a reading and writing room
for the men who work at the desulphurising kilns—a
little isolated hell a few miles out of town, along the rail-
way. General-Manager Delprat takes an interest in such
things—the first of the line who has done so.
* * * •
Block 10 shares are firmer “ in consequence of
the co. having made a profit on the past six months
operations. This is very satisfactory in view of the low
price,” &e. And it is. But the ore in this mine has been
picked during the last six months as it never was picked
before. When it was seen lately that a small profit was
being made it was suggested by some of the directors
that it should be doubled by putting the mill on two
shifts instead of only one. But it couldn’t be done;
there was not enough of the best ore available to do it.
Of course this skimming of the mine is going to make
Block 10 a tougher problem by-and-bye.

A few days since, the cabled price of silver was
3-16ths of a penny under 2s. This is probably the lowest
figure at which the metal has been quoted. There has
been a recovery of about Jd., which encourages the silver-
mine speculators. It speaks volumes for the management
of the B.H. mines that, despite the low prices of silver
and lead, they can pay divs. Had the business been
understood at the outset as it is now, what a harvest
would have been reaped 1 A slight fall in Proprietary
shares is attributed, not so much to the low price of
metals, as to the large holding of the late James Dick
being placed on the London market.

“Sessil”: “Glen Dale” tinfield, Dry Creek,
Bundarra district (N.S.W.), was found by J. Robinson,
three years since : now three parties on field, including
Robinson’s, Pearce’s, and Ready’s. As usual, all are
hampered by lack of water, having washed no dirt since
last year. About 300 loads at grass. Country a succes-
sion of gutters, running from edge of tableland hard by.
Within short distance latter shows numerous lode indica-
tions. Pearce tunnelled for atime with 7s. 6d. per foot Govt,
grant, and got good specimens right through, but had to
abandon work through lack of means. Wash will go a
fair 401 b. per load all through gutters, and is from 3 to
4ft. average depth. Carries dark grey metal, and there
are other indications that the alluvial field is not of great
extent.
• * * •
“ C.P.” : Ryder and party’s “ Commonwealth ”
block of 40 odd acres, near the head of Bora Creek
(N.S.W.), is interesting as being the top of the diamond
drift, so far. The stones increase in size as this is fol-
lowed up, and from Ryder’s have come some of the
finest yet found. Worked for five years under the most
primitive conditions, without machinery, etc. the tin won
paid all working exes., the diamonds being clear profit.
Gold and silver are also found. Should any remarkable
diamondiferous discovery be made in the district, it will
probably be near this claim.
Shuttleton mine, near Nym agree (N.S.W.) said
to rival Great Cobar in its palmiest days. Option-holders
have completed purchase—£3o,ooo cash, which they have
netted from shipping ore for less than half the time of the
option. Melbourne has appropriated the plum, and a £5
rise in copper would probably mean a £500,000 flotation.
Property was offered to a Sydney firm for a modest sum
in four figures. Reports were submitted to a well-known
expert for his opinion, and he returned them with this
comment: “ 111-conceived lies ; it would be waste of time
to inspect show.” Now he is a doubly-cursed man—he
joins his principals in anathematising himself.

Corowa jubilant over the discovery of gold in the
Deep Lead mine. Good as prospects are reported to be,
co. has a large amount of dead work to do ere it can hope
for payable returns. It has made more calls than any other
mine in N.S.W. Shareholders all local men, which means
that, practically, they are Victorians. Genuine Sydney-
siders would not have had the patience to stick to
a pumping show for five years, neither would they have
been clever enough to wheedle solid assistance out of the
Govt. The longrtalked-of scheme to form a big English
co. to work the late “Johnny” Wallace’s Quat Quatta
estate on the N.S.W. side of the river may now be heard
of again. The nucleus of this scheme will be the granting
of a special lease of 1000 acres, which, hitherto—always,
in the interests of labor—N.S.W. department has bucked
against. Estimated that to work the Rutherglen lead in
this area an initial expenditure of £lOO,OOO will be
necessary. * • .
Lady Grace Mine, Wyalong (N.S.W.)struck rich
mineral ore 30ft. east from the bottom of the underlie shaft
which, it is thus proved, hat been sunk on the wrong side
of the “ formation ” for a considerable distance. The
mine adjoins Neeld’s on the north. The new find is over
250 ft. deeper than Neeld’s bottom level. As the chutes
of ore pitch to the south what a prospect Neeld’s have
before them i
* * • •
Reported that an English co. has been formed
to work the Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) deep lead for tin.
V.O. shallow alluvial was as rich in tin, correspondingly,
as Forest Creek, Ballarat and Bendigo gullies were in
gold, and there can be no doubt that there are immense
bodies of pay wash in the basalt-capped deep ground. If
the co. picks up the gutter where it was last worked, if
it avoids the customary extravagances of London
management, and if the full capital is reasonably pro-
portionate to the actual working capital, writer, who
knows the locality well, believes that there will be
successful results, assuming that tin keeps above £lOO
per ton.
• • * •
Blayney (N.S.W.): New syndicate has started
to unwater the mine. Understood that only develop-
ment Work will be proceeded with at present.
Shaft to be sunk to 400 ft., and lode to be syste-
matically opened up between 300 and 400 ft. levels
before smelting is ■" ~ ed. Had the old co. been
content to pursue this policy, mine would never have
got into the dense cloud of disappointment and obloquy
which enveloped it.
• * • *
The Lloyd-Burraga copper-mine (N.S.W.) in
full swing again. Concentrating plant now working
fairly well. This mine, despite the disadvantage of
nearly 40 miles of mountainous road-carriage, shares the
distinction, with Great Cobar and Mt. Lyell, of being a
profit-earner with copper at £52 per ton, but, unlike the
other two, it has no precious-metal value to bolster it up.
The 15-year-old story about the lode dipping into an
adjoining block is once more in circulation. The late
Lewis Lloyd’s sardonic smile at the mention of this sug-
gestion ought to have been snap-shotted and printed for
the information of shareholders in the new co.
* • * *
Brown’s Creek mine, near Blayney (N.S.W.)
about to be unwatered which is regarded as
a “ hopeful sign that the mine will soon be
at work again, which will be a great boon to
the district.” The unwatering process looks h*®
sparring for wind by the owners who have been officially
notified that no further suspension of labor conditions
will be granted. Even if it were deemed advisable to
give the mine another trial, no practical man would
suggest attempting to pump the slurry out of the old
workings, which would be just about as sensible a thing
to do as it was to provide 12001 b. stampers to pulverise the
mud. The cheapest and most expeditious way to rework
the mine vvould he to sink a new shaft. Frank Gardner
is reputed owner of the property, and now that he is
wrestling with a project to amalgamate all the mines in
the “ Golden Mile ” of Kalgoorlie, it is hardly likely that
he is going to pay much attention to a show of the
Brown’s Creek calibre.
« • * »
Cabled that Sandford’sironworks at Lithgowhave
been bought by an English co. with a capital of
£750,000 another Australian industry going into
the hands of the absentee. As Sandford publicly
stated before leaving Sydney that all that he
wanted to complete the equipment was £250,000,
it seems that the new co. is overloaded. Rumored that
the promoters of the project for establishing extensive
iron works on the Parramatta River were so disgusted
with the bluff and deception of N.S.W. Ministers that
they withdrew all negotiations, and are now completing
arrangements for the establishment of the works on the
Yarra.

Charters Towers returns for April: Mills 16,212
tons, 17,4350z5. ; cyanide 11,1760zs ; total value, 190,000.
Dividends, £26,716; calls, £2633. Divs. for four months
of year, £108,233; calls, £14,066. Bonnie Dundee mine
now the.centre of expectations ; No. 2 shaft has reached
“ formation,” and there is much speculation as to when
the reef will be struck, but, of course, no one doubts that
it will be a bonanza.
* • • *
Mount Garnet: Shares selling at about 9s.
Furnaces shut down, and will stay so until mine is suffi-
ciently developed to keep them going continuously—
which some people have doubts of it ever doing. It is
three years since Mt. Garnet started, yet the depth
attained is only 150 ft. Two-thirds of the copper-mines in
Australia are closed for the same reason—failure to keep
development work well ahead. History will repeat itself
in this particular until such time as company promoters
and directors study the mines rather than the share-
market. Copper-mining, conducted on legitimate lines,
should be as much an industrial enterprise as iron manu-
facture. The man who would erect iron-furnaces and
railways and then set to work to find ore to keep them
going would be voted a lunatic.
* * * *
Smith’s Creek tin (N.Q.) is Adelaide’s favorite
gambling stock just now. Slump from £l3O to £9O in
two days, with subsequent recovery to £l2O. Local paper
seriously asserts that reason for slump was that the broker
who made a market for the scrip was out of town. Stock
to be watered to an extent which suggests that original
holders must have an insatiable thirst. Bulletin’s
advices are that it is a good prospecting show, but, on
developments, present value of shares is fanciful.
* m * *
Stannary Hills Tin Mine (N.Q.), during past
half-year, in developing work, raised 885 tons of 12 per
cent. ore. Directors ecstatic about prospects; results
will have to better these to make adequate return on the
abnormal capitalisation—£6oo,ooo. Co. heavily indebted
in several directions, and, handicapped as it is, is likely
to end in inglorious fizzle. Show owned and run by Ade-
laide men.

• * * *
Briseis (Tas.) nearing the crucial stage ; 57
sluice heads of water now being delivered at the mines,
and work is to be started on the Briseis hill over-burden
and at the Derby face. A few weeks ought, to determine
whether or not there is an intolerable deal of over-burden
and a disproportionate quantity of tin-bearing drift. On
the law of averages, Briseis is now at the good-selling
stage.
• * * *
How are the mighty fallen ! At Bendigo, in
March, 77 companies were getting gold, and only five
were on stone worth an ounce per ton. Sixty-two were
getting less than half-an-ounce, and 37 were grubbing
along for less than sdwt. Seven of them were pegging
away manfully for about ldwt. per ton. Out of the 77,
only eight scraped up a dividend during the month
And, when writer was in Bendigo, in ’Bl, the board that
was put up at the Beehive every second Saturday with
the list of dividends was about Bft. high, and it used to
be crowded sometimes, and, on dividend night, the only
sober men in the town were Alf Josephs and his partner,
Sloman. Bendigo doesn’t pay for the stockbrokers’
drinks nowadays. ♦
* * » •
Rumoured that the next big Yankee trade move
will be a reach out for England’s continental coal mar-
kets. Although the U.S. output has grown in the last
30 years from 30,000,000 to 250,000,000 tons per annum}
the enormous increase has only been about enough to
supply the ever-growing demands of the home market,
and export has been comparatively neglected. Now, ’tis
said, the plans are well forward for a sudden increase of
output that will enable them to close on a lot of the
European trade that brings England so much profit at
present.
* * * *
Already the over-sanguine workers find that
the N.S.W. Arbitration Act has spots on it. The miners
of Broken Hill, who under the contract system often take
home a most miserable wage, hoped for great things from
it; but the Maoriland Labor Department writes over, in
reply to a question, that the Act, in that State anyhow, is
powerless to prevent a man, or party of men, agreeing to
do a certain job at a wretchedly low rate. All it can do
is to prevent the contractors employing men at low
wages ; but that doesn’t get over the trouble, for a party
of miners on contract rarely employ anyone—a trucker
occasionally, but no one else.
* * * *
John Waddington, for long Chairman of the
Great Boulder, has sent a letter to the press protesting
against “Mr. Zebina Lane’s repeated to take
the whole credit for the success of the enterprise.”
Waddington says it isn’t true, as generally supposed, that
De Rose was one of the peggers ; “he did not go to Kal-
goorlie till some time after.” “ The mine was brought to
London by O. P. Doolette, and I purchased it from him,
paying him the deposit. The only part whioh Mr. Lane
played in connection with it was in giving his report as
the mining expert, for which he was paid.” When
markets slump the public gets the news.
• * * *
Late financial papers from London report rich
discoveries of banket in Portuguese territory N.W. of
Delagoa Bay. A Denver paper, sent to Australia by a
man en route to Alaska, reports the discovery of a large
lode in Montezuma which “ a strong man cannot walk
to the end of in a day ” ; value from £to loz. per ton. If
these reports are true the chance of much foreign capital
coming this way for a long time is not great, as Bull and
others will have something more attractive. This may,
perhaps, not be an unmixed evil for Australia. Poor
“ Portugoose,” though—it won’t take long for “ Brum.
Joe ” and his parasites to discover that England has a
prior claim to the country, or that a missionary has been
insulted, or some excuse will arise for annexing the
country if the goldfields are anything like the Rand. It
took a very few years for the British Government to find
that the Diamond Fields did not belong to the Orange
Free State but to a nigger named Waterboer, and almost
immediately after it had taken the fields on Waterboer 8
account it found that nigger’s title was all wrong—and
kept the Diamond Country. Probably some equally
handy nigger will turn up this time.

Moreton Frewen, who was captivating even
when he lectured on currency reform, has given bimetal-
lism best. In 12 years the gold harvest has more than
trebled, “ and it is entirely safe to estimate that in the
coming 12 years it will again double ”; so bimetallism
has become unnecessary. But he adds that the remark-
able variation in the yield of gold knocks out the theory
that “ gold was a fore-ordained material for a money-
standard because its production fluctuated least of all.”

SOCIETY
The ignominious defeat of Barton’s at-
tempt to raise Hopetoun’s salary another £BOOO a
year has resulted in an immediate cutting down
of exes at the big white house. Three or four
flunkeys—including the chief cook—got their sail-
ing tickets for dear old Britain. Also, the Earl
is said to have sworn a polite vice-regal oath
to spend no more than he can’t conveniently avoid
spending on pomps, vanities, and ’freshments for
the bong tong of this ungrateful country. Why
should he disburse more than his official income in
Australia when he has to “keep up” his places of
residence in Scotland and England? Echoanswers:
Why ? Australia doesn’t want the ruffled earl to
encourage snobbery on a lavish scale. It would
rather have him penurious than “popular,” in a
financial sense of the word. All the same, it objects
to any wealthy vice-regal person from abroad mak-
ing a private fuss about the alleged “ drain ”on his
unearned private increment, considering that he
would have to draw upon his own treasury for the
means of support himself wherever he might try a
change of air. Lord Hopetoun is greatly overpaid
for his Australian services. His salary, without
reckoning extras, is equal to that of the President
of the United States. His other riches are the
accident of birth. He is a spoilt child of fortune,
and, like all spoilt children, he grumbles at his
luck.
* * * *
The King and the Pope are getting along
very nicely indeed. Cable: —
The King has invited the Right Rev. Robert Brindle,
Bishop-Auxiliary to Cardinal Vaughan, R.C. Archbishop
of Westminster, to bless the colors of the regiment of
Irish Guards on June 24.
Another cable: —
Pope Leo XIII. has granted the Roman Catholics of the
British Empire a dispensation from fasting and abstin-
ence on Friday and Saturday, the 27th and 28th June.
Fair tit-for-tat.. The disloyal King solicits a
“Popish ” blessing on certain of his troops, and,
A POSER.
(Time, 1.45 a.m.) MRS. JOHNSON (loq.): "Now, Mr. Johnson, just you say 'Shibboleth.'”
o 1 t’other hand, the ancient of Rome allows his
flock to chew forbidden pork at King Ned’s Coron-
ation time. These things will surely be the death
of dear old Dr. Dill-Macky. And then the Pope,
when Cardinal Moran emptied out his Peter’s
pence, congratulated the Australians “on their
bravery during the war ! ” The Bulletin begins
to suspect the Pope of Dan O’Connorism.
»• •- * *
The decision of Judge Murray in the case
of that N.S.W. military man who gave his fellow
military man a loaded cigar that nearly blew his
features off, was rather rough on an officer and a
gentleman. The judge’s Own obvious'Opinion was
that he played a silly, vulgar and cruel joke—the
gutter-kid kind of joke that a grown man should
have left behind along with his childhood—and
that in denying all explosive intentions he swore
to what was wholly untrue. All of which was
not the thing that an officer and a gentleman is
accustomed to.
* * * •
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners request
passengers on Sydney trams to “destroy their
tickets, _on alighting from the cars.” The
result is that the street corners are strewn
with a litter of variegated paper-scraps, which
causes much profanity in the City Council
cleansing department. As every passenger—even
the most respectable—looks at his ticket number,
to see what sort of a eribbage or poker hand he
has, The Bulletin suggests that a solution of the
difficulty would be for the Commissioners to
establish a lottery, with weekly drawings and
prizes of value. Passengers would then hoard
their tickets, instead of strewing them about the
streets of a city which is dirty enough already.
* * * *
General Hutton, farewelling the sample
soldiers sent from Victoria to take part in the
military beauty show at the Coronation, told
them that it was “ a significant coincidence that
they were departing on the anniversary of the day
on which H.R.H. the Duke of York landed in
Australia.” The Commandant discovered the
finger of Providence in this, and The Bulletin
blushes to acknowledge that it pulled its boots
on, that auspicious morning, without observing
any significant coincidence. And yet that day
twelve months the Duke of York had pulled on
his boots for the first time in Australia as Duke
of York !
* • » *
The single-screw again ! The break-down
of the horse-boat Boveric, bound S’Africavvards,
may turn out a very gruesome business. The
vessel is drifting about in sub-tropical seas at the
mercy of the waves, 1500 odd miles from any-
where, with some 950 horses on board, and but 45
days’ fodder. The tale of that dumb freight may
unhappily contain more shocking and heart-
rending details than Cutcliffe Hyne put into
his story of the wrecked cattle-boat. Being such
a salvage-plum, however, it is unlikely that the
Boveric will be long without aid. The one bright
spot in the mishap so far is the putting off, when
1500 miles from land, of the first and second
officers and two men, in an open boat, in search of
aid. That the hero is possible in this day of the
notoriety-hunting crank is a joyful fact.
• * * *
Liverpool Asylum (N.S.W.), where the
consumption germs are grown, is in fresh trouble.
An inquiry is now proceeding into various charges,
and cross charges, made by various officials of the
unhappy institution. A Police-Magistrate is
president, and one indignant personage has re-
tained G. H. Reid, K.C., as counsel at the inves-
tigation.

THE LATEST LOVE-DODGE.
1ST. MAID: “Em leaving.” 2ND. M AID: “ What for?”
1ST. DITTO: “Em full of ping-pong people—they spend all their time under
the furniture spooning, and then growl like the deuce at every speck of dust.”
Maoriland railway department’s econo-
mical and happy-go-lucky system of train-running
on the unimportant branches (and very often on
the great trunk lines) has brought about another
smash, this time on the Otago Central—four
people badly injured and two trains just about
knocked to pieces. The cause of the accident was
the usual absence of check due to cheap working.
A guard and a driver received written instruc-
tions that they were to cross No. 6 down train at
Hindon. (For some reason this writer has never
been able to fathom, most drivers in these matters
trust implicitly to the guards. Writer, who spent
years in Maoriland railway service, has nevet
known a driver to read special crossing instruc-
tions; he merely wipes his hand on his pants,
signs the receipt for the instructions gingerly, and
then very carefully puts the unopened envelope
into the only clean pocket of his jacket—one
which he keeps specially for the purpose. Writer
has seen that pocket so full that some old, un-
opened instructions had to be taken out to make
room for the new ones. Then he goes oiling and
asks the guard, “What are we gawing ter do ? ”
This, however, is merely a side issue and has
nothing to do with the present case.) The train
reached Hindon, where it was supposed to cross a
train coming in the opposite direction. Here the
guard received a ’phone message that he was to
cross a train at Barewood. He thought it meant
that the original train was late and that he would
not cross it till he got to Barewood ; in reality it
referred to a second train which had to be met.
So ho pulled out of Hindon and met the first
train end on.
Maoriland, with its cheap and shoddy methods
and; its utter want of decent management and
decent appliances, is a place where it is much
healthier to walk. Coghlan publishes, in his
“Seven Colonies,” some statistics covering a
period of ten years which show that Maoriland
kills ten times as large a proportion of its passen-
gers as Victoria, Great Britain, Germany, Austria,
Belgium, Sweden, Norway, or Holland ; five
times the proportion shown by France or South
Australia ; over three times that of N.S.W., 2i
times that of Switzerland, nearly twice that of
Spain, and a larger proportion than any other
country on the list save Canada. Also, it maims
a larger proportion of its passengers than any of
these countries save Canada. In the latter direc-
tion M.L. puts up some noble records. It injures
42 times the proportion of its passengers that Nor-
way does, 21 times the proportion shown by
Sweden, and ten times the proportion of Germany,
Holland, or S.A. No person who has led an evil
life should travel in a Maoriland train—there is
no ; Bible in the carriage, and prayers are not
offered up before the train starts, as they should
be on a railway system with a record like that.

Melbourne:
I begin to have
doubts about the
efficacy of these
Missions simul-
taneous and other-
wise. Lately I en-
gaged a gentleman
in the bricklaying
industry to re-set
our oven. He ar-
rived, with a hand-
cart, about 10 a.m.
I took no particular
notice at first, but
finding he was
rushing the job
through at a tre-
mendous pace I
became interested.
Finally he informed
me that he was
hastening in order
to attend the mid-
day meeting at the
Town Hall—he said
he hadn’t missed
one since they
started and felt
they did him much
good. Iwasabashed
in the presence of
such holiness, and
highly esteemed
the honor he was
doing me in con-
senting to lay cer-
tain soulless bricks;
also I humbly gave
this walker in the
straight and nar-
row path a “ cup
of cold water ” in
the shape of a de-
cent lunch. Then
I settled up and he
departed for the
Mission, with his
hand-cart. It sub.
sequently tran-
spired he had re-
built the oven-set-
ting from a small
stack of bricks in
my own yard, and
had carted away
the remainder —in-
cluding a row
planted round the
youngster’s flower-
bed. A day or two
later I meandered
into the Town Hall
and observed my
friend lustily join-
ing in the chorus
of “ Beautiful Zion,
Built Above.”
Edging alongside,
I ventured to ob-
serve that I hoped Zion wouldn’t be built of stolen
bricks, but the virtuous bricklayer treated me as a per-
fect stranger—in fact, he even threatened to give me in
charge for insulting behaviour. As I said before, these
Missions may do good, but I have my doubts.
• • • «
Sydney City Council is throwing away a
splendid opportunity of giving that filthy city a
real cleaning. After actively carrying on the work
for a couple of months with a staff of 250 men
(1000 would have been better) a tired feeling came
over the Council, and it decided that most of
the men should be dismissed, and relapsed to the
old state of inaction. It thus loses the chance,
which won’t crop up again until next plague, of
using the mild panic of the public to enforce sani-
tary conditions. Meanwhile the pest is just about
as active as ever. This sudden stop in washing
operations recalls the old story of the shearer who,
after spending years wayback without a wash,
arrived in Melbourne for the Cup and went for a
Turkish bath. An attendant, after rubbing
him down for two hours, came upon some hard
substance, which turned out to be an overcoat that
the back-blocks gentleman said he remembered
missing some years before. The shampooing
operations of Sydney City Council have only got
as far as the old overcoat. Yet Sydney arises and
goes into the cooling room to put on its other
clothes.
• • • »
The dirty landlords of Sydney, together
with a few who are not dirty but only angry, have
banded together in a Defence Association to resist
the City Council’s demands for cleaning and
repairs. At the preliminary meeting, Mr. Rogal-
sky was in the chair, and Mr. Himrnelhoch sup-
ported* him cordially. Mr. Himrnelhoch, who is
something in the money-lending way, said he had
had bitter experiences lately, and possibly ’a good
many of his clients could say the same. Mr.
Rogalsky said wildly that the City Council was
“making an enormous sum of money out of the
people,” and Broughton, M.L.A., moaned that
the rates were “exceedingly heavy,” being, he
said, 2s. in the £. Broughton, M.L.A., had better
try London, where the rates are 6s. 6d. in the £,
and perhaps he will feel better. Also, under recent
English legislation, Mr. Himrnelhoch might have
some more bitter experiences.

suspected. But it is to be scoured asfar as possible
by the Health Commissioner. The L orth Brisbane
Council fought for months as to the size of the
garbage tins and whether the cleaning rate should
be or lfd. in the £.
In Sydney, also, the spectral visitant has
brightened up and been breaking out in new
places, but Sydney may lose its spectre when the
winter really sets in; up North it will
hang out comfortably through the cold season.
It has every facility to do so. Brisbane smells
the same all the year round. Its perfume rises
superior to times and seasons, and the river which,
loafs round it like an unwholesome brown cravat
rolls as muddily to the sea one month as another.
Twice a day the tide deposits its late_ lamented
dog on the mud flats, and twice a day it calls tor
the same. The bullock’s liver that is dropped
overboard at the meat-works drifts up to Bulimba
and looks in at the city’s back door when the tide
is rising, or drifts down. to Lytton if it is falling.
The greasy mud smells in the Bulimba reach, and
the Philp Government’s political job smells in
Queen-street, and the State’s finances smell in
London, and the odours of all these things are
marvellous.
Brisbane takes its plague philosophically.
It is a city that has lost heart. Years ago it was
young and riotous and full of broken-down
blue omnibuses and cabs whose drivers looked
like unhanged murderers. It had some energy
then; in those good old days the writer saw as
many as eight fights at Eagle Farm on race days,
observed a P.M. and a high police official both
cheating at cards in the city’s best hotel, and
threw a bottle at an Acting-Governor, said Act-
ing-Governor having damned him for not taking
his boat out of the way of a launch whereon were
the A.-G. and about a dozen politicians, half of
them drunk. And now it would be as riotous if it
could, but its credit has run out at the pub., and
it is a hot, desolate place with the frog of ages in
its throat and a half-formed impression that it
has missed the ’bus—that ’bus where the live
nations sit on the box-seat while the dead ones
miss the back step and drop off into the roadway.
• * * •
A health-officer visit of inspection to the
Chinese quarters at Alexandria (Sydney) disclosed
a loathsome state of affairs, which ought to in-
terest Judge Murray, who lately went out of his
way to give a certificate of first-class citizenship
to the Chow :
The want of light and air to the dwellings, the interiors
of which were all of a more or less filthy description, was
most marked. In the tenements occupied by hawkers,
baskets containing vegetables and fruit were to be seen
lying close to a lot of rubbish, and in close proximity to
old and dirty bedding on which the residents slept. In
answer to a question as to what was to be done with these
vegetables the answer came, “ Me sell ’em you countly-
man,” meaning that they would be taken out for sale
next day.

The proportion of persona attacked by
plague under the age of 21 has not yet been satis-
factorily explained. Nine cases were reported in
Sydney during the week ending May 12th, and
the ages of the patients were 8, 12, 13, 17, 18, 18,
20, 32, and 72 ; the last two were Chinese.
ENGAGEMENT. A LA MODE.
HE (tenderly): “ Been thinking — haw—of getting
married lately, are you? ”
SHE (dreamily): “How much?”
HE (tenderly): “ Fifty thou’ in city property and
a house at Toorak.”
SHE (softly): “Right.” HE: “Right.”
(No cards.)
Plague sufferers at Little Bay are not
debarred from receiving visits from their friends
and relations, but the nature of their illness is
kept from the patients as much as possible. One
day a visitor left a newspaper behind with one of
the sick men, who soon read his own name among
the list of plague patients. “Good God!” he
exclaimed “ we’ve got the Plague ! ” And,
much to the annoyance of the medical staff, the
whole ward suffered a relapse in consequence of
learning the character of its illness.

Dr. Ashburton Thompson’s health report
on Redfern did not come as a surprise to anyone
familiar with the history of that delectable Sydney
suburb (which has a population of 23,000 people,
living in 4500 houses). About 30 years ago Redfern
was a range of sand-hills ; rubbish was tipped in
the hollows of these hills. As soon as a piece was
made level enterprising landlords snapped it up,
on account of its being within easy reach of the
city, and built on it. Dr. Ashburton Thompson
reported on one Redfern area only, and by no
means the worst. Let him visit the haunt of the
Hindu and Syrian, known as Syria-town. In
that quarter the tenements are vilely insanitary,
and as many as 51 persons used to live in one
house.
Under the proud title, “ Adelaide
Will Be First,” S.A. Register (April 23)
describes with much detail a city im-
provement which is calculated to make
the eastern towns snort with envy.
Adelaide may not have pride of place in the fine
arts or the sciences, but according to the Register
it will soon be her haughty boast that she has—
“ the most handsome lavatory at any railway-
station in Australia.”
* * * •
A few leprosy statistics from piebald Q.,
where the curse of Naaman is coming along
steadily. Since 1892, when the lazarettes were
established at Stradbroke Island (Dunwich, in
Moreton Bay) and Friday Island (north of Cook-
town), 96 lepers have been incarcerated. Of these
41 were Kanakas, 20 Chinese, 21 Europeans, 9
aborigines, and 5 assorted black-and-tan. Of
these 56 died, and 10 Chinese were shipped home
at great expense. Of the 56, 27 were Kanakas, 12
Europeans, 9 Chinese, and 8 assorted. Between
June, 1808, and July, 1899, 22cases were reported
—22 in 12 months ! Of these 14 were Kanakas, 5
Europeans, 2 aborigines, and 1 Chinese—and 9 of
them came from Mackay, the Q. Sugaropolis. Con-
sidering the size of the top corner of this island,
and the difficulty of catching a leper whitehanded,
so to speak, it would seem that scores of contami-
nated blacks, brindles and browns would be found
if the authorities were keen on the scent.
Naturally the Q. policeman doesn’t rush around
after the leper—especially seeing that awhile back
a leper fighting for liberty savagely bit a con-
stable with terrible consequences to the unlucky
officer. With two exceptions all the later cases
came from the sugar districts where Tommy
Tanna, Ram Sam and Ah Sin are looked upon as
being cheap and reliable. They certainly are
reliable as regards murder, lust and leprosy.
The latest addition to Q. lepers is an unfor-
tunate who, a few years back, was a giant in Q.
Civil Service and a favored invitee at all the
exclusive dinners at Government House. The
steps downward were retrenchment, poverty,
book-canvassing, Dunwich asylum, and now Leper
Island.
» » » *
The proposal of Sydney Water and Sewer
age Board, that religious institutions, which are
now exempt, should pay their rates like anybody
deserves general support. More than that,
religious and charitable institutions should pay
all rates and taxes that other institutions pay, and
where they do sewing, washing, wood-chopping,
and the like for the public, they should be subject
to all the rules of the Factories Act as regards
hours of labour, ventilation, &c.—to everything
except the minimum-wage law, which it would be
impossible to enforce. Whatever aid the country
desires to give to religion or charity should be
given openly by a grant of money—not surrepti-
tiously by exemptions. The present system sub-
sidises, at the public expense, squalid little sects
and creeds that are as unpicturesque to look upon
as a corroboree, and shabby, private speculations
of the sweating and money-grubbing order like
the Salvation Army.
There is something ludicrous in the idea of the
Salvation Army being exempted from public
burdens as a charity when it ought rather to be
struck with an absentee-tax old man Booth,
whose private and exclusive money-grinding
machine it is, being a dweller in the other hemi-
sphere. The Governments which mistook Booth
for a charity evidently failed to notice that ancient
speculator’s nose, but it is not too late to repair
the oversight, and there are many similar noses in
the business which call for observation. Further,
the “charitable” lodging-house should not only
pay its rates and other public burdens, but it
should be at least made to keep itself as clean as a
merely secular beddery, and before it receives
State aid or countenance it should be ascertained
beyond a doubt that its benevolence doesn’t really
consist in supplying a dirtier bunk than usual for
a penny more. Charity covereth a multitr.de of
bugs, and when you see a text hung up on the
wall it is advisable to hit it a good hard knock.
If done properly the insect behind the text will be
killed every time.

“ Dugald ” : Crying out against the sweat-
ing of the youngster comes well from the peda-
gogue, who, to be successful under the Vic., S.A.
or W.A. Depts., must be the most remorseless of
sweaters. It isn’t the work (mainly physical) that
youngsters have to do at home that makes them
the pale, anaemic, spindle-shanked animals they
are. The successful (from Dept, point of view)
pedagogue in thisdistrict works theyoungsters from
6to hours per day at mental work, and if they
wereonly game they could tell the departmentsome
wholesome truths (concerning the system) that
might account for the awfulness of vital statistics
in years to come. The average kid can stand any
amount of light physical labor and benefit thereby,
but none is benefited by the infernal grind of 5
hours (minimum) mental activity in school,
coupled with from one to two hours home lessons
to be pored over after tea. The pedagogues
should kick their own dead horse.

“ Wully ” Affleck, M.L.A., turned a
deadly argument on the Presbyterian Assembly
parsons who, the other day, badgered about
Government allowances to chaplains in N.S.W.
insane asylums. “I dunna,” said Wully, “see
vera weel whit ye want preaching to the insane
fur at all.”

Bananaland Government is doing penance
for past sins of patronage by sacking police magis-
trates, stock inspectors and supernumeraries
wholesale. Among other notables Clement
Wragge is being retrenched—also the Stiger
Vortex. Henceforward Sirius will cavort un-
bridled with the festive Isobar, and the “ Supreme
Meteorologist ” will run His universe for a brief
space unassisted.
Re prayers for rain. The accountant in a
Wagga bank was chaffing a Celestial about the
recent innovation of Joss, and expressed surprise
at the prompt rain which followed the Sydney
Celestial ceremonies. “Oh, yeh,” said John,
beaming blandly, “Sydney Joss all li’; eountlee
Joss not so goo’. Clistian Joss no goo’at all.; .
Look here,” excitedly; “ ’sposem Clistians bet.
hundled clid (hundred quid) we put em up our
stuff all lite.” The local heathen are very keen
on arranging a match.

Barrister Herbert Harris takes another
turn at acting District Court Judge. When Judge
Forbes retired Mr. Harris was first in the running
for a settled seat, but political competition was
then brisk. Political and social competition is,
unfortunately, the curse of N.S. W. minor Bench,
and only that cliques cut one another’s throats
from time to time it would be a worse Bench than
it is.

ON HOSPITAL SATURDAY.
This is the German gentleman who called on pa.
A Vic. warrior specially famous for his
command of langwidge, has told his Kharki
minions what he said to one of the discontented
Fifth Contingenters. The‘rebel,’ it seems, had
the audacity to expect that the warrior would
approve of his views re certain British officahs,
but the gallant Colonel undeceived him on this
point by roaring. “If I had been there you
should have dug your own grave, and then I
would have shot you like the cur you are ! ”
Whereat the Kharki audience' cheered vehe-
mently. The notion of a man preparing his
own place of burial previous to being polished off
for insubordination seemed to appeal to the
noblest instincts of their military natures.
* * • *
In Bendigo (Vic.) Hospital recently, a
man was operated on for an unexplainable com-
plaint. A small tumbler was extracted from his
body; he had got drunk over-night and next
morning was found lying helpless in a paddock.
a- * * - *
Nearly three months ago, a bear horribly
mauled a septuagenarian attendant in its cage at
the Adelaide Zoo. The man, who was given up
for dead (the bear was shot on top of him), has
just limped out of hospital. The most unpleasant
sensation he feels now is the icy coldness of the
arm and hand which the brute chewed off. But
the doctors say that the uncanny feeling will last
only two or three months more.
BEREAVEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Across the cliff the other night I sped,
With stealthy step I crept,
The waves wept wild their requiem for the dead,
Dear God—l have not wept!
Along the path your foot so often trod—
At last I reached the gate,
Then shudder’d back, for you beneath the sod—
What cared you were I late ?
A little breeze came whisp’ring from the sea,
Aloud I called your name—
Then breathless waited,—listened tremblingly—
But nothing stirred or came.
Only below, I heard a sea-bird call,
And heard an echo moan ;
The troubled sky her painful tears let fall,
... 1 stood alone . . . Alone!
Across the cliff I homeward turned and sped,
With stealthy step I crept,
The waves wept wild their requiem for the dead—
Dear God—l have not wept t
QUITS.
VALE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The day like a rose
In the garden skies
To an ending grows,
Blooms red, and dies ;
And colder than stars, and as icy white,
Gleameth your sainted face this night.
The last bread broken,
The last song sung.
The last word spoken,
The last bell rung,
And away on a misty, measureless Sea
Goeth the last who was dear to me.
P. E. QUINN.
