A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, May 13, 1902.
Mt Dear Arini,—
Summer and Winter are holding a midway
truce. I should not be surprised if a crop of
spring poetry were raised by what some one has
called this “ golden-tissued weather.” When
Nature herself falls in young poets might well be
deceived. There is a nice crop of strawberries in
the shops, and added to the street cries of “ fish-
ho ! ” and “ wile rabbi! ”is that of “ strobberies ”
at 2s. per pound. “ Queensland ! ” you’ll say.
Not so—local gardens and no glass-houses either.
First wild flowers of the season in the streets
last Saturday ; but the big dominant chrysanthe-
mum still fills the baskets. Our ’mum-snow was
a small affair held in a small hall to a small
attendance. Splendid blooms, mind you, but
from the Town Hall, with a Governor opening the
display, to the Queen’s Hall with a group of
cynical-looking nurserymen eyeing the other
fellows’ blooms, and a group of evening-paper
reporters eyeing the catalogues, is a far cooee.
Tne growers’ subsidy has dwindled, the seasons
have been bad for the “golden hairs,” and we
have no Governor. And if we had perhaps he
would have the same dislike to opening a chrys.
show as had Gov. Brassey. “I don’t like it at
all,” he remarked to a sympathetic woman, “for
my Treasury of Poetical Quotations hasn’t a single
verse of powtry on that flowah. Now, with a
daffodil show, I’m all right. ‘ Daffodils that come
before the swallows dare,’ you know ; the rest is
easy, you know, and soon I’m walking at the
head of a queue of narcissus committeemen.”
The ingenuousness of the old Sunbeamer to admit
his possession of a book of poetical quotes !
At Rosehill races last Saturday there was a
tremendous display of tweed costumes—on men.
At these meetings the chosen vessel always pre-
dominates over the sex that St. Paul slighted
and ordered to keep its hat on in church,
in a proportion of 50 to one. None of the women
whose names are for ever in the fashion-columns
of the dailies was present, but that’s not saying
there were no well-dressed, well-looking women
there. There were numbers of both, also plenty
of women who looked like over-worked laundresses
or cooks in large, cheap boarding-houses. They
came in their working clothes, poor things, and
they came on business—punting on every race.
Since the stopping of letters to “ Tattersall’s ”
sweeps one sees more of these haggard, cheerful
women in the grandstand enclosures at the race-
meetings. Some of them are jockeys’ mothers ;
nearly all have some connection, no matter how
remote, with a racing-stable, and the five shillings
that used to go to Hobart is gambled direct on the
horse. They take their losses quite cheerfully—
the mangle is good and work plentiful; and when
their winner is coming home they stamp their
utilitarian feet and exclaim: “ That’s him!
Good boy ! —now you’ll see how I’ll look on
Sundays! ” By the way, when the winner
of the final race on Saturday was assured of
his place, a group of fashion-plate women on the
grandstand let loose most unseemly squeals of joy.
They danced and jumped out of their frocks, and
one caught the hand of a man near her, shook it
violently, and with the other thumped the ground
violently with my umbrella —to which she had
not been introduced. - )
Concerning the frocks: There was a gray one,
a fog-gray in a material like a glorified horse-
blanket. It was a frieze or a zebiline (the monks
of old might have worn such as a penance gar-
ment) with a most perfectly-cut train, which kept
a complete semi-circle on the grass instead of
bunching into a draggle-tail. Some trains, when
let go, look about as effective as if they swathed
hot bottles. The blouse was slightly Russianised
and the hat was a white felt with white and green
leaves and a roll of old-rose velvet. But now I
come to think, it was the girl under the hat and
inside the frock that gave both distinction.
Of course something was due to the dress-
maker, but this girl was tall, dark and
graceful. Her chin was held well up and
she had a proud, innocent face like the
girls Howard Pyle draws in Harper’s Mag.
Other noticeable gowns were a white panne
trimmed round the foot with ermine and guipure.
Sounds royal, but it didn’t look it. Some white
linen costumes were out, and a semi-demi evening
gown with chiffon sleeves, which were not sleeves
at all in the sense of covering. It is the exception
now to see hats worn tilted over the face. Women
have discovered that their eyes won’t stand the
effect. If you have large, candid eyes, or ox-eyes,
you are, perhaps, safe enough, but if there is any-
thing furtive or foxy or watchful or roving about
your eyes, and you wear your millinery roof sloped
forward, just notice the staring you’ll receive from
the bold, but harmless, bounder. There is more
of a moral effect in the way of wearing your hat
than the manner of carrying the rest of your out-
fit.
Forty girls who have no need to take thought
for the morrow, at least in a financial sense, con-
stituted themselves syndicate hostesses of a dance
at the Paddington Town-hall last week. Forty
girls, all young, mostly good-looking (no young,
well-cared-for, educated, healthy, well-groomed
girl can be very plain), beautifully dressed, princi-
pally in pink or white, and carrying posies of
roses—what a sight for one bashful young man !
One such of my acquaintance was there, and at
the sight of so much beauty he had a rush of
modesty to the head and heartily wished that the
old order of the solo hostess, with her frosted hair,
matronly form, expansive smile and big bouquet,
was at the door. Then, with chattering teeth and
perspiring palms, he ventured in behind a brace
of hostesses’ fathers. But presently he longed to
be 40 men or even 20, so as to be able to dance
with his collective hostess. As it was, he blun-
dered around the walls asking her mother to dance.
The mothers were there, but not in the sense of
chaperones. The Countess of Something in an
English mag., the other day, wrote: “Chaperonage
is rapidly disappearing. In another few years it
will be a thing.of the past. Girls in their first and
second season invite men to tea at fashionable city
tea-rooms, and any attempt on the man’s part to
pay for the refreshment insures his never being
asked again. These tea-rooms are beautiful in a
decorative sense, but their tea is the most awful
wash at 2s. 6d. a cup.”
An interesting engagement is that of Dr.
Ashburton Thompson, President of N.S.W.
Board of Health, to Mrs. Lilian Simpson [nie
Salomons, daughter of the Q.C., and widow of
the late G. C. Simpson, only son of Mr. Justice
Simpson). Since her widowhood Mrs. Simpson
and her three children have lived with her mother
at Woollahra. The wedding will not take place
till after the return of the bride’s father from
London. It’s a triumph to all women when one
compels the surrender of a gallant bachelor after
such prolonged enjoyment of single-blessedness.
Miss Josephine Deakin (Mrs. J. E. Scantle-
bury) gave a concert at the Stein way Hall, in
London, on April 8, to crowded audience, and
some press notices to hand are very flattering.
Amongst Miss Deakin’s contributions were—
“ When the Swallows Homeward Fly” (given
with mezzo voce effect), the “Jewel Song,” and
Allitsen’s “ Since We Parted.”
One who has returned tells a tale of a lady stay-
ing at the same hotel where the fraud Princess
Radziwill stopped. For every fraud generous
Heaven sends a fool to keep the balance even.
This lady was young and attractive, and “she
was waiting for her husband, who was to arrive
from England.” She had waited three months,
when one day she went out and returned with a
gentleman, whom she introduced as her husband,
and he registered as such. The gentleman’s
business objective was towards Kitchener’s direc-
tion, and after two days stay at the hotel he called
for .his bill. It was presented, embodied with it
“ his wife’s ” bill for the three months—£2oo. He
swore a protest, and refused to pay the lady’s
account. “What! not pay your wife’s bill?”
said the shocked landlord. The fool went to the
fraud and remonstrated: “I have only known
you two days, etc.,”and much more. “ I am very
sorry,” cooed the fraud, “but the bill must be
paid,, and it will place me in a most unpleasant
position if you do not pay it. Remember now you
registered ! ” He remembered.
A London-announced engagement that will
interest the run-holding section of Maoriland
society is that of Miss Florence Watt, daughter
of the late James Watt, of Napier, M.L., to
Frank Baden-Powell, a barrister, and brother of
the Man of Mafeking. The Watts sheep whiten
many a hill.
Australian artists are really writing their names
in the London fog, which is quite as difficult to do
as marking them in water. Longstaff (in addition
to his King’s Commission) is represented at Royal
Academy Exhibition, opened the other day, by a
portrait of Alice, daughter of Hector Macdonald,
of Melbourne, who is one of the directors of the
North Mount Lyell. The portrait shows a fair-
headed girl of about fourteen, dressed in Lincoln
Sreen. Percy Spence and Rupert Bunny also
ave pictures in the same exhibition.
The debutantes at the Adelaide Mayor’s big
ball mourn over the frigidity of Lady Hopetoun’s
hand-shake, but are in raptures concerning the
merry way in which Lord Hopetoun himself
skipped through the opening set of lancers with
them. “He might have been my brotner,” re-
marked one enthusiastic damsel.
Reported that Mr. J. C. Williamson will pro-
bably make Melbourne his head-quarters. Mrs.
M'Quade’s decision not to re-build Her Majesty’s
Theatre makes J.C.W.’s Melbourne interests
heavier than his Sydney ones. If he does go his
family will accompany him, and his town house,
“ Ithaca,” will be put on the market.
Amy Castles,, who has been drawing record
houses in Maoriland, will be back in Sydney on
22nd. She takes a day’s rest and sings on 24th
and 26th. Miss Ada Crossley will engage Dolly
Castles, Amy’s sister, for her forthcoming Aus-
tralasian tour.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The approaching Divorce boom in Melbourne
is looked forward to with unusual eagerness by
students of domestic melodrama. There has
never before been such a large and varied
assortment of shows billed for one season ; indeed
if the Law authorities had any managerial enter-
prise they would shift the Divorce Court to the
Exhibition-building, provide an efficient orchestra,
and charge for admission. A large audience of
sporting, commercial, and Chosen people is assured
for one entertainment, in which a pretty sister of
a. well-known citizen will sue for final separa-
tion from her husband on the double plea of
habitual drunkenness, and “ leaving her without
visible means of support.” The lady’s family
connections are said to be somewhat averse to her
action, but she means business. She has one
particular grievance that should appeal to the
hearts of all maidens about to marry. Among the
vanished means of support heretofore mentioned'
are £7OO worth of wedding presents, and, although
those silver cruet stands would now be souvenirs-
of a sorrowful event, the fair petitioner mourns
the loss of them, all the same.

kind of personage. She pens familiar little notes-
to ‘Dear Mrs. Jones’ and ‘Dear Mrs. Brown, 5 '
asking the favor of their company to tea on such a
date, and any members of the family who may be-

and go and take tea at Stonington with much
gusto. The Guv’nor brims over with the same-
spirit of brotherly love, while the smilesome Mi«»<?
Clarke raises the ire of the Naicest by being civil
to the commonest of those horrid official people.
The smart set are so porcupiney towards the dowdy

“The German Commander, closely clasping his
partner, meandered about the ‘ Rippon Lea ’ gar-
dens that Monday of the German and Jappy
shivoo, with not a care or thought beyond the
lady by his side. Easily singled out in the
gathering was Mrs. Sep. Miller, who wore,
with a cobweb woven silk lace and glace
costume, a green-ribboned hat, Venetian red
vest points, and a string of marvellous coral.
Each round of coral was as large as a
pigeon’s egg. This is the latest supplement to a
regular cave of fine gems. Mrs. Miller is a worry
to her admiring circle. She is dainty, graceful,
petite, but she persists in drooping her head
shyly, and sidling along anyhow. She lacks an
‘ air.’ Other women assume it, who have neither
figure nor any kind of attractiveness. Ladv
Hutton, who bothers about all the Naicest, while
her frowning hubbie lurks disconsolate in the
background, was at this function. Her gown was
black, and faultless in style and fit. Aide Browning
is learning the A B C of Diplomacy and Discipline.
At this affair, he left the giddy flutterers of the
smart set and went off for a tece-a-tete with Lady
O’Loghlen—that proud matron who despises the
mushroom rich with a nostril-curling disdain.
There is nothing more invulnerable in Smell-
bourne than Lady O’Loghlen’s haughtiness.
“The Sargoods made a brave show at the
wedding of their daughter, Winifred, with Dr.
Rupert Hornabrook, on Saturday. The bride is
the daughter of the first Lady Sargood, but step-
mamma did things as nobly (she always has done
so) for her as she would do for her own daughter,
Charlotte —a pretty, gazelle-eyed charmer. Dr.
Hornabrook looks a young, pale-faced, fair-haired,
naice young man, of short stature and martial re-
putation. In India and Africa he made a record for
neat work with the lancet. Now he plies that in-
strument in Holy Adelaide. St. George’s Church,
St. Kilda, never was so decorated for any previous
knot-tying. Ribbons by the hundred, flowers by the
gross, and palms by the score, helped to deck out
the edifice to a highly festive degree. The bride
had for a wedding-gown whatever richness and
style accrue from the heaviest of ivory satin,
point lace, chiffon frilling, and tucking. The
veil and coronet counted to the credit side of smart-
ness. The four attendant maids —Miss Charlotte
Sargood the most fetching—were tricked forth in
sweet little frocks of cream with a dressing of
blue; pancake toques in cream and blue follow-
ing suit. A bid had been made for simplicity.
Assisting the parson was Dr. Be van, his
white curls making a round moon of his head,
and his voice rolling round the corners of the
church in slow, rich, theatrical tones. A little
black skull-cap covered his crown. The guests
were Mr. Justice Hodges (who afterwards made
the most memorable wedding speech of the
season) and sundry Federal and State members,
smart sassiety, and the solid class of rich mer-
chants and other magnates of commerce.
“Merchants are moving off their daughters
like winter blankets. No class likes making such
a splash as these Knights of the Garter and
monarchs of self-raising flour. The other week it
was merchant Harper’s daughter. Last Wednes-
day it was merchant George Bruce’s fourth
cherub; on Saturday, merchant Sargood’s third
daughter. In a few weeks or so hence it will be
merchant Robert Reid’s daughter, to say nothing
of his son. It is a peculiar weakness of the
‘Trade’to make a kind of front-window display
of house decorations and chiffons over the hy-
menal sacrifice.
“ Miss Irene Bruce, whose knot-tying was the
flutter of the week, was wedded to a very youthful-
looking son of Aid. Davey (who figured as a
widower-bridegroom himself not so long ago). The
ceremony came off with a full flourish of trum-
pets at Christ Church, St. Kilda, and the con-
tinuation shivoo at ‘ Brooklawn,’ St. Kilda. Miss
Ida Bruce and Miss Winnie Davey were helps to
the bride. Miss Davey,—an extra-chic charmer —
is engaged to Lieut. G. Bruce, back unbruised
from the battlefield.
“ Colonel Tom Price had been very crusty about
that engagement of his, and whenever the an-
nouncement of it appeared a contradiction was
elicited. His friends never alluded to it, and
although some pleasant innuendo was made on the
subject at his own daughter’s wedding months
ago, the gay widower ignored it. And one woman
kept the secret, and in spite of dress-builders,
hat-riggers, and caterers knowing the facts, not a
rumor of the date of the wedding leaked out before
the eventful day. On last Thursday week the
fact was first made public, when the marriage of
the Colonel to Miss Emmeline Shadforth Reid,.
eldest daughter of the late Mr. Robert Dyce Reid,
had already been accomplished at the St. Alban’s
Church, Armadale. The honeymoon, spent at
Ballarat, was brief. Military duties called the
Colonel back promptly to the barracks, where
the bridal trip was never even whispered of.
“ Melba’s good-nature is no fiction. Even the
Armstrongs, who boast the doughtiest crest of any
of the alleged Family Tree slips in this part of the
globe, own to it. Some of the she-Armstrongs
wear massive gold rings with ‘ Melba ’ raised in
diamond chips. The diva’s former hubbie and
son are in Texas —a most united pair. The Arm-
strongs here aver that Melba thought her long-
ago spouse much nearer to the title than he was—
being four removed. Certain secrets have been
leaking out of late. In the first place, Melba is
no stunning frocker —her appearance, despite rich
gowning-, is commonplace. A Collins-st. medico,
just back from ’Ome, declares that he couldn’t
listen to the diva’s marvellous voice for marvelling
at her plain presence. Others endorse that view.
Two residences—one ‘Doonside,’ Richmond—are
being prepared by the Mitchell-Patterson clan.
Then ‘ Nellie ’ is so capricious that neither might
be selected—and Menzies’ may house her. Her
fads are legion.
“At the Princess’, Thornton’s play, ‘ Facing
the Music,’ doesn’t run to rapturous frocking.
The daintiest clothes-peg on hand is Leon-'e Nor-
bury, as Curate Smith’s wife, a little, golden-
haired, blue-eyed, neat-nosed blunderer, who
starts all the trouble by arriving in a midnight
fog at worldling Smith’s, and sleeping there
instead of in her own clerical hubby’s tenement,
higher up in the same mansions. Throughout’
Miss Norbury wears a becoming heliotrope gown
with cunning corsage-posy of violets. Out
of doors, she favors grey - feathered hat,
with bow and gold-topped umbrella, and,
when masquerading as a parlor-maid to spy
on Mrs. Secular Smith, sne gets into spry
muslin cap and pinny, and hoists her skirts
extra high to show patent-leather shoes and silk
hose. The other ‘ Mrs. John Smith ’ is repre-
sented by Yera Fordyce, as a tall, dark, rather
dashing young woman, with irregular features,
and long, loose fawn coat, worn over red travelling
suit.

“Over 40 debutantes radiated under the4ooyds..
of foliage, and the 4000 electric lights at Mayor-
Cohen’s hall, at Adelaide, last Thursday night..
Forty _ more girls to swell the already great-
majority ! They say there will be no newcomers;
of the Lily of the Yalley series to Melbourne-
social ranks this year. The old girls—the ‘ No-
Surrenderors ’ of the Past—are putting their
younger friends off. They have been Lancering-
and hopping around with Eton youngsters and
gouty old foghorns for the past season or two, and!
now warn off the young and untried from a simi-
lar pitfall. Private theatricals will use up part.
of the smart contingent of girls. Skating pro-
mises an allurement for others. But dances are-
dreaded like crows’ feet, and chilblains, and other
inevitable irritants of the flesh. Melbourne was.
never more manless.

Ex-Gov Beauchamp is presenting a por-
trait of King Edward to N.S.W. National
Gallery, and has commissioned John Longstaff to
paint it; the King has promised to sit after the
Coronation. Lord Beauchamp keenly appreciated
Longstaff’s work—and so, for the matter of that,
did the British Academy’s late President, Sir
Frederick Leighton, a great artist and a discern-
ing personage quite unaddicted to gush.
Talk about provincialism. Thus a Sydney
paper:—
That, with all due deference to Mr. Longstaff’s ability,
a New South Wales artist now in London, painter or
sculptor, could have been obtained by Earl Beauchamp
for his gift to the (N.S.W. National) Art Gallery.
Now, the Earl decided to give a portrait of the
King to the Gallery, and you can’t quarrel with
the man who pays the piper calling the tune; you
must not look a gift horse in the mouth. And
what “N.S.W. artist now in London” (or else-
where) could paint a portrait to compare with
Longstaff’s work?

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A legend that Steve Byrne and Dan Kelly
are still in the flesh, haunts the Kelly country.
Mark Twain on Cecil Rhodes, deceased :
“A great man. After his death 1 should like to
have a piece of the rope as a souvenir.”
“ Mr. Seddon is a great man. The only
doubt is whether he is not rather too great for a
small concern like the British Empire.”— The
New Age.
Judge Dowling, aged 83, and his sister,
wife of Arthur Hodgson, formerly of Queensland,
aged 81, died within a few hours of each other,
cmein Sydney and the other in London.
N.S.W. pension-tree has been dropping its
branches rapidly of late. The deaths of ex-
Judges Forbes, M'Farland, and Dowling repre-
sents a taxpayers’ easement of £2250 a year.
Joseph Ward (M.L.) seems unable to get
over the fact that he owns a Court dress. _ Stated
that he even turned up in it at the privately-
organised banquet given in Wellington to_ farewell
Seddon. J.G.’s knighthood is going to his head—
and his calves.
S.A. Assembly has been robbed of its two
worst bores. Caldwell (Tory whip) a veteran
politician, pietist, poet, and long-winded Wednes-
day afternoon talker, and Carpenter, the only
pious member of the Labor party, were both
“ left ” at recent elections.
Barton tells a friend : ** During the past
36 years I have not averaged five hours a day in
bed.” But the Prime Minister is one of those
people who don’t need to go to bed to sleep. He
can sleep whenever and wherever he wants to, and
sometimes when he doesn’t want to.
Among the people who are moving the
local heaven and earth to get the office of taxing-
officer for bills of costs (a high-salaried office
created under a recent Vic. Act) filled by the
appointment of their worthy selves, is one of the
foremost committeemen and agitators of the
drastically retrenching Citizens’ Reform League.
DUNCAN GILLIES.
“ Would that your knocking might waken Duncan.” — MAC[?]ETH.
Alfred Deakin has attained the Acting-
Premiership of the Commonwealth in his 46th
year. He is the youngest member of Cabinet;
25 years at the Bar, and 23 in active politics. He
became Minister of the Crown at 27. His press
work has been contributed mainly to Melbourne
Age and Leader and N.S.W. Daily Telegraph.
Not generally known that Harry Morant
was rejected by a certain N.S.W. amateur military
potentate for the “ Citizens’Bushmen ” because
he couldn’t ride; when the potentate was told
that the rejected one was the famous “ Breaker ”
he graciously reconsidered his decision, and
applicant was condescendingly informed to that
effect. Morant’s reply was brief and character-
istic.
Died last week, Albert Lee Tucker, who
was an alderman for nine years, and a member of
Vic. Parliament for 24 years, during 21 of which
he represented Eitzroy. He was for three years
(1883-1886) a humble and hard-working member of
a Ministry run by an almost forgotten wine-
merchant named James Service, with Gillies,
Deakin, Berry, and Sargood among his colleagues.
A peaceful, ordinary man, whose name suggested
Food.
The most defeated man in Australia,
easily beating Sydney’s J. G. Griffin, is J. Z.
Sellar, of Adelaide, who for 45 years has been
fighting seats, with two municipal wins so far to
his credit. Sellar was a Chartist who made
money at “ the diggings ” and has been spending
it cautiously ever since in building political plat-
forms 20 years in front of the crowd. This Par-
liamentary election, carrying the Labor vote, he
went down for the city division.
Lady Hodgson, whose death is reported from
England, was a daughter of the late Chief-Justice
Dowling, and was born in Woolloomooloo, Sydney.
Arthur Hodgson, an intimate acquaintance of the
Dowlings, led the van in the pastoral settlement of
Queensland. As soon as he had got up a weather-
board humpy on the Darling Downs he voyaged
to Sydney and married Miss Dowling. She was
as fond of adventure as he. The Queensland run
quickly brought them wealth. Lady Hodgson
was for some time the only white woman in the
squatting line in Queensland.
David James, for some years past South
Australia’s most successful racehorse owner, has
gone into politics—he ran into second place for
Wooroora. A teamster and station-contractor, he
had the dazzling luck to be working on a job near
Broken Hill when Rasp, the boundary-rider, put
in the pegs of his “tin-mine” (anyhow, Rasp
thought it was tin); and afterwards he had either
the good luck or the good sense to hang on to the
interest that he reluctantly took. One way and
another a lot of Broken Hill money has gone in
horses—through St. Albans Wilson (Broken
Hill’s early manager), Kenneth Brodribb, Philip
Charley, James, and others.
World without end, Amen.
Probably there will be a thanking of
God for “Captain Cook, who dis-
covered Australia.” And so the last
Kurnell function is of no more note
than an ordinary church-service. But
how strange that Cook’s mate, Cap-
tain Clarke, never gets a word in
edgeways! Clarke commanded the
second ship of the expedition, and
when Cook was killed for coming back
with a sick ship and using up the
peope’s gods for firewood, Clarke
took charge, and went away to com-
plete the real work of the voyage if
possible. Cold and hardship slew him
■Up north of Saghalien, and no man
wrote his epitaph. His real life would
have made better reading than
Cook’s, for Clarke was not a moral
man. Two bi ts about him are amongst
the old papers still, characteristic and
quaint. Cook wondered why he was
late joining. He explained : “ I can’t
speak to the minute. The infernal
Israelites hot foot in my wake, I must
shake them off somehow and then
decamp without tuck of drum.”
Whereat, doubtless, Cook wondered;
but not so much as did austerely
proper Sir Joseph Banks, when down
on the corner of a long letter he found
the following: “Excse ppr Sr Jsp.
It was gilt-edged, I assure you ;
but the damned cockroaches ! ”
The rest is unquotable. A hard case
this Clarke, but seemingly good grit
ana staunch right through. Pity
there’s so little of him left.
The booming by Melbourne
press of the Vic. “ Reform agitation ”
has resulted in the simple folk of that
obscure hamlet, Kyabram, getting a
big advt. The agitation started with
a resolution passed at the village in
Nov. last, and this has been magnified
into the “Kyabram platform,” and
the humble local folk into the “Kya-
bram pioneers.” The only person-
ality among these “pioneers” is one
B. G. Goddard, whose boast is that
a letter to “B. G., Northern Vic-
toria,” would find him. A quaint
habit of his is to ignore, in conversa-
tion, the first personal pronoun. His
end of a colloquy would go thusly :
“B. G. came down this morning.”
“ Yes, B. G. finds it pretty warm.”
“Eh ? B. G. isn’t sure.” “ Oh, yes.
Jones told B. G. so.” “No, thanks;
B. G. doesn’t drink.” Already, a
grandfather B. G. has lately ventured
on the sea of matrimony again. He is Kyabram’s
storekeeper.

combative president of the Melbourne Peace and
Humanity Society, quotes his own case as evidence
that the early Christian professions of the Sunday-
school boy are not worth much. He was one of
two “ lost sheep ” in a class of 14 Gospel sucklings.
Some of the piously-disposed twelve have gone to
hell, says the Doctor, whose word is law as regards
the whereabouts of departed souls. One has

Tas. Mercury Davies, C.M.G., etc.,
bustled very hard for a Coronation trip to London
as Commandant of the Bisley rifle team. When
the Labor Party refused to let Mr. Barton vote
£2000 to send out the team, a less exceptionally
ordinary man than Davies, C.M.G., would have
given up the idea in despair. But Mr. Davies
rose to the occasion, squatted on every door-mat
in the Commonwealth, and by sheer energy and
bustle has got his trip and his team and his funds.
A well-advertised Southern leader-writer
is said to be negotiating with the pro-
moters of the mooted new Sydney paper
for the editorship, and should get it, too, if
the M'Eacharn influence counts for anything and
if the paper ever comes to light. Of late the jour-
nal which he is credited with the desire to leave
has seen nearly the whole of its leading men
drift away in search of new pastures, and the
trouble seems to be that there are very few front-
rank pressmen anxious to take their places.
Mr. W. Darcy Uhr, to The Bulletin,
from Coolgardie : —
Credit to whom credit is due ! I am told that Mr.
Carnegie lately stated to you that Mr. Latour was the
first man who crossed from Queensland to Port Darwin.
I wish to state that in March, 1872, 1 left Burketown,
Gulf of Carpentaria, with 105 head of fat cattle and 133
horses ; Mr. Rowland Edkins (now of Mt. Cornish), a very
old and respected magistrate of Q., saw me across the
Nicholson River, which is six miles west of Burketown.
My party consisted of six men and myself. I made a
most successful trip, losing only one horse and no cattle ;
and I was the first, by very many months, to cross the
continent. Mr. Todd, the present S.A. Postmaster, can
certify to this. If Carnegie, or anyone else, can prove
that any man crossed before me I will give £50 to any
charitable institution you may name.
As to the early life of Harry Morant (“ The
Breaker ”), some interesting circumstances (which
seem to have all the marks of truth) have been un-
earthed by Charters Towers Miner. According
to Mr. David Green, that paper, Morant arrived
in N.Q. in 1884, and in April of that year was mar-
ried at Charters Towers (though not exactly in the
name of Morant). He gave his age up there at 21,
and the place of birth not as Devon. The
Bulletin forwarded to Mr. Green a copy of
Morant’s signature, and, in return, received a
tracing of the signature in the marriage register.
There can be no reasonable doubt that the hand-
writing in each case is the same. Shortly after
this marriage Morant went to Hughenden, then to
Esmeralda Station, then to Winton and finally to
Cloncurry, doing odd jobs the while as a news-
paper writer, drover, etc. All the while he
spoke of rich relations in England ; from
Hughenden once he cabled to a titled per-
son for a large sum —he didn’t get the
money. But the fact that he spent a good
deal on the cable would seem to indicate that he
had some hope of “raising the wind” in that
quarter. Whilst in Charters Towers, it is alleged,
“The Breaker ” purchased two horses, giving in
payment a cheque signed “Morant” which was
dishonored afterwards. This is said to be the first
occasion on which he used the name “ Morant.”

Three years ago Miss Amy
Castles was so pleasingly unsophis-
ticated that she was unable to
appreciate the significance of lavish
newspaper criticisms. _ After one
of her early concerts in Melb., a
well-known musical critic ■wrote a
most glowing eulogium. The powers
that were behind Miss Amy worked
themselves into a perfect fever of
delight. They instructed the young singer that
she was to be introduced to the great critic that
evening, and she must thank him most cordially
for his splendid notice. The introduction took
place, and little Amy did not forget her orders.
“I must thank you, Mr. Thunderer,” she said,
“ for what you have done about my advertise-
ment.” “What was that?” asked the critic> in
amazement. “.I don’t know,’’_was the candid
reply ; “ but Father Robinson said I was to thank
you for it.”
The late “Johnny” Wallace, the Vic-
torian mining enterpriser, who assisted his private
investments by speculating with his children s
“Monaghan estate” moneys, what time
sole trustee of the property, seems to have “ cut
up” fairly well, though not nearly the millionaire
he was supposed to be. The old man left £6350
realty and £115,000 personality. His widow and
children get large annuities, and the residue, if
any, goes to the charities of the country districts
where he loomed largest when in the flesh.
There is not in Melbourne a more correct
man than Waxellency Madden. He practically
never makes mistakes —except when pronouncing
legal decisions. But, the other evening, at the in-
augural meeting of the University extension move-
ment, he stated, not once, but several times, that
the extension lecturers received £2 6s. Bd. for each
lecture, as they gave six for .£2O. The reporters
scribbled away, and, so staunch was their faith in
their Chief Justice, that the calculation appeared
that way the following day in both dailies.
The United Typewriter and Supplies Cos.,
one of the largest concerns manufacturing type-
writers, seems to be getting more of its share of
the world’s business. One of its machines, the
“Yost,” was favoured recently by the Indian
Government with an order for one hundred and
twenty machines. In addition to this their
Australian representative now reports the sale of
five hundred “ New Century ” Typewriters for use
on the Government Railways. An' important
fact is that these two are record orders for the
world, and, what is more important in the eyes of
the Company, were obtained in competition with
all the leading makes. Australian buyers can
obtain both of these machines from the Company 1 *
office in Sydney, Melbourne and Perth.*
The Song of the Bushveldt Carbineer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, chant me a lay of the days of old;
Of the days when men were brave—
When they loved the strife that brought no gold.
No fief, but a hero’s grave;
When a woman’s love was a noble prize
But not for a woman’s sake ;
When a kiss, and a glance of a leman’s eyes,
Were on show for a Man to take.
I would ask no star of a woman’s love,
No star of a man’s regard ;
I would ask no aid from a Hand above,
Nor for mercy always barred.
The puling snivel of weak-kneed fools
Is a smirch on our ancient race !
By Man ! If this be the fruit of the schools.
Give me straight what they call Disgrace.
For ’tis good to die as a strong man dies,
E’en though by the hangman’s rop<3;
For nothing on earth so currishly cries
As the cry of a poltroon hope.
I will do as I will—as a strong man should :
I will reck when the reckoning comes ;
Then march me to doom in the haltered wood.
Or brave the Rogue’s March of the drums.
F. J. BROOMFIELD.

J. URE SMITH (Late of Menzies’), Manager,

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“Drannel,” from the drought-stricken
N.S.W. West :-
In many paddocks on this run (Oanonbar) new tanks
have been lately put down, and, when rain threatens,
sheep “ line up ” along the drains and round the batters
in anticipation. Picked positions, such as junctions of
drains, or where main drain enters silt-tanlc, are fought
for like remnant counters at a draper’s bargain-sale.
When clouds disperse (no rain, alas !), woollies do evens
back to the old tanks, and drown their sorrows (and
themselves oftcimes) in the drop of liquid mud remaining
therein.

A Queensland back-country traveller re-
cording his experiences to Brisbane Coubieb :
At the homestead I found the youngest child (little
more than a baby) tethered to the door-post by a rope
round its waist, while a supply of water and “ browney ”
(bread) was placed within its reach to last it until the
other members of the family returned.
Arcadia 1
• • • •
Certain recent alleged arson cases round-
aboutly recall the good old days, when Bendigo’s
male population adjourned so largely to the
Barrier:
Hubby in most cases left his wife and youngsters in
Eossession of house—the same being insured—which he
ad built on mining-occupation lease. Houses being
then a glut in Bendigo, there began a remarkable series
of fires, invariably taking place when all connected with
the scene of the bonfire were in evidence at one of the big
Bendigo stores, surrounded by friends to swear an alibi,
in case of trouble. The insurance co’s at last refused to
pay cash, offering, instead, to re-build to the value to
which property was insured. There were no more fires.
One cause of fire was alleged to be this : Before leaving,
an ordinary and easily-lreakable lamp was placed on the
table, tied to it being a few yards of string, having at the
other end the family cat. Then the door was locked, the
rest being left to Providence and the family “ Tom.”
Many a cat was burned in those days to provide a Bendi-
goniau bean-feast.
“Brumby” : A lame laughing-jackass of
mine opened my eyes witli a jerk, t’other day, by
his capacity for holding rat-meat. Poked a rodent
out of a hole in the ceiling, and as he fell Jacky
met him half-way, and had him bolted before you
could say “Great O’Sullivan ! ” Repeated opera-
tion a few minutes later with another rat, which
was as promptly “ scoffed ” by Jack, although he
couldn’t spring to iner t it as before, the weight of
his first victim probably telling on him. Both
rats seemed almost as big as the bird himself.
The back-blocks do not mono-
polise all the women who do men’s work. Saw a
woman t’other day working a team of horses with
a roller on a cultivated field between Ballarat,
and Geelong (Vic.), about 10 miles from latter
village. Meanwhile the train was picking up
men at each station to go over to S’Africa and
burn Boer farms—also being worked by female
hands ! What is this earth coming to ?

Dear Bulletin, — Re “ Walrich’s” inquiry
(8., 15/3/’O2) about earless pigs found by him on
Buckingay (Cobb and Co.’s, N.S.W.) station.
Everyone from Warren to Brewarrina knows the
cause, which is this: When pigs became a pest
several years ago a shilling apiece was paid for
their scalps— ears meant “scalps.” Ergo, to stun
the pig, young or old, and slash off the ears was
all the pig-hunters wished to do, and did with a
vengeance. Hence the hundreds of earless pigs
found on the Lower Macquarie. Messrs. Hill,
Clark, and Co. alone paid for 1600 pig-scalps in
one year on Batterbone. — H. J. M ‘Cooky.
• • * *
For years the collection of the Maori dog-
tax has been a strain on the resources of Maori-
land. Mostly it has been collected by an over-
whelming force of constabulary armed with rifles.
Sometimes a whole district has been gaoled till
the Maoris tired of the confinement, or the Pake-
has of keepiiig them in idleness —generally the
latter. In Gisborne district they have solved the
problem. _ There a Maori Council looks after
Maori affairs and receives a subsidy. The Govt,
struck an estimate of what the dog-tax should
yield, deducted it from the subsidy, and told the
Council to collect the dog-money and keep it.
Then the Council armed itself with an axe and
went round stealthily, seized the dog by the ears,
and demanded instant payment on pain of
butchering the precious mongrel right there.
The Maoris fumed, said “Ouwee!” and other
things, but they paid promptly, and now the
Council sits smiling while it looks at a bank
receipt for £3OO dog-tax collected in one week,
which is a record. It only killed 18 dogs in the
collecting process, too.

“L.C.” : Re swaggies as pedlars
(8., 5/4/’O2). Met a man selling cheap sheet-
music the other day, arid had a talk with
him. Said he had only just tried it. He
invested ss. in Melbourne, and in one day,
wayback, disposed of his stock at a profit of
nineteen shillings —not bad work. He is
not a swaggie now. He travels per rail
and puts up at the best pub.

The dignity of the law, as interpre-
ted by the Vic. country J.P. A swagraan
was recently convicted of larceny, and the
Bench gave him two months. “What?”
cried the swaggy, glaring at the court,
“ two months ! All right, I’ll make it warm
for you when I come out.” As the ser-
geant started to remove the prisoner, the
paR Jaypee called in quavering accents,
“Wait a bit. One month.” The sergeant
hustled prisoner away before he could say
anything further; but, on getting out-
side, swaggy said reproachfully: “You’re
a dead nark, you are. If you’d a-given
me time to bluff him a bit more I’d ha’ got
a quid out of the poor-box afore I’d
finished.”
* * • •
A Chinese cabbage-man, charged
at Christchurch (M.L.) with turning a
comer too quickly, didn’t “savee,” but
stalked up to the Bench and handed in
his summons, wrapped in which were two
£l-notes. Then he rushed for the door,
but was detained. He continued not to
“ savee,” fearing the Bench would ask for
more. But it only cost him 17s. in all, and
when given back 235., a look of utter in-
credulity crossed his features. He couldn’t
understand the two mandarins not accept-
ing a pound each—it struck him as uncanny
—for in China the more money you give
the Bench, the more justice you get. So,
with a yell, he banged the 235. at their
Worships, and bolted.
Two wild whites killed two other whites
out at the back of Queensland lately, and tried to
burn the bodies; and ever since (quite rightly)
about a score of police have been hunting them.
But how many police are hunting the other white
who is believed to have lately potted two Aus-
tralian blacks in the Kimberley district, Westralia
—their charred bodies have been found—and who
made tracks, it is supposed, into the N. Terri-
tory? What has the Westralian Protector of
Aborigines done about it?* The official view of
the relations between blacks and whites up there
was pretty well indicated, t’other day, when a
white, committed for attempted murder, was let
out on small bail.

“ Meera ” : Re horses pulling by their
tails. The practice is, no doubt, cruel in most
cases, and with a heavy load. But I have heard
of instances in which stockmen, finding cattle
bogged, and having no harness at hand, have
hitched a stock horse to the bogged beast by the
tail, and the horse has pulled the beast out with-
out apparent discomfort, although considerable
strength had to be used.
• • * *
A letter to Dungog (N.S. W.) Chronicle :
I notice a challenge from Mr. W. J. Yates to pull with
his bay stallion Mr. R. Tickle’s black horse for £2O aside,
to pull the heaviest load on level ground, the furthest of
three pulls. I cannot understand how a man can be so
stupid as to force a horse, or allow him to pull a load
until he is able to go no further. Only an ignoramus
would do it. If I could put a heavy load on Mr. Yates’
back and get behind him with my whip and flog him along
until he was compelled to drop, then I’ve no doubt he
would think himself badly treated. If we respect men
why not respect the horse? —Yours, etc., J. HANDCOUK.

“Myall,” concerning
a certain pea - striken
horse:
“D.H.R.” (B. 19/4/’O2) re-
commends hand-feeding as a
possible remedy for the effects
of Darling pea. Two years ago
I unsuspectingly bought a
useful-looking nag cheap, as I
thought. Shortly after the
purchase he frequently dis-
played an ardent desire to
walk in mid-air and perform
various other impossible cir-
cus-tricks. During one such
exhibition a seedy-looking in-
dividual emerged from the
small expectant crowd which
had congregated on the pub.
corner and remarked : “Lor’s-
truth he’s got the pea. Yer
want to stable-feed him for
awhile, Mister, and he’ll be
orlright.” The wise coun-
sellor was of the decided
“ horsey ” type, and I took
his advice. When half a ton
of chaff and a proportionate
amount of grain had been con-
sumed,’and the animal’s eccen-
tric manners had apparently
vanished, I began to belaud
the efficacy of the seedy man’s
remedy. But early one morn-
ing I was awakened by a
great commotion in the back
yard, and just got out in
time to see the stable door
collapse and the gee-gee
emerge with an erratic slip
and elevated cranium. He
scrambled through a couple of
fences, and made an effort to
climb my neighbor’s chimney,
getting a hoof hopelessly
fastened in the aperture ’twixfc
the bricks and the wood-
work. The chimney threat-
ened to collapse, and the
neighbor talked fight [and
litigation. A new chimney
would have cost six or seven
pounds, so when the town
constable arrived at the scene
of excitement, I arranged for
the disposal of the ill-starred
neddy with a revolver-bullet.
The hand-feeding remedy cost
me i'B for. fodder, 7s. for re-
pairs to my neighbor’s chimney, and 10s. for removing
the carcase of the defunct. Therefore, although it
may be possible to cure a pea-stricken horse by such
means, I am strongly of opinion that the result is too
uncertain and the experiment too expensive.

While deepening a well at Derby (Vic.)
a workman discovered two large black snakes, at a
depth of 60ft., embedded in soil that (so far as is
known) had never been previously disturbed.
Both snakes were alive and wriggling.
“ Echo ” sends a
mining story:
It was when I
was engaged driv-
ing in the No. 6
level at the Mad
Ox mine. Only
Ted Stretton and
myself, besides a
trucker, used to
work on that level,
except when an
extra trucker was
put down for a
couple of hours.
There were two
undeveloped levels
below us, and
about 250 ft. of
shaft. A raw new
chum came to
work on Hanley’s
shift. Hanley’s
was the shift that
used to relieve ns.
he was a big, silly, soft sort of
fellow named Mitchell, and he
was a subject sent especially
for the amusement of McKillop,
the trucker. McKillop devised
the glorious plan of playing - a
huge ghost joke on him. Ac-
cordingly, the hour of changing
shifts one day found McKillop
ready wai-ing for the cage to come
down with Mitchell in it. Three
successive days he waited, and on the
fourth day Mitchell descended alone. He
stepped out of the cage, hung his billy on the
nail at the plat, and knocked “one.” The cage
flew up the shaft, and Mitchell turned round, his hand
on the open shaft-door. McKillop stepped out from
the darkness of the drive, about 20ft. away, an awful
apparition. Mitchell caught sight of him—l think he
said two words, “ Good God ! ” and staggered back. His
feet caught on the truck-rails right close to the shaft, and
he tripped and went down from sight—2soft. I rushed
to the knocker and signalled for the cage. McKillop was
prostrated by the shock. We ripped his simple ghost-
gear off, and hid it in an old drive. The cage arrived and
Stretton and I descended. Mitchell was quite dead when
we found him. He looked very peaceful—-not a mark on
his face, strangely enough. McKillop came up—“ Just
the shock of seeing the poor fellow go down you know.”
Nobody breathed anything about ghosts. At the inquest
we suppressed the truth manfully, and McKillop’s"spectre
was buried in oblivion. 1 met his mother next day, the
bonniest old Scotch face and the prettiest accent in the
world. “ Aye, Mr. Jackson ye mind, the Lord he hale
gi’en, and the Lord he lia’e taken awa’ ; puir laddie, he
was aye the onlucky bairn of the hnose, but it’s aye hard;
ye ken, on his ain auld inither.” “ It's aye hard,” she hi ut-
tered again. McKillop died 15 years ago. He took t 6
drink, and in the horrors he fell down imaginary shafts in
his mind till the shock killed him.

T. C. Williams, one of Maonland’s wealthy
men, is a great believer in feeding sheep on'gorse
where grass is scarce, and has put his theories into
practice on his property in Auckland district, with
good results. The gor.se is the seedless variety,
and the sheep are turned in when the plants are
green and tender. In three years’ time they
become tough and uneatable.

THE DESERT’S BRIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" O, murm’ring, mournful river,
Why move along so slow,
With saddened glances ever
Oast backward as you go ?
“ O, I go slow with sadness—
Tis madness when I haste ;
How should I go with gladness
These years that I must waste ?
“ My love is ever calling,
O, so hungrily, to me ;
Whilst I pass by him, falling
To feed the greedy sea.
“ I know his brow is burning
At Famine’s fiery breath—
And I am yearning, yearning
To kiss him back from Death.
“ I’m yearning for the day, love,
When men, at Fate’s behest,
Will lead me out your way, love,
To sink upon your breast ! ”

THE WAY BACK CALENDAR.

The luck of drunks : Recently in Mel*
bourne, a cab crashed into a tram in Swanston-st.
Horse and driver were complete wrecks, latter
being taken to hospital. When the shattered
vehicle was being removed a constable looked in-
side and saw a well-known citizen peacefully
asleep on the floor, blind-drunk. On awaking, the
gentleman was quite astonished to find that any-
thing unusual had happened.
* *
At Ipswich (Q.), the other day, a jockey
was fined for furious riding: he had gone on a
fear with his horse, and among other foolhardy
feats, had negotiated at a gallop (without hurting
himself or horse) a railway foot-bridge about 20ft.
high, on either side of which there are 17 steps,
then a landing about 3ft. in width, and then seven
other steps to the top. The luck of drunks !
• * * *
“Haneer”: Is “ Akenehi ”
altogether right in speaking of
the Maoris as exceedingly brutal
fighters? My great uncle, who
was in charge of the Armstrong
battery inthe Second Maori War,
was shot down in a charge; and
while he was lying in agony the
cfiief, Te-ore-ore, chivalrously ex-
posing himself to the fire of both
mends and enemies, went out to
take him to a place of shelter.
The same chief personally saw to
the safety of Dr. Temple, when he
went out on an expedition of
mercy to him.
At recent A.N.A. confer-
ence, R. D. Meagher, M.L.A.,
said that Jan. 26 was the anni-
versary of the day on which
Great Britain officially proclaimed
that Australia was hers. Not
so: Phillip’s commission covered
N.S.W., and N.S.W. only. The
limit of the colony was a meridian
of longitude which practically cut
rigljsjffciirough the middle of South
Australia.
* * *
“Bob. R.” : How long can
a sheep live without water ? Dur-
ing one very hot and dry summer,
when water practically failed
altogether, I turned a flock of 80
rams into a little paddock, sans
water, sans scrub, sans green grass,
to take their chance. Removed
them in six weeks’ time not visibly
the worse for their “ perishing.”
Have heard of sheep living through
a hot summer without water.
Have also seen sheep, a week
without water, drink greedily,
and then fall dead.
* * *
Talk about luck ! The other
day a Broken Hill mine-trucker,
wanting to become a miner,
rushed his loads along, and thus got a few
minutes to practise beating a drill. He was at
that when a bit of stone, only a few pounds
weight, broke off a roof which had just been
sounded and passed as perfectly safe, slid on
another rock on the side, and found him out 12ft.
away. It . wasn’t a Very bad injury ; but he died
of blood-poisoning within a week.
Edwin Arnold, a pleasant British
versifier, who seems to be developing an
unexpected vein of humor in his old
age, nas been advising Australia, from
12,000 miles away, to cultivate the
prickly pear for the purpose of raising
the cochineal insect. There’s any quantity
of prickly pear scattered over Aus-
tralia already, but the kind that accom-
modates the cochineal insect is a special
one, and Arnold thinks the mallee
farmer should introduce it and see how
things pan out. If it doesn’t pan out
anything to speak of, the industry can
be forthwith dropped, and presumably
the m.f. will then be able to besiege
the Government for money to help him
get rid of too much prickly. And the
far-away poet would have a niche in
the same temple as the men who first
introduced the rabbit and the sparrow
and the thistle to an ill-used country.
* * * •
“ S.S.” : The Christs of Trade and
Labor are strangely crucified at times.
From the West copies the thousandth
warning against belting a wedge with
an iron hammer in place of a maul.
Hugh Moore was driving a wedge with
a sled, when a bit of the burr flew from
the head of the wedge, penetrating his
side, and killing him. When young I
was once watching a country blacksmith
at work with a heavy hammer; sud-
denly the steel-face flew from the ham-
mer-head, broke the arm of a small boy-
companion, and so badly injured a horse
standing to be shod that the animal had
to be slaughtered. Cheerful, too, was
the experience of a Melbourne foundry-
man. A piece of white, hot metal fleyv
from under the hammer, penetrated his
thigh, and burnt itself out in the flesh.
* * * *
A curious accident at Donnelly’s
Creek (Yic.) Two fossickers were work-
ing in a gorge and had placed a charge
of dynamite and detonators on a shelf.
One noticed the dynamite fizzing and
tried to brush it out when it exploded
and shattered his hand. A severed
finger struck his mate and gave him a black eye 1

“ Wurry-m ” : Be emu chasing calf (B.
7/9/’01). About 10 years ago, all the emus on Yan-
dilla, Darling Downs (Q.), had to be destroyed, as
they had taken to stampeding mobs of sheep and
chasing them till they dropped.
“ If I might give a short hint to an impar-
tial writer, it would be to tell him his fate. If he
resolves to venture on the dangerous precipice of
telling unbiassed truth, let him proclaim war with
mankind—neither to give nor to take quarter. If
he tells the crimes of great men, they fall upon
him with the iron hands of the law; if he tells
them of virtue, when they have any, then the mob
attacks him with slander. But if he regards truth,
let him expect martyrdom on both sides, and then
he may go on fearless.” —De Foe.

Just now, the ambitious N.S.W. bush
person who cannot attain the “honor ” of a J.P.-
ship, seeks fame as a member of the presently-to-
be-constituted Rabbit Boards. One country
“mimber” boasts of a collection of 100 letters;
requesting his support, and only half the appli-
cants want to be chairman of the board !
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

A GENUINE CASE.
BEAT: “'Sense me, Guvnor, I’m not arstin’ for
money, but I’m starving. Couldn’t yer take
me down to lunch with yer?”
Fe the mysterious origin of fires. About
last Christmas a serious outbreak occurred at the
railway offices, Eveleigh, Sydney; but most
searching inquiry failed to throw any light on the
origin of the blaze further than that it came from
a heap of waste paper and oil-rags lying in a corner
of one of the rooms. For experiment a similar
heap was placed in the corner and watched. Soon
after, fire broke out again—showing the arsonist to
be the sun, which, blazing overhead at mid-day,
shone through a window that acted as a burning-
glass.
Can a Man be Young at Seventy-Three?
After the prime of life do increasing years
always mean a decline of physical strength ?
Does it never happen that in this fight, against
Nature’s law, man is able to pose for a time as the
winner? _ .
The problem is as old as mankind ; a Wmgelah
settler’s story (says a local paper) is only the
latest expression of it. Old age and increasing
infirmity seemed to have set their seal upon Mr.
William Shearer, of Wingelah, Cate Creek,
Brewarrina. During his early life lie suffered
many hardships, and whilst in Queensland,
thirty-seven years ago, took fever, from the after-
effects of which, until three years ago, when lie
was made hale and sound by the famous Dr.
Williams’ pink pills for pale people, he continued
to suffer. Indeed, at seventy-three years of age
the old gentleman is as healthy as possible,
declaring that he feels as well as he did when a
youngster. The interesting story of his rejuvena-
tion (writes an interviewer) was unfolded to me
the other day.
“I came from England to Melbourne in 1853,”
said he. “Twelve years later I went overland
on horseback to the Gulf of Carpentaria, a
distance of 1800 miles, living a life of hardship all
the time. Whilst there the heat was especially
severe ; I took fever, and until lately have never
been free from its after-effects. My body and
limbs were partly paralysed so that at times I
could not mount a horse or move about quickly.
I was thin and debilitated, and lost all appetite.
Medical treatment failed to benefit me.”
“ How long did this continue ? ”
“Until about three years ago, when a friend
writing from Scotland advised me to try Dr.
Williams’ pink pills for pale people. I did so,
and after taking the contents of one box I felt
.greatly improved, my blood began to circulate
properly, and I became stronger. Several more
boxes cured me. Since then I can mount a
horse with ease, work actively, and ride twenty
,qr thirty miles every day. I have recommended
Dr. Williams’ pink pills to scores of sufferers,
many of whom have derived great benefit from
their use.”
Lots of men (and women, too) who are not as
old. as Mr. Shearer, would yet like to be a little
younger, and the rejuvenating_ effects of Dr.
Williams’ pink pills are often' mentioned by
those who have used them.
They are known to have cured a number of
critical illnesses, most of which, indeed, are
only aggravations of a lack of “tone” in the
nerves ,or a watery or vitiated blood. Dr.
Williams’ pink pills for pale people (see that
Dr. Williams’ name is on the box) have cured
nervous exhaustion, paralysis, premature decay
of the powers, consumption, ansemia, biliousness,
indigestion, St. Vitus’ dance, nervous exhaustion,
&c. They are sold at two and nine by chemists
and storekeepers, or, if not readily obtained
without question, they can always be had, post
free, from the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney. Nervous and anaemic
sufferers are earnestly warned against medicines
which act upon the bowels; they are particularly
dangerous. Dr. Williams’ pink pills are not a
purgative; on the contrary, they purify and
enrich the blood and tone up the nerves, so that
risks ,of infection by the bubonic plague are
avoided.


*distributing kitchen.

noon at The Block, Collins-street. It was
unanimously decided that the scheme was a

PERSONAL.
Captain Wallington states that the
Governor-General is making certain
reductions in his establishment, and is
sending some of his horses and servants
home. —Daily paper.
Our artist does not go in for pathos, as a
rule, but the picture of a Commonwealth
Governor stopping in bed pending the
reconstruction of his only pair, strikes
the imagination forcibly: The Govern-
ment really ought to have raised this
young man's screw.

WORDS OF PRAISE FROM Mr. BLAY.
No. 5, Ripley Terrace, Luddenden Foot,
W. S. Rice. April, 6,1901.
Dear Sir, —After using your treatment for three
months I find myself cured of my Rupture, at the
age of 55. I had been ruptured for ten years.
I shall advise ail people afflicted with this com-
plaint to use your treatment, as I know from
experience that it will cure permanently. You must
have a wonderful knowledge of rupture to Atone
so perfectly without seeing the rupture.
I am in good health now, something I have not
enjoyed for ten years before. You can use this
letter in any way you choose, and I hope it will
guide others to a perfect cure. —Yours truly,
T. BLAY.
There is absolutely no question about the curative
ability of this combined and perfected method. It
has stood the test of time; it has been through the Are
of critical cases ; it has come out victorious in every
curable instance. It has saved the child to a life of
independence; it has given the youth his natural
endowment of health and strength to Aghtthe battle
of life successfully; it has conquered the blight upon
old age and turned years of suffering into ending
days of joy and giadness. Whoever is Ruptured, or
knows a person offering with Rupture, should keep
in mind the fact that in eighty per cent, of all cases
there is a daily liability that before night they may
say good-bye to earthly things. Does not this fact
influence one to pau e and consider whether they
are using their best efforts in the way a wise Creator
intended they should? And if the forethought,the
research, and the painstaking effort of one who
invents and studies for the relef of others is placed
in our hands, is it not our duty to read and listen
patiently, that we may take advantage of that which
contributes to our earthly beneAt?

POLITICAL
POINTS
Kyabram reform mainly means putting a
oheap ass in the place of a good horse.
The rather far-away billet of surveyor of
the Federal capital is already exciting sharp rival-
ries in the States.
The Adelaide papers cut down the honorific
titles of Federal members to “Sr.” and “Rp.”
It is short and business-like, bub there is a certain
wildness about “ Sr. Higgs ” and “Rp. Knox.”
It is an open secret up north that Philp
would have gone to the Coronation but for the
fear that internal strife for his billet might wreck
his Cabinet. As an alternative, ex-pounder Rut-
ledge may go and bring a bauble back.
The Kyabramapootra has a suspicious
aversion to reform of Vic. Leg. Council, the only
Second Chamber in the world which has an abso-'
lute veto on all popular legislation, the only one
which can be neither dissolved, ignored, nor
swamped.
Tasmania is locally governed by 102 road
trusts, 33 cemetery trusts, 21 municipalities, 19
town boards, 15 water trusts, seven marine boards,
six rabbit boards, and some fruit boards and
main road boards. Some of the municipalities
refuse to levy any rates
at all and let things sfide,
and one of them last year col-
lected a total income of £1
12s. Id. A Government which
carefully parcelled the island
out into districts, and placed
one looal authority in charge
of each, making it council and
road, water, cemetery, rabbit,
marine and fruit board all in
one, might do some good.
THE WORD OF PROMISE.
JONES: “Hello, writin ’! What
are you writin’ for? ”
SMITH (who is perming a proposal of
marriage): “ For posterity !”
Dear Bulletin. —For brazen cheek, it
would be hard to beat the Sydney morning dailies.
It isn’t generally known, but I intend it shall be,
that all the time the D.T. and Herald have been
shrieking for economy their officials have been
simply wearing out Premier See’s door-mat endea-
vouring to bull-doze him into granting £2500 a;
year of public money to pay the Railway Com-
missioners to run special early morning trains, to
take their papers free into the country. I have
myself stumbled across them in the Premier’s
aute-room twice in the last fortnight. I don’t
know if Mr. See has any intention of granting
their preposterous demand, but I am inclined to
think, from the attitude of these papers towards
his Govt., he has bumped it out.— Arthur
Griffith, 9/5/’O2.
S.A. democrats, not too cheerful at the
result of S. A. elections, were quite cheered by the
defeat of Von Doussa, the politician who engineered
the unspeakable Snow Tram Grab through S.A.
Parliament. Strange to say, it was this that
killed him, for Von Doussa’s country constituents
reckoned that abolition of the present trams
would rob them of their market for horseflesh !
Barton’s studied vagueness and elusiveness
in recent speeches begin to disgust even his most
ardent admirers. Some of his recent deliverances
are almost cryptic. Barton’s figure leads us to
expect robustness and plain speaking ; the disap-
pointment at his anaemic futilities is on a par with
that when a great robust Italian, whom you sur-
mise is a basso profundo, turns out to sing with a
piping treble.
The new S.A. Constitution Act reduces
the Assembly from 54 to 42. Only four of the old
members gave it up (and one of those four was
dead), so that eight had to be thrown out. And
the State, while it was about it, threw out eight
more in favor of new men. The Leg. Council
drops from 24 to 18. Four old members stood
out, and only one new man came in, so that only
three had to be forcibly ejected.
“ A disappointed political boss requires
the services of a handy Premier. Must be
vigorous, yet servile ; rigid, yet pliant; a strongly
democratic Tory, intensely economical while
spending large suras of money, an independent
time-serving weathercock of fixed principles, and
a stem and upright groveller. Religious infidel
preferred. Will be thrown aside when done with.
Apply Age Office, Melbourne.”— Copy of the advt.
which should have appeared, hut didn’t.
South Australia still follows the policy of
supporting strong men, irrespective of the views
they hold ; the policy which enabled her to send
to the Federal Convention, and afterwards to the
Federal Parliament, such a good, if mixed, team.
The one district of Torrens has just returned to
the local Assembly (as was freely foretold) the
Premier, the head of the Opposition, and the
leader of the Labor party i
A commentary on the change in the nature
of things at Sydney Town Hall was furnished by
the episode of the Mayor (Thomas Hughes) being
served with a notice, signed by himself, to carry
out certain structural alterations in some proper-
ties he owns, or in default prosecution would
ensue. In the old days, to have interfered with a
mayor’s or an alderman’s property, or that of an
influential friend of a mayor or alderman, would
have meant early, if not instant, dismissal of the
official concerned. It is said that the person most
delighted at the discovery of the change is Mayor
Hughes himself. It was Hughes who started to
break down the rotten old system by exposing the
officials who allowed one high personage’s rates to
run into arrears, and another high personage to
alter the assessment on a friend’s property.
The Federal Electoral Bill pro-
bably embodies the simplest and most
democratic electoral law in the world.
Every adult man or woman who is
sane, out of gaol, has put in six months
residence in any part of the Common-
wealth and is a natural-born or natu-
ralised subject can have one vote for
both Senate and House of Represen-
tatives, and only one. Also no person
who has a vote for a State legislature
to be debarred, by any reading of the
Federal law, from having a vote for
both Federal Houses also. The under-
lying trouble is that, apparently, every
British Hindu or Singapore or Hong
Kong Chow who comes here can claim
a vote as a British subject—which is a
further solid reason why they should
not come here.
Melbourne Age pegs away vigor-
ously, but with little intelligence, at
its “ Parliamentary reform and re-
trenchment ” campaign. Its usual
process is to shake its arms in the air
and say, as in its leading article of 29th
April: “ What sense is there in a little
State like Victoria employing an army
of 30,000 public servants?” It looks
awful enough, no doubt. But if police,
railway officials, school teachers, and
other sundries were called public ser-
vants in England that country would
have more public servants in propor-
tion to population than Victoria has.
So would many other countries. No
doubt the Vic. public service can stand
reduction, but all the same it is already
no larger than the average. The Age
should reason instead of whirling
like a maniac. It is not the want of re-
trenchment nearly so much as the
want of a bigger revenue, and the
right application of it, that is the trouble in
Victoria, and a bigger land-tax is one way to get
that revenue.
“Kiwi,” of Maoriland, takes his solemn
oath about this:
A paragraph is being published in the press here that
Trenwith, the Labor member of the Jingo Victorian
Cabinet, is about to offer a boon to working-men by
Introducing a Bill to enable them to make their homes
inalienable. On paper, the thing looks beautiful, but it
may interest the Vic. working-man to know that we have
had such a measure on the statute-book in Maoriland
since 1895—“ The Family Homes Protection Act.” It
originated with our great, benevolent, but “ faddy ”
Stout, and, when before the House, many columns of talk
took place about “ free homes.” The Act enables anyone
who is out of debt to make his home, to the extent of
£l5OO, inalienable for all time. But, though it has been just
seven years on the statute-book, not a single home has
been “ protected ” under it. Our statesmen had better
leave off such slipshod experiments, and try their hands
at enabling the great mass of men to get homes.
Westralian Arbitration Court, the Chief
Justice presiding, has settled its first wage-dispute
by cutting the child in two. The most important
part of the proceedings was the Court’s ruling-out
of the lawyer, though here again it cut the child
in halves by letting the lawyer’s managing-clerk
in.
The agitation in favor of reduction of
members of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, without
regard to any other reduction, may be interpreted
by the fact that almost all who originated the cry
were either not known when payment of members
was under public discussion, or were pronounced
antagonists to that Radical measure.
Maoriland Liberal press begins to show
signs of bartering its white blood for a Japanese
meat-trade. It is proposed that now “we are
allied with Japan,” although the coolie should be
prohibited, the trader should not be discriminated
against—in the interests of frozen meat. How
the over-running of M.L. by a horde of Jap. shop-
keepers and hawkers is going to assist an export-
trade of frozen meat to Japan is very hard to see.
The Melbourne law-students’ monthly, the
Summons, for May, contains an article on the
Marais court-martial case, recently decided by
the Privy Council in a manner which has shocked
constitutional legal opinion in England. The
article in question is evidently by a skilled hand
in constitutional analysis, and hints that if the
Privy Council is right many trite chapters in the
English constitution will have to be re-written.
An interjector at a Kew (Vic.) reduction
of members meeting concentrated much food for
thought, and sobered the whirling reducer visibly
with, “ Why not reduce them to one ? ” Reduc-
tion may have its charms, but the historic savour
of the number of the Decemvirs, or the Council of
Ten, or even of the Thirty Tyrants, to say nothing
of the one-horse plan where the solitary legislator
would be called a Czar or a Sultan, is not exactly
sweet. Besides you can’t bribe a big legislature.
Jenkinson, ex-leader of the Queensland
Remnant, is out of Parliament, and his organ
Progress, has just published its last issue. Pro-
gress thus summarises its own career :
Progress opened as a red-hot Opposition organ, de-
veloped into a Ministerialist, and then somersaulted back
to the Opposition. It was everything by turns, and no-
thing long ; and the public do not trust a political light-
ning-change artist.”
A touching death-bed confession.
There is strong evidence that the first
victims of the coming financial “cold snap” in
N.S.W. will be the old-age pensioners. Sugges-
tions from various quarters, that the relatives of
pensioners should be compelled to contribute to
the allowance granted, and that “ a careful test
as to character” should be made, indicate just
such a movement as that which, in Victoria, has
degraded the pension scheme to the level of a cold
and grumbling charity.
ONE FLAG, ONE FLEET, ONE THRONE, ONE
BOSS, ONE FATE.
Oaptain Pursey Chirnside, M.L.A., told
his Victorian constituents that he accepted the
Kyabram programme (46 members lor Leg.
Assembly) and also “ believed in civil servants
having a vote of their own, and returning, say,
one or two members.” Certainly, why not? In a
House of 46, public servants, 2 members; publi-
cans, 2; cold-teaites, 2; tote supporters, 3;
bookies, 2, and so forth ; and for the squatters a
whole House all to their own cheek, as at present.
The ungrammatical Pursey has brilliant ideas at
times.
In the Commonwealth Senate a Pulsford bird sat
Singing “ Tariff! low tariff 1 no tariff 1 ”
And I said to him, “ Pulsford bird, what are you at
Singing ‘ Tariff ? low tariff 1 no tariff! ’
Don’t Australia want an incentive to grow?
Don’t Australians need any favor and show?"
But he only replied, “ It is D.T., you know,
Singing ‘ Tariff? low tariff! no tariff!’ ”
A country correspondent :
Editor Bulletin.— N.S.W. Parliament will do well to
keep wide awake when transactions under the Closer
Settlement Act come up. Around several country towns
there is a tendency towards “ syndicating ” small worn-
out holdings with a view to offering them to Govt, as a
big estate of considerable value. Now, the spirit of the
Act is clearly to “ burst up ” large estates—to make com-
fortable homes for 20 or 30 families where one, a few sheep
and many rabbits previously subsisted, not to simply swap
owners. A Minister might be cajoled by a persistent
representative of Smith’s Curse to put a “liberal”
interpretation on the Act, so Parliament needs to' be
careful.
When the revolution took place in Maori-
land the struggle was between the pawn-com-
panies and land-sharks on one side, and organised
labor (including both brain and manual laborers)
on the other, and the first-named went under.
Now another fight is impending, between organ-
ised labor and the organised cockatoo—a most
grasping, soulless sweater, who sweats the life and
brain out of even his own children. The Cookies’
Union numbers 25,000, but that only represents
about one-third of the Cocky voting-power, at the
very least. *
According to Kir wan, M.H.R., women
postal officials at Kalgoorlie, Coolgardie and
Boulder City (W.A.) are, in some cases, paid only
£7O a year, and women employed in the telephone
service even less. If so it is soulless sweating: a
woman simply can't live decently and keep herself
bathed and clothed in clean linen on £7O a year
on the Westralian goldfields. And an Australian
Government shouldn’t put itself in the position of
cutting down a fair supply of food and tubs to its
women employees.
The Federal Government proposes offer-
ing £324,000 in bonuses to encourage the produc-
tion of iron and steel (£250,000), galvanised iron,
wire netting, &o. (£50,000), spelter (£20,000),
reapers and binders (£4000). The bonus system,
combined with Protection, has already been tried
in Australia, and has proved eminently successful:
the Sydney Freetrade newspapers, protected
under the free-postage system, and liberally
bonused by special trains and other concessions,
have grown to be very large and profitable
properties. Under these circumstances, the
Federal Govt, is walking on a well-trodden path
with the light of a Targe and patriotic precedent
to guide it.
Barton’s job in London will be rendered
more difficult by the presence of Seddon. Barton
cannot, in view of his Melbourne banquet speech,
pledge himself to Imperial Federation, Imperial
defence, or preferential trade. Seddon will be
there, ready to bid for popularity against all
comers, as he did in 1897. He will be petted and
caressed, and held up as a shining example—and
reported, there’s the rub!—and his Jingo gush
will be contrasted with Barton’s reserve, because
the latter cannot pledge his Commonwealth with
the assured recklessness of King Dick of Maori--
land. The contrast between Seddon’s expansive-
ness and Barton’s caution will be so great that ere
long Barton will come to be looked on in London
as a kind of a traitor, although Australians know
him as a decided Jingo.

A PIANOLA in the Home.
ITS PRACTICAL USB.
Does the Cost of the Pianola bring enough pleasure
in return to warrant the investment ?
THE Pianola will enable every owner of a piano to play
upon his own piano whenever he desires. Not only
this, but every member of the family can also play upon it—
not one or two pieces, but practically every composition ever
written for the piano.
Did you ever stop to consider how much pleasure there is
stored up in your piano ? Have you counted the number of
times in a month it is used, aside from the tedious practice
of a child ? Perhaps you or some member of your family
“plays a little,” but the difference between playing a little
and playing everything you wish is considerable. It is
worth thinking about. The Pianola supplies the deficiency.
It even makes no difference if you do not know one note
from another.
The fingers of the Pianola rest upon the piano-keys, and
strike the notes for you just as they are printed on the
music sheet.
You experience all the pleasure of playing, because you
control the expression, which is the soul of music. Your
mood or taste enters into, and gives a distinct individuality
to the rendition. It is this which gives the Pianola its
fascination to the musically critical, and caused Paderewski
to endorse it. It is this human element, permitting a musi-
cal result in the true sense of the word, which has caused
Paderewski, Sauer and Rosenthal to have Pianolas in their
own homes. The repertory of these great pianists is
limited to twenty-five or fifty compositions which they can
play in concert form at any one time. They are glad to
have it increased by the Pianola, and to be able to play
without troubling with the notes.
With the Pianola you can play every style and class of
music ever composed—Liszt Rhapsodies, Chopin’s Noc-
turnes. the grand operas of Wagner and Verdi, light operas
of Sullivan and De Koven, and all the latest popular
favourites. In fact, with the aid of a Pianola you can play
upon your piano any selection you wish, you can have dance
music or song accompaniments, and the question of finding
“some one to play ” need never arise.
It may be purchased on the hire system if desired. Send
for Catalogue.
The Pianola is the only piano-player endorsed
by musicians of note.

Jenkins Iscariot had his reward at the
S.A. elections. Among the five members for
Torrens, he crawled in a bad third. Among the
three members for Flinders, Minister Foster got
third place. Minister Gordon got fourth place
among the four members for the Southern District.
Treasurer Butler was the luckiest; Barossa
returns three members, and he got the second
position. Such a unanimous crawling-in of
Ministers at the tail-end of things has
seldom been seen. The Tory leaders, both
in Council and Assembly, topped the poll,
and the Jenkins Iscariots didn’t score one first
place among them. Only an alliance with the
Labor party or the Tories can keep Jenkins in
office now, but the Labor party won’t look at his
new policy, and the Tories have come back so
strong that they may reckon they can do without
Jenkins.

The suggestion of a Mr. Goodchap, of
Sydney, that Premier Barton shall take a hand at
the actual Coronation of the King will give the
performing Archbishop a shock —if he hears of the
weird idea. A spheroid of gold with H.M.’s
dominions beyond the seas marked thereon in
gold or Royal red enamel, so runs the scheme —
and the spheroid aforesaid is to be placed on
Edward’s crown by Mr. Barton just before the
swearing-in ceremony. Mr. Goodchap forgets
Mr. Seddon, who would naturally desire to add
his little emblem to the pile. A spheroid of
greenstone with M.L. in a bas-relief of kauri gum.
Why not ?
The Sydney Technical College scandal
makes no visible progress towards clearing up.
Months ago it was understood the Public Service
Board had handed over the whole business to
Attorney-General Wise, as some of the financial
transactions alleged were too serious for even the
almighty Board to deal with. Since then nothing
has been heard. The P.S.B. knows nothing, the
Minister for Education, whose department is very
much discredited by the whole business, knows
nothing. All of which ignorance is most remark-
able. Either certain officials in the Mining
section of the Technical College were guilty of
gross corruption, or are much maligned, and it is
due to them and the public that they should have
a chance of clearing their characters in fair, open
trial. The present inaction is calculated to make
the public think—lots of things on the subject of
corruption.
