“ "Veteran,” to The Bulletin :—
Why not tell the story of the “ ease somewhat akin" to
Morant’s referred to (B. 3/5/*2) as having occurred at
Delhi. The officer in question was Hodson, and the
affair ruined him. If some of the staff on the spot, in-
cluding a late Field-Marshal, had had their way he would
have beeen hanged. He murdered not two but four sons
of the old King, and he did it with his own hand. No
other prisoners were killed—none were taken except the
old King; his family, and half-a-dozen other important
personages. Delhi, a city of nearly 200,000 people, wasn’t
at all like a S’Alrican village. An army little over 20,000
strong could not venture to play any monkey-tricks in
such a place with its narrow streets and stolidly-built
houses, every one of which was a potential fortress. It
took nearly a fortnight after the place was entered to
occupy it even in name. On the day after the place was
entered, old Wilson, -the General in command, con-
sidered the situation so dangerous that he actually
wanted to withdraw, and only the energetic protests of
his chief engineer, Smith, Neville Chamberlain, and
Henry Norman made him change his mind. Hodson’s
murderous folly made the situation more dangerous. The
four men he shot could themselves have done no harm,
but their murder caused much discontent among the
native cavalry, who were nearly all Mohammedans, includ-
ing Hodson’s own regiment, which got into such a state
that he had to be removed for his own safety. That was
the second occasion on which his violent and brutal
temper got him into trouble. He was never employed
again, and narrowly escaped the gallows.

The present is the season of the year,
with its sudden cold changes, when influenza,
bronchitis, asthma and toothache germs get in
their fine work. Oh ! the joyful toothache ! The
best way to fight off these persistent intruders is
to fortify the human system with occasional
“ contingents ” of a good, reliable whisky like
D C.L. _ The medical faculty almost unanimously
concur in their advice. No one who has once
tried D.C.L. can doubt it to be the very best
stimulant for such work—or any other. D.C.L.,
the Whisky of the Wise.*
Mr. James Forsyth, M.L.A., writes to
point out that his majority for Carpentaria (Q.)
was “not 8, but 8 times 8 ” (re B. par., 10/5/’O2).
A Victim to Files.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills Effect His
Escape.
“For the past twelve months I have been a
sufferer from piles,” states Mr. Charles Morrison
who resides at 72 Webb-street, Fitzroy, Vic!
“ 1 was under several doctors during that period,"
but received no permanent cure. A friend of
mine one day, in the course of conversation
advised me to give your Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills a trial, which I did, and I am very happy to
he able to say they have made a thorough cure of
me. I might state that I had only taken three
bottles in all. The first two or three doses gave
me relief. You are at liberty to make what use
you like of this, as it may be the means of helping
other sufferers. ” •
Straight as an Arrow.
A Family Cured.
Truthful words are like well-armed arrows;
they are straight and pointed, and fly true to
the mark. There is no fooling about the truth,
it always strikes home, and this is how the words
written by Mrs. W. Clarkson, Enmore, N.S.W.
make such a deep impression on everybody "who
reads them. She writes Myself and family
have been cured of biliousness and liver ailments
by using Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I can
also state that they are superior to all kinds of
tonics for the hot and muggy weather that we
experience in New South Wales.” Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and
a positive cure for biliousness, indigestion,’ con-
stipation, headaches, sallow complexion, liver and
kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils and blotches,
and for female ailments. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers, Is. per bottle, or six bottles
6s. 6d. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
S.A.J.C. meet ended Saturday. Money
(£13,512) put through the machine wag £669 ahead of
same day last year. Caledonia went out red-hot for
Fisher Stakes (15 starters) but failed to get a drum. He
had the steadier of 10.10 up, conceding over 4st. each to
winner (Lome) and second (Clifton), and 2.3 to third
(Annotate)—a big contract. Lome was so little fancied
that his backers secured a dividend of close to £62 Flag-
ship (unlucky in the Cup) annexed S.A.J.O. Handicap
cantering.
* * * *
Fifteen starters for Adelaide Cup, Amiral
being the only malcontent. Flagship was nearly 2to 1
more favored than second fancy, The Idler; Footbolt,
third, was a shade less in demand. Adelaide and Lome
made the early running. At the 6fur. Footbolt, who had
been showing prominently, dropped right back. The
favorite came from the rear to fourth place at the home
turn, where The Idler got to the front and stayed there,
winning comfortably from The Victory, who finished
well, with Flagship third. Footbolt travelled very fast at
the finish. The lm. sfur. was cut out in 2.61 J. Footbolt
ana Flagship were unlucky, and Flagship’s rider alleged
foul work against L. Kuhn (on Nitre) but stewards, on
the question of premeditation, gave Kuhn the benefit of
the doubt. Amount invested on the winner totalled
£4930, which provided a div. of £5 12s.
* * * *
The Idler was got by Far Niente from the
Goldsbrough mare Waiting, at Hobartville (N.S.W.). At
yearling ssles he fell to bookmaker Barney Allen for
65gs. Said that stable netted £3OOO through The Idler’s
success. Present ownership is something of a mystery, for
when he won at Moonee Valley (Vic.) a protest alleged
misstatement and omission in entry,” under a rule which
demands that if bookmaker or trainer have an interest in
a, nag he shall be raced in the names of all concerned.
Though their ruling might have affected The Idler’s
Adelaide Cup engagement, the Moonee Valley stewards
haven’t, so far as is known, yet investigated the objection
—which inaction smells somewhat.
* * * .
The first Adelaide Cup, in ’64, was worth more
to the winner than the present race, there being a sweep-
stake of £5O each among the 12 starters, and £5OO added.
But an owner can start to day for £8 in all, and the stake
is £BOO. Explanation —the Jockey Club can always rely
on getting something well over £lOOO out of the totalisator
commission during the afternoon. In other words, the
public finds the money instead of the owners.
• » • .
Positano colt Milano, on the strength of sur-
prising track work, topped the market in Rosehill 2yr.
old race Saturday and again sent punters up badly.
Fractious at the barrier, some broken gear necessitated a
return to the paddock, and then, though jumping away
well, he did no good. Scarlet Runner got a great go and
headed the field of 14 home, but was lucky to beat Kelso’s
Mrs. Grundy as, following upon a bad run, that filly came
out wide at the end and, streaking up, only lost by half a
length. Scarlet Runner was bred by Mr. C. McPhillamy
and got by Haut Brion from White Rose; she is there-
fore half sister to Tecoma who shaped so disappointingly
among the galloways. The promising Mrs. Grundy is by
Wallace from Duenna, and consequently related to
Amberite, Pelissier, Flintlock and Acetine. She still
belongs to her breeder—Mr. John Lee—who leased her
to Kelso at yearling sales last season for all two and
three year old engagements, the consideration being £lOO.
* * * *
Much-waited-for and watched Transvaal looked
to have a cut, judged by betting operations, for Granville
Stakes, but shaped poorly. Rush to “ get on ” bewil-
dered Mo for a while, and once the nag was at 2’s ; but
The Jester won in a canter, pulling up with Hazel Lad
second, and Neptunus third. At Canterbury a fortnight
before, Transvaal, after being made particularly hot by a
misguided public, fell back to almost any price, and in
the actual race seemed to own the situation if he wanted
to. On certain deductions from little happenings
attendant upon working the “commish.”, this scribe
purposes speculating another crown when things seem
within Transvaal’s scope. '
* * » .
The .Jester, winner of itosehill Granville Stakes,
Saturday, is a five-year-old gelding got by Antaeus,
and is an up-country performer of note. Backed down
to 5 to 2, he was sent out a hot favorite in a field of 22,
and, humping 8.6, won easily from flag-fall to judge’s
stand. At Forbes (N.S.W.), recently, War Cloud, with
41b. less, beat The Jester, and on the second day he went
under to Merralie in the Cup, the latter carrying Ist. 101 b.
more. After his manner of gathering in Granville Stakes,
R.vdalmere Mile looked “good goods” for The
Jester, and money was showered on him till nothing
better than 7 to 4 could be had ; then he fell as flat as he
rose brilliantly before, which caused some hard thinking.
Finish of the struggle was stirring. Fanfare, apparently
hopelessly out of the hunt ere turning for home, bounded
up and pipped the money on the post. Certainly not
more than heads separated the first four.
* * * •
Mr. H. C. White’s big disappointment, Tatter-
wallop, by Clan Stuart from Tatters (Old Clo’s dam), col-
lapsed, apparently paralysed in the hind quarters, at
Randwick, Saturday, and was destroyed. Tatterwallop
carried a lot of money in S.T.O. Hurdles, previous week,
but ran nowhere. He won Oby Hurdle Race at Caulfield
m October. Said that Tatterwallop hurt himself at last
Melb. Cup meeting, bringing a swelling in the side, which
may have had something to do with his sudden break-
down.
• • • *
Till now three two-year-olds have heaped up
£lOOO each of M.L. prize money, and two of these owe
their origin to Seaton Delaval. Stepniak colt Orloff has
won the biggest lot (£1125), Poriura (£1040) follows him,
with Idas (£1015) third on the list.
* * • *
Old-time jock., “Paddy ” Piggott, up on Melb.
Cup winners, Haricot (’74) and Chester (’77), is doing
“ riding work ” at Brisbane. T’other morning the St. Jean
colt, Wabash, unseated him, but tough old “Paddy”
escaped unhurt.
* • * *
Pensioned-off Bacchus has had eight seasons at
racing, and won 19 times in 105 starts. He’ll luxuriate
in the paddocks of Yarra Glen henceforth.
* * * *
A back-blocker’s impressions of Sydney pony-
racing -“ I came to the conclusion that the starter is
the principal figure at the gathering ; he decided three of
the five races that I saw, and it was pure bad luck on his
part that he didn’t score a fourth, as he gave one horse a
break which seemed to put the remainder hopelessly out
of it, but another managed to get up at the right end
and score a neck-win. The people who attended were
there for purely business reasons, and I seemed to be the
only innocent abroad in the whole congregation. Ic was
worth the price of admission to get a glimpse of the excel-
lence to which ‘ tick-tacking ’ has attained. Half-a-dozen
experts were practising this * art ’in the paddock. The
rapidity with which they pulled their ears and noses
waved arms, extended fingers, and slapped their breasts
and the evident ease with which their pals on the flat
interpreted the movements and signalled back for further
information was a triumph of art. Coming home in the
tram half-a-dozen perfect ladies, varying in age from 25
to 60, squeezed into our compartment. They had spent
the afternoon punting. Thev claimed intimacy with
crack pony jockeys, and seemed sanguine of being able to
pick about two-thirds of the winners of all the pony races
which would be decided the following week. I feel
bound to confess that even * picnic '-race officials could
glean a lot of useful information on Sydney pony
courses."
* • • •
A jumpers’ flat-race, at Aspendale Park (Melb.),
last week, eventuated in the disqualification, for sus-
picious riding, of the jockey who rode the winner, Good
Day. The case was doubly remarkable. In the first
place, Good Day made a dead-heat of it with Nansen, and
it was not until he had won the run-off by a short neck
that jockey Short was called to account for his con-
spicuously erratic performances in both races. Onlookers
were mostly disposed to think the man guiltless of inten-
tionally bad riding, though he took the nag “all over the
shop” each time, the run-off being his masterpiece of
mismanagement. The stewards seemingly drew unfavor-
able conclusions from the betting market. Nansen
started at 6 to 4 on, whilst Good Day was friendless at 10
to 1, in a field of four.
• • • •
The Federal Postmaster-General now tempers
the wind to the shorn “ Tattersall ” by allowing the
Hobart Deputy-Postmaster to decide whether letters
posted to Mr. Adams’ address shall or shall not be de-
livered. Locally understood that Hobart Postmaster has
been instructed not to be officially aware of such letters
unless upwards of a bushel arrives in one mailbag.
• * » »
The nasty little Bisley rifle team squabble in a
nutshell : N.S.W. demanded that the team should be
chosen on a basis, not of ability, but of population. It
was a miserably paltry claim. The cricket team now in
England has far more N.S.W. reps, than the population
basis would give the State ; and, though naturally disap-
pointed, the other States didn’t go crying about their
wrongs, but cheerfully agreed to the choice on the ground
of ability. N.S.W. is truly like a querulous old woman in
her Inter-State relations. She didn’t raise the population
basis issue when she got more than half the cricket team,
but “ wouldn’t play” when there seemed a danger of her
having a fraction of a Bisley rifleman less than her popu-
lation entitled her to.
* • * •
The senseless squabble about the rifle-team just
sent by the Commonwealth—or rather by certain sub-
sidised private associations within the Commonwealth
to compete for prizes at Bisley, ought to show the folly of
trusting to that sort of encouragement as a means of
training good rifle-shots for the defence of their country.
Side by side with these subsidised associations and clubs
is a system of so-called “ musketry instruction ” for the
different regiments and corps, in which each man is
expected to fire away a certain number of cartridges
every year. Last year the practice, according to the
official report, was “ unsuccessful.” “There was no im-
provement.” Nearly 20 per cent, of the men were not
exercised at all, and of those who were, 21.28 per cent
turned out “ marksmen,” 22.52 per cent, first-class, 39.96
per cent, second-class, and 16.24 per cent, third-class.
That means that one-sixth of the men could not be
depended on to hit a target twice as big as a man
placed less than 300yds from them. There are
no prizes to be won at these ordinary annual rifle-
practices, and so 20 per cent, of those who should
enter shirk them and reserve themselves for the matches
got up by the subsidised associations where they can win
something. Rifle associations don’t help to make average
men good shots. If a man can shoot well to begin with,
or finds that he improves very quickly with practice, he
will stick to it ] but the average man who is never likely
to rise above mediocrity and does not win prizes soon
drops rifle-practice for good. If the Oommonwealth
really wants a good average standard of skill, not
a few good shots and a host of very bad ones, such
as it now has, it should cease subsidising the asso-
ciations. They are only good for the few superlative
shots who divide all the prizes among them. The money
thus saved should be divided among the marksmen and
first-class shots—the good average men who cannot hope
to win matches. That would soon raise the average
shooting of the regiments to something respectable, and
make them good fighting machines, which they are not
now.
* * • «
All States but Tas. were represented in eight-
oar race at Adelaide, Saturday, when five crews—a record
—competed. Victoria from the first showed superior
form and pace and left the Orow-eaters behind in the first
mde. N.S.W. popped right out after the first 200 yards ;
Q., S.A., and W.A. made things hot for each other, till
S.A. just showed in front, with Q. second, and W.A.
third. Second mile of the course (3m. 364yd5.) saw S A
make a big effort, but it had no hope. A mile from the
finish Vic. was rowing beautifully even and winning
comfortably. N.S.W. was at this stage a mere speck on
the horizon. The race was virtually between Vic., S.A.
and Q., and ended in Vic. winning Dy 20yds. from S A
who held a margin of 1£ lengths from Bananaland’s
heavy-weight combination after a gruelling last half-mile.
Westrahans were three lengths further off, fourth, and
poor old N.S.W. 200 yards away last. Time was 17 7.
• * • *
Up to date Victoria has competed in 26 inter-
state eight-oar races, won 21, been second 4 times, and
third once. N.S.W.’s count is 24 races, 4 wins, 18
seconds, one third and one last. Bananaland has taken
part in 13, won one, been second twice and third seven
times. So far neither S.A. or W.A. has succeeded once.
* * • *
At Adelaide, Saturday, Australian champion
amateur sculler Daley again qualified for the title. Only
opponent was local crack, Fahey, who was completely
outclassed. Queensland R.A. brought this feature into
existence ten years ago, and, though contested at irregu-
lar intervals, it has proved interesting occasionally.
Slack was the first winner (’92). Latest is Daley’s third
success. He didn’t compete in 1900, but squelched that
year’s winner and others a year later.
* • • #
Veterans are almost afraid to suggest to the
young ’uns that visiting English teams of 15 and 17 years
ago were better stuff than the men whom Stoddart and
Maclaren captained. The idea sounds old-fashioned and
conservative. Also, it is an attempt to belittle the vic-
torious achievements of the modern Australian cham-
pions. Likewise, it is “un-English,” because it insinu-
ates that the old-country cricket has grown weak and
flabby. Nevertheless, the simple faith in those purely
professional Shaw and Shrewsbury Xl.’s of yore is based
on an undeniable strong foundation of—Shrewsbury.
The Nottingham “ crack ” has suffered much ill-health
since the time when he used to take the field in Australia,
with long Gunn, Billy Bates, and Barnes (the original
batting and bowling Barnes), Maurice Read, Flowers,
Attewell, Sherwin, Briggs and co. He can’t be
altogether the champion he was, but his per-
formance of last week showed him able to take the
measure of the best Australian bowling to-day. Arthur
Shrewsbury is the last rose of his cricketing summer
blooming alone. A slow batsman to watch, inasmuch as
he never plays riskily if he can help it, but a moderately
fast scorer. A stonewaller who gets a fair percentage of
boundary hits in a casual way. Hayward is a more showy
batsman of the Shrewsbury type when he gets set, but he-
doesn’t start with the confidence that marked the Not-
tinghamshire play when the now “ old man ” was leader
of the school. In fact, Australia, in the past dozen years,,
has seen no English bat who equalled him for “ sound-
ness ” — i.e., skill, patience, and nerve; combined—nor any
visiting Eleven that had as much cold courage in its com-
position as the Shaw-Shrewsbury teams displayed.
Seeing that Jones, J. Gunn and Jessop, the
batting failures of M’Laren’s crowd in Australia, have 1
each of them reversed their “ form ” at the first attempt
in England, it would be only natural for Mac. and Hay-
ward to reverse their “ form ” and get disposed of very
cheaply. But the brilliant success of the other returned
tourist, Braund, is rather ominous. Braund’s perform-
ance—an average of 50 runs per hour for two hours and
five minutes—suggests that the Australians can’t afford
to have Trumble invalided when a Test comes along.
• • • •
Australians made no mistake about their second
match, when, with a total of 474 to t’other side’s 287 and
183, they beat Notts by an innings and four runs. No
previous Australian XI. has done a better thing than
this, which was chiefly brought about by Darling (128),
Hopkins (80), and Armstrong, who took eight wickets for
47 runs in the finishing couple of hours. Yet Armstrong
is rated much more of a bat than a bowler. This perform-
ance is reminiscent of Bilowell’s achievement, when he
accounted for 10 Surrey wickets in one innings. Darling’s
doings so far discount the idea that he is a back-number,
and should never have been chosen.

Present Australian Eleven will hardly whack the
same amount of coin among its members as previous teams
have done. Match with London County at the Crystal
Palace failed considerably from a “gate” point of view,
but unfavorable weather may have had much to do with
that. At Nottingham, where they ’thuse over cricket
(and this was the only occasion the Australians would
visit them), the cable man tells that the attendance was
“ only meagre."
• * • •
Ttastling premier Harry Pearce and Jack Pearn
supplied a lively maul (Graeco-Roman rules) at Perth
(W.A.), recently. Pearn, by sheer brute power, secured
the first fall in 17Jmin. Next, both rolled off the stage
amid a great uproar. Pearn was badly hurt but went on.
Things were torrid till Pearce, with a marvellous head
spin from a “ full Nelson,” flattened the big fellow out—
7frain. Third bout went to Pearce in 1.40, and, during
the fourth, which had lasted 3min. Bsec., they rolled,
tightly gripped, to the stage edge, where Pearn scraped
himself severely and refused to continue. Probably
they’ll come together again on the turf. Australia hasn’t
for years known a fiercer or better ’rastle than this, in
which science triumphed over superior force.
* • • «
At individual supremacy of N.S.W. Bowling
Association, the last two in—Carrie (Petersham) and
Harrison (Ashfield) —met. Early stages saw scoring
fairly even till Harrison (with short “ heads”) went along
to 16, while Carrie stood at 8; but latter, with a spurt,
had a run to 23, while the other only accumulated 4. Harri-
son then brought his lot up to 25, with Carrie’s at 27.
Thence to the close Harrison added but one, and the game
finished 31-27. It took 36 “ ends ”to decide the match.
Carrie, to carry off the supremacy, defeated Rummell
IWaverley), Moran (City), Strong (Balmain), Young
(Ohatswood), and Lewis (Balmain).
* » » »
Final of N.S.W. bowling rink supremacy
(Saturday) went to Hale’s City four who beat Coker’s
team (also City) by 26 to 16. It is interesting that City
Club chose three rinks to represent it and paid their
expenses, but the successful combination, though coming
from the same quarter, entered entirely “on its own.”
Skipper Hale was twice champion bowler of M.L., and
three years since played third in Mr. Harris’ N.S.W.
champion four.
* * * *
Interesting light was shed on professional
cycling when Bilmartin’s trainer, Henry Gordon, sued the
“ Plugger” for £5O training exes. Plaintiff swore “that a
few weeks before the Austral Martin said it would be his
last race, and if Gordon (his trainer) kept his mouth shut
as to his intentions to win he would give him 15 per cent,
on the total of the first prize, £4OO. If plaintiff had put
a single pound on Martin, or opened his mouth, the
betting market would have been ruined, and Martin and
his friends would not have been able to win the £7OOO or
£BOOO they did.” Martin didn’t pav up and allegedly gave
as his reason : “ Yon are not entitled to anything ‘at all.
I bought the race, and had to give £3200 to win it.” It
was further alleged that Martin bought £9OOO worth of
suburban property since the race. Just as the noses of
the public were beginning to gather around the case it
was settled out of court.
* * • •
Jack M'Gowan, the most business-like glove-
fighter in Australia, who has returned to Melbourne with
a bag of Sydney money, claims to make the income of “ a
professional gentleman” at the game. There being
nothing in his class in Melbourne to match him with, he
is taking on the heavier men, and undertakes to account
for Bob Fraser, the Port Melbourne slugger, on a date
fixed. “ There is no money in the business unless you
fight hard and fight often,” M‘Gowan says, “and I’d
fight horses rather than not fight at all.” M'Gowan is a
most industrious young man, and is understood to “ ’tend
Sunday-school.”
* • • *
Veteran pug Abe Willis, verging on 40, will
fight Westralian Joe Dempsey in Sydney three weeks
hence. Willis was a good ’un and fought ’Murkan negro
Dixon when both were at their prime, a decade ago, in
’Frisco for the world’s 8.4 supremacy. Abe can hardly
show anything near the form of those days, though few
have looked after themselves better. Dempsey is a quiet
but vigorous youngster whose appearance belies him if
he can’t fight, but he’ll want more than brute force to
win this match.
» * * *
Bruisers* James and Frost, at Sydney Golden
Gate A. 0., Saturday night, made things fairly’huzz right
through six rounds. James stopped most of the thumps
but he hurled a few at Frost, and twice looked like a
winner, despite that bets of 2 to 1 were freely shouted
on Frost at the end of every round. Hair-raising rallies
were frequent, and James’ grit stood out often, but his
stock of wiles and vigor didn’t at all equal Frost’s ; still,
he promises splendidly. Early in the seventh, a heavy
cross spread-eagled James, and as he staggered up, the
wipe soared from his corner.
• • * *
Sydney’s crack middleweight bruiser issues a
“sweeping challenge” and threatens to go to work if
nobody takes it up. This seems to imply that somebody
is bound to fight to prevent a pug going to work.

The Ensign, winner of Victorian Coursing
Derby, at Moonee Valley, sprung a surprise on most
people, and local coursing scribes knew little of him. He
is by Blarneys tone (Livingstone-Honeysuckle) from Miss
Reveller and three months ago was sold by his breeder,
Mr. O. Troy (Williamstown) to present owner for £B.

Australian fencing premier “Tierce” Peterkin
closed on N.S.W. Gun Club’s pigeon -slaughtering
Challenge Cup (alleged value £2l), and £2O in money, by
killing 20 out of 22. Great merit of Peterkin’s butchery
is that he had to win three times in all before becoming
boss butcher. First competition took place in October
last year.
One Chamber, or Two.
In' the present agitation for the reduction of the
State Parliaments and other economies, the Labor
parties are fatuously throwing away a great
opportunity. They ought to be in the forefront
of the agitation, like Uriah the Hittite and other
conspicuous characters, and directing it into
a popular demand for the reduction to a
moderate extent of the State Assemblies,
the better payment of the reduced Assem-
blies, and the abolition of Upper Houses
—these fretful dry bones which hang use-
lessly on the tree of legislation. But for no better
reason, apparently, than that some of the Labor
members might lose their seats in the process of
reduction,_ the party has held aloof and left the
agitation in the hands of the Tory gang, which is
adroitly turning it in a strictly Tory direction.
The wild cry of the Tory party is, in most cases,
for a reduction of both Houses to one-half their
present membership, whereas if the utterly
futile Legislative Councils were cast on to
the dust-heap, which is their fitting sphere of
usefulness, a reduction of the Assemblies by
one - third would be quite sufficient. The
crowd which is now engineering the movement
aims at having a few large electorates where it
hopes that, by reason of the cost involved in can-
vassing so great an area, the money-bag element
will carry the day as a general thing. It wants a
very small Assembly in which it will be possible
to buy outright a majority when any very large
public Job is under way—a thing that is often
impossible in a larger body. (The Council doesn’t
need to be bought when jobs are under way ; it is
for the job every time.) It aims, in the name of
economy, at reducing the Assembly salaries below
the level of a living wage, which is liable to fur-
ther reduce the Labor membership. And it seeks
to give the Upper'Houses a new lease of life ;
when they are reduced, any demand for real
reform will be met with the plea that the Coun-
cils have just been reformed, that the new order
of things should have a fair trial, and that con-
stant tinkering with the Constitution is undesir-
able. Parliamentary reform is a war-whoop
of some weight just now, and through
u j dismal lagging behind of the Democratic
body the Tory reform programme is the
one most before the public. It is especiallv
strong on the preservation of that aged
but mouldy bulwark of the Constitution, the
Legislative Council—the body which never origin-
ates anything or effectively repairs anything, but
merely blocks every reform measure for four or
five years, and then, when the Assembly passes it
for about the seventh time, lets it go through,
fretting as it does so, and stating that now at - last
the country is really and definitely lost. Great
Britain acquired its bi-cameral system by accident.
When the Commons first sent members to the
venerable English Parliament the landed digni-
taries seem to have had them put away by them-
selves for the same reason that the dog is put
away by himself in the kennel instead of being
allowed in the drawing-room. Britain has two
Houses now, not because it is supposed to be good
or useful or advisable to do so, but because the
people insist on having one House (the Commons)
to represent them, because they don’t want peers
m it, having realised that peers are, legislatively,
a bad lot, and because the old impression handed
down from the days when the peers formed the
whole show, that a peer has an inborn right to
sit somewhere, hasn’t been wholly got rid of.
Almost all the bi-cameral Parliaments of the
world are a servile copy of this purely accidental
British system. Yet it is a curious fact that out
of the 51 legislatures (a very miscellaneous lot of all
sorts and sizes)commonly enumerated in the British
Empire, 33 consist of only one House, and only
18, including the Mother of Parliaments itself, are
built on the bi-cameral system. The Common-
wealth might do a great deal worse than go with
the majority in this matter.

The plague, in killing Dr. Wray, Queens-
land Government medical officer, broke out in a
new direction, he being the first medico to be
attacked on the’ continent, so far. (As this is
written Dr. Maclean, of Brisbane hospital, lies
seriously ill of plague, which he is believed
to have contracted from a woman who died
at the hospital gates.) The medical man takes
fairly strict precautions when holding post-
mortems on plague subjects, and, considering
his risk, has so far come out very well. In
Vienna, recently, there was a localised outbreak
of “ lung-plague ’’-—the most deadly form of the
pest. The victim, bis doctor, his nurse and the
undertaker all died. The Sydney Chow, who has
shown, until recently, a remarkable immunity
(considering all things) in the two outbreaks,
seems to have suddenly lost it; t’other week five
Chinese died in as many days.
The Vic. Ministerial situation :
The determined effort of Melb. Age to kick out the
whole of Smiler Peacock’s team of incapables with the
object of getting the scalp of the rebellious Trenwith
seems likely only to hurt Syrae’s two intimate pals,
McCulloch and Gillott. Tuft-hunter Mac. is about the
most addled person in the whole Smiler crowd, but there
is never a word in Syme’s paper reflecting upon that fact,
and now that the old man is away crawling at Court,
that journal may reasonably be expected to award a
double thumping to Works Minister Morrissey. Also,
Samuel Gillott is about the most crumbly' old Tory in
Vic. Assembly, and every time he speaks, gives away the
fact that he is hopelessly out of touch with any kind of
reform, but David sticks by him nobly, and divides his
share of the pummelling between Burton and Dan
®uggan. But it seems pretty' definite that their two jobs
will be absorbed by other Ministers, and, as Solicitor-
General Wynne, David’s only other intimate in the
Cabinet, is cordially detested by his colleagues, he also
may find it convenient to quit. The man on top just now
is “ Weary Willie ” Trenwith, who seemed doomed to a
speedy political death until Syme’s ill-advised attempt to
finish him off still more rapidly than he was going
galvanised him into fresh life.

The rich man, even during a drought, has
the best of anything going. The rebate of 50 per
cent, on fodder required for starving stock is a
help for big sufferers from the drought, but the
rebate does not apply to the small man, who can
only obtain a few tons of fodder at a time from an
agent or storekeeper.
Writes “ Bronze Medal ” :
Bulletin (3/5/’O2) gives a touching description of a rescue
by a distinguished M.P., as (allegedly) related by himself.
The rescued was the presiding goddess of a Manly private
bar, and the story was started by the politician gallantly
expressing a wish that he might some day be able to
prove his devotion to the p.g. by dragging her front a
cruel, watery grave. Next day, the politician was
overwhelmed with congratulations on the/actof his brave
deed. So glowing were the details as supplied to the
hero that he began to half-believe that he had dis-
tinguished himself as suggested. All town got to hear of
it, and the M.P. might be still gathering laurels had not
an officious person from the National Shipwreck Society
called on the rescued Hebe for details of her saviour’s
bravery. Her indignant assertion that she—well, what
he did assert upset the joke's future career.
Sydney Telegraph’s Maoriland correspon-
dent (sth May):—
As to the Australian criticisms anent the now famous,
if not notorious, speech delivered to the Maoris of the
Wairarapa, founded on the idea that Mr. Seddon had
advocated the letting loose of all savage customs of war
with Maori contingents, our public does not go with
them. We know here quite well that Mr. Seddon is not
bloodthirsty, and when he opened his mouth a bit wide
to the Maori, we all felt the matter would end right. The
real meaning we, most of us, took to be that the Maori,
being a better bush fighter than the Boer, would have
settled him long ago, according to the rules of modern
war, had he been allowed. It was a statement flattering
(and so intended) to the Maori, and probably far from
accurate.
Even this admirer doesn’t venture to deny that
Seddon “opened his mouth a bit wide ” and said
the things ascribed to him—the things that Seddon,
with his cheerful, off-hand lie, has since denied.
But his excuse is that Seddon didn’t mean them
when he said them, and that they were “ far from
accurate” (in other words, they were false), and
the matter “ would end right ” (i.e., Seddon would
crawl out somehow).
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer Of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
Premier Barton left the information be-
hind him as he departed Englandwards that a
commission was to be appointed to consider the
advisability of the Commonwealth taking over the
Northern Territory. Barton, who rushed to take
over New Guinea and those lands in the New
Hebrides, is strangely cautious nowadays. South
Australia can’t make the Territory pay because it
hasn’t the money to open it up; the Territory is
almost as useless to it as Australia would be to
Bulgaria. If the Federal Government doesn’t
take it, it will probably in the end be jobbed away
bodily to some syndicate. Whether it is a paying
property or not, it is necessary to take it over in
self-defence; the present tenant or bailee can’t
carry it much longer, and 500,000 square miles of
Australia can’t be dropped beside the highway,
in a manner of speaking, for anybody to pick up.

In addition to its wonderful tone quality, the Harp-Zither has a great many
advantages over all other Zithers. It is the only Zither which may be played
while holding vertically like the Harp, or it may be laid upon a table as is
necessary with the ordinary Zither.
Observe the diagonally crossed strings, almost the same as in a piano the
melody strings passing over the chord strings. By means of this improvement
in construction the similarity in tone and volume of the piano is produced.
Beautiful in Design, Grand, Resonant Tone, Perfection in Every Point,
and it is the easiest to learn to pla.v of any musical instrument in existence-
A child can play it almost at sight. The reason anyone can play this instru
ment on first trial, even though the person may know absolutely nothing
about music, or may not have an ear for music, is this : Each string is num-
bered, as is each note in the music, so all one has to do to render the most
difficult selections is to strike the strings as indicated by the numbers ; hence
we guarantee that anyone able to read figures can learn to play.
The Harp-Zither is built on the lines of the large harp which sells at
£2O and upwards, and to the astonishment of all the Harp-Zither has the
louder tone of the two ; in fact, its tone is similar to that of a piano.

English law-students, in a recent Univer-
sity examination, had this question to answer :
Whether the South’African Republic, under recent cir-
cumstances, was entitled to issue an ultimatum, and to
proceed to hostilities?
All the students answered in the affirmative, and
the examiner approved this view, and publioly
stated the fact. On this point, indeed, all
teachers of international law are agreed. It is
only blind Jingoes who differ.
“ A remarkable surgical operation is re-
ported from Glasgow. An elderly woman was
admitted to the Victoria Infirmary there as an
urgent case. She was in great agony caused by a
tumor in the stomach. After she had been in the
institution for a couple of weeks an operation was
decided upon, and the task was undertaken by
three surgeons. The abdomen having been opened,
the existence of a very large growth was revealed,
and it became apparent that the woman could be
relieved only in one way, namely, by removal of
the entire stomach. This was successfully done,
and the bowel now takes the place of the stomach
effectively enough. It is reported that the patient
digests her food very well, and is steadily recover-
ing strength.”

PROSPECTUS
OF
TOOHEYS, LIMITED
Capital: £550,000, in 550,000 Shares of £1 each.
Of this number 375,000 Shares, fully jjaid up to £1 per Share, are to be issued to the Vendors, and the
balance of 175,000 Shares are offered to the public (and for these applications by bona-nde
customers and employees of the Vendor Company will have priority to the extent of 10,000
shares), payable: ss. on application, ss. on allotment, at the office of the Broker of the
Company, and the balance at the Office of the Company one month from date of allotment.
The proceeds of the 175,000 Shares will be paid to the Vendors.
Directors: Hon. J. T. TOOHEY, M.L.C. Hon. HENRY MOSES, M.E.O. J. T. TOOHEY, Esq.
Hon. F. T. HUMPHERY, M.L.C. F. T. THOMPSON, Esq.
General Manager: Mr. G. MARTIN. Secretary : Mr. J. MAURICE TOOHEY.
Bankers: THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA.
Solicitors: Mr. F. B. FREEIIILL ; Messrs. MACNAMARA & SMITH; Mr. WALTER G. PARISH.
Broker: Mr. JOHN R. JONES, Yaralla Chambers, Pitt Street.
THE Company is formed for the purpose of acquiring- from the Vendors as a going concern the old-established and
lucrative Brewery Business carried on by them as “ J. T. & J. TOOHEY, Ltu.,” in the State of New South Wales,
including their extensive Brewery Establishment in Sydney, together with their capacious Cellars, Wine Stores, new
Malt House, Aerated Waters Factory, Ice House, Bottling Establishment, Stables, and Various Freehold and Lease-
hold Hotels, and other properties in Sydney and Suburbs, and at Newcastle, and the Book Debts, Plant, Goodwill and
other assets of the business in terms of the contract hereinafter referred to.
The Freehold and Leasehold Properties (exclusive of a very large number of Leasehold Hotels having various
unexpired terms up to 2] years, which are given in) have been valued by Messrs. Hardie and Gorman and Richardson
and Wrench, Ltd., in conjunction (as to the Newcastle properties) with Messrs. Creer and Berkeley. The Valuation,
dated 24th March, 1902, is £287,418, of which £273,168 represen s the value of the Brewery Premises and Freehold
Properties actually owned by the Vendors, and £14,250 the value of Leases of Hotel Properties leased to the Vendors,
having various unexpired terms from 21 to 90 years.
The, preponderance in value of Freehold Hotels over Hotels held on Lease evidences the strength and stability of
the concern.
Messrs. Hardie & Gorman, and Richardson & Wrench, Limited, in their report, say, “ In estimating the value of
the Hotels, we have not taken into consideration the twofold advantages which you possess—namely, the rental
value on the one hand, and the hold upon the business on the other. We have rather regarded them from the fair
market value point of view.”

book debts are stated at £136,067 ss. The stock is valued at £54,447. Accordingly the valuation of the assets, ex-

Shareholders will (as is not the case with a Company formed to establish or develop a new industry) sf ire a
really remunerative and increasing business, in full working order, and returning well ascertained profits. A
ence to the Joint Certificate of Messrs. Jas. Robertson, Priestly and Co. and Messrs. Miles and Vane, set out i.t foot
hereof, will make this clear, and the profits should be substantially augmented by the Aerated Waters and Cordial
Manufactory, which is practically a new addition to the business. The new Malt House has started work, and con-
siderable savings will be thereby effected.
The position of the Brewery is both central and attractive, and the erection of the new Central Railway premises
in its immediate vicinity will no doubt increase its value. A large portion Of the buildings (which are of stone and
brick) has been recently erected, specially designed on up-to date lines for the most economical carrying on of the
business. The buildings, which are most substantial, having been erected by Messrs. Toohey to suit the require-
ments of their extensive trade, are in good order, and, without doubt, of their kind, unsurpassed in the Southern
Hemisphere.
The Brewery Plant and Machinery are of the very best description, and are so extensive that any future
expansion of the business can be provided for by a comparatively small outlay.
The high quality of the Vendors’ Malt Liquors is well known.
Founded about 1870 by the Hon. J. T. Toohey and his brother, the late Mr. James M. Toohey, the Brewery has
within a comparatively short period attained its present strong position.
The Hon. J. T. Toohey, after many years of active business, is desirous of being relieved somewhat from the
pressure of management, and that is the sole reason for this Company being formed.
It will be observed that the Vendors retain 375,000 shares out of the 550,000 shares into which the Capital of the
Company is divided. No stronger guarantee can be offered of their confidence in the continued prosperity of the
concern.
The Hon. J. T. Toohey and Mr. J. T. Toohey are under obligation to serve the Company for a period of Seven
years, with special remuneration for so doing. The former is not to be compelled to devote his exclusive attention
to the business, and will only draw remuneration while actually engaged in the service of the Company. Mr. J. T.
Toohey is to devote the whole of his time and attention to the business of the Company. The Hon. J. T.* Toohey and
his representatives have, under the conditions set forth in the contract hereinafter referred to, power to nominate a
successor for the period therein mentioned.
Both gentlemen have entered into an agreement not to be engaged in a similar business within 300 miles of
Sydney for a period of Ten years under very heavy penalties.
The Company takes over the business as from the 31st January last, and becomes entitled to the profits from that
date. Out of such profits the Vendors are to be paid interest on the amount at the rate and for the term mentioned
in the Contraot hereinafter referred to. After providing for this payment, there will be a substantial balance to go
to the credit of the new Company.
The Company receives the benefit and undertakes the obligations of Current contracts, as well as of current trade
liabilities, which are estimated at about £13,500.
It is intended to declare dividends half-yearly.
The whole of the charges incidental to the formation of the Company, including brokerage, advertising, legal
expenses, etc., will be borne by the new Company.
The following Contract has been entered into, and can be seen at the office of Mr. Walter G. Parish, Solicitor,
Martin Place :
Ist May, 1902.—J. T. &J. Toohey, Limited, Ist part; Hon. John Thomas Toohey, 2nd part ; John Thomas
Toohey, 3rd part ; John Rider Jones, 4th part; the said Hon. John Thomas Toohey, John Thomas Toohey, and
Francis Bede Freehill of the sth part.
The original Certificate of the Accountants and the original Valuation of Messrs. Hardie and Goiman, and
Richardson and Wrench, Limited, can be seen at the office of the Broker of the Company.
The business is being taken over subject to all existing contracts in connection therewith. These are of the
ordinary trade character, but, including as they do numerous contracts with the Company’s customers, Builders,
and others, cannot be specified. Technically such contracts are or may be contracts within section 66 of the Com-
panies Act, 1899, but applicants for shares will be held to have had notice and to have waived their right to be
supplied with particulars of such Contracts.

The Greatest Thief in the World.
No, it is not the man who removes your goods
in the night, nor the plausible diamond-studded
gentleman who takes your hard-earned cash in
exchange for shares in some worthless mine.
Thieves they are, without doubt; but not the
greatest of thieves. There is a far subtler thief
than they, and, moreover, what he robs you of is
more valuable than your money. He was named
and rated many years ago by a celebrated poet
when he rote “Procrastination is the thief of
Time ” —procrastination, postponement, the put-
ting off of the doing of something which ought
to be done now; time, the intangible stuff of
which life is made up. The man who is robbed
of his gold may possibly recover it; the man who
is robbed of his time has lost so much of his life
utterly and for ever.
Now, here is Mrs. Maria Jenkinson, of No. 7
Searle-street, Sydney, N.S.W., a lady who has not
yet reached middle age, but whose health broke
down so long ago as June, 1886. A good many
things have happened since 1886, among them
the recovery of Mrs. Jenkinson’s health in 1891;
but there is no good and sufficient reason why
that desirable event should not have taken place
five years earlier. The means which restored
Mrs. Jekinson to health were as easily obtainable
in 1886 as in 1891, or as they are now. Perhaps
Erocrastination has something to answer for here.
iet us listen to what Mrs. Jenkinson herself has
to say on the matter. —“Until June, 1886, I was
biassed with sound health; I could eat, sleep,
and work well. Then came a change; I grew
listless, despondent, and irritable. My sleep was
disturbed by dreams, and there was an ever-
present sensation of weight on my chest. My
appetite failed, and I became so emaciated and
weak I could scarce crawl about. A furred
tongue, biliousness, and severe headache had
become my normal condition. For five years did
I fight against my illness, and was attended by
six doctors, who all agreed that I was suffering
from chronic dyspepsia, and were all alike in
failing to relieve me. After also trying various
advertised remedies with no better result, I was
induced to buy a bottle of Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. The first bottle mitigated my
worst symptoms, and when I had taken the third
bottle I felt like myself again. Eight bottles
of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup did for me in
six months what six doctors failed to accomplish
in five years—restored to me health and strength.
For years past I have never on any account
been without Mother Seigle’s Curative Syrup in
the house.”
It is never too late to do a good thing; but it
is better to do it first than last.*

The Collapse of Party Government.
Nothing shows the complete foolishness of the
Party Government system better than the present
mixed condition of Victorian politics. The
Ministry as a whole doesn’t possess the confidence
of either the House or the country—in fact, the
sending in of its joint resignation shows it doesn’t
even possess its own confidence. Yet the only
result of its dismissal would be to put the Opposi-
tion in its place, the said Opposition being
mostly the feeble, futile Irvine, the awful
Gillies, and the dilettante, disastrous Shiels.
And through this country’s slavish imitation of
British customs, and a confirmed notion that
nothing political is good that doesn’t follow on the
lines of the “ Brit’sh Cons’tooshun,” it is impos-
sible to dismiss any one or two Ministers—though
they be as incompetent as an ant under a
road-roller —without sacking the good as well
as the bad and replacing them by a pos-
sibly even worse combination. The party
system was not created in England because
England saw any merit in it, but because
the Commons happened to split on a question
which made reconciliation or coalition practically
impossible, and it has remained split ever since.
Australia adopted the idea without any definite
line of cleavage, just as it might have ordered that
the Premier should always walk with a crutch
because the British Premier was lame, or just as
it might have ordered that there should be tunnels
on all its railways, even on the western plains,
because such things were necessary (and darned
expensive) in Britain. In Queensland the old
Opposition perished because it couldn’t tell
itself from the Government. In N.S.W.
See and Lee are duplicates of each
other, and in S.A. Jenkins and Darling
are supposed to hate each other for think-
ing exactly the same way. The process
suited to these conditions is to let the House elect
the Ministry. Then the party which doesn’t like
that Ministry, if there is such a party, will form
an Opposition and elect a leader; and if there is
no such party it won’t. As things are now a Lee or
a Darling party has to be bribed by the reversion
of the Premiership to hate the Government, even
if it has no objection to the Government. Also if
there are just enough good men in the House to
form a Government the country has to do without
the services of some of them, and put up with
inferior men in their stead, for some of the good
men must be saved up for the job of hating the
Government on the Opposition benches, and
objecting to it, and calling it bad names. Since
the fiscal question went out of State politics the
struggle to prevent the so-called Opposition from
looking so like the Government that they can’t be
told apart has become pitiable. And the real
Opposition, which is mostly the Labor Party, and
which opposes on principle, is abused for upsetting
old traditions—whereas it is the only party that
adheres to the old traditions.
Queensland’s Central Sugar Mills.
A cane-grower in one of the leading tropical
sugar districts of Queensland writes the following
remarks. His name and address can be supplied
to any inquiring sugar-mill director who desires
them —but they wont:
If the Federal Parliament is sincere in its desire for a
White Australia it should take over the control of the
Sugar Works Guarantee Act from the Q. Government.
This Act, when passed, was supposed to be a measure to
enable the small cane-growers to co-operatively erect
central mills. There is no doubt the central-mill idea is
a good one—the only means by which it is possible for the
small men to enter upon sugar-growing. But the mills
must be owned by the cane-growers and the con-
stitution of the central mill co.’s—in order to ensure
these being of service—must be such as will provide
against any one man, or two or three men, having control
of the management of the co’s. That was the original
conception of the S.W.G. Act. But that is not the spirit
in which the Q. Continuous Government makes the ad-
vances. In the majority of the mills, the State moneys
lent under the Act were lent to a few large landholders,
who used the Act to enable them to sell their properties
at a greatly enhanced figure while retaining practically
all the voting-power in the central mill co’s. In this
matter, the Pleystowe Central Mill Co., of Mackay, stands
out pretty prominently. A syndicate bought the old
Pleystowe mill and estate (about 4000 acres) for £4OOO.
This was valued under the S.W.G. Act, but was not of
sufficient value to secure the necessary advance
to erect a mill. Balnagowan estate (of 5000 acres)
was then joined with Pleystowe, and the Govern-
ment advanced a sum, of which, at the present moment,
£39,000 is unpaid. It might be mentioned here that the
Pleystowe Land Syndicate sold the old mill site of about
10 acres to the Pleystowe Central Mill Co., Ltd. True,
they say the money has not yet been paid over, but when
the prominent members of the land syndicate are direc-
tors of the mill co. there is nothing to prevent them
handing over the money to themselves at any moment.
The Pleystowe Land Syndicate and Mr. John Cook, of
Balnagowan, or their agents, induced people to settle
on Pleystowe or Balnagowan, selling them land
at, in the great majority of cases, from- £8 to
£lO per acre. Incidentally it might be remarked
that the Pleystowe land had been growing cane for
20 or 25 years at this time in the usual plantation style
(manureless) and that one farm on the Balnagowan
Estate which was sold at £8 per acre had been offered to
a man, who refused to take it, at 10s. per acre a year or
two before the erection of a mill was contemplated.
The Central Mill, built with State money, thus
increased the value of these rich men’s land sixteen-
fold. In the Central Mill co. the farmers were
allotted one share per acre. The farms contained from
50 to 100 acres each. The qualification for a director
was to be 500 shares in his own right, thus making it
practically impossible for a farmer to become a director
at the price which was charged for the land, to say nothing
of the fact that only 5000 shares were allowed to be allot-
ted to farmers (vide Mr. W. Steedman, one of the members
of the Pleystowe Land Syndicate and promoter of the
Mill Co., at the recent inquiry) out of an issue of 60,000
shares. The other 55,000 shares were reserved for the
members of the Pleystowe Land Syndicate and Mr. John
Cook, or, as the articles of association had it, the
directors could dispose of shares as they pleased.
The only men who held sufficient shares to
qualify as directors were E. M. Long, chair-
man of the Pleystowe Land Sj’ndicate, W. Steedman,
secretary of the same, and John Cook, of Balnagowan ;
so the situation may be summed up thus : One or two
big landholders received from the Government, at very
low interest, the money to put up a central mill. The
fact of the mill being put up (with Government money)
increased the value of their land about sixteenfold, and
enabled them to make large fortunes. And these big
landholders still retain sole control of the mill (put up
with Government money), take the biggest part of the
profits, and as bosses of the mill, on which the prosperity
of the district depends, can make the farmers obey them
in almost everything. Moreover, the fanners are
mostly mortgaged to the land syndicate, which is
practically the same as the Central Sugar Mill direc-
torate. The directors are thorough believers in black
labor, and, in fact, colored labor of every description,
and possess the power to make things very unhandy for
any farmer with white labor tendencies. Wherever the
large landholders secured mills the same state of affairs
as at Pleystowe exists in a greater or less degree. If the
Federal Parliament desires to establish a white cane-
sugar industry on a firm basis in Australia—and this can
only be done by a system of small farmers growing cane
for central mills—it should take over immediately the
control of the S.W.G. Act. There is no use in expecting
ths Philp Govt, to administer fairly any Act tending to
d away with alien labor.

An American writing from St. Helena :
Colonel Paget has cleared the camp of all the Boer
scum. These number about 200, and have sold their souls
for a tin of jam—that being all the extra ration they get
for taking the oath of allegiance to England,

Melb. Age : “ Mdlle. Lotty will introduce some new
features.” But how can she ?
People up in the Beechworth and Wangaratta district
(Vic.) have just found a male Amy Castles, and seem
intent on booming the young man for all he’s worth.
Local press describes him as “ possessed of a voice of
extraordinary compass comprising four octaves, and his
rendition of the high 0 showed a phenomenal ability.
Either as a tenor or soprano he appears to be equally
successful, his voice being powerful, but well modulated,
and in some passages of surprising sweetness.” Also,
they remark that this fortunate youth was greeted with
“ simultuous applause,” which is probably a new kind of
hand-clap specially invented for the laudation of the
combination of Reevesand Patti. Itma3 r beparenthetically
remarked that if this prodigy from the Kelly gang dis-
trict really does possess a four-octave voice, with the high
C, presumably soprano, or an octave higher than Dani’s,
as the terminal note, he must likewise be a basso
profundo, as the lower C is far and away deeper
than any but the most phenomenal basses can dive
after. The best that could be done with a voice
of three different kinds would be to abolish two of the
varieties, after which it would probably be found that the
one remaining was only small and tin-kettly after all.
Many women can sing from 2.) to 3 octaves, and London
was once paralysed temporarily by a lady contralto-bari-
tone, but the four-octave voice seems physically impos-
sible, unless the young man has two throats of different
formations—which would be a doubtful advantage in the
event of his being very thirsty.
Veteran Wybert Reeve has collaborated with Sutton
Vane, the successful melodrama-merchant, in the produc-
tion of a play of M.L. life which will practically introduce
Maoriland to the London stage. The new play should be
a good payable subject, appealing alike to the stummick
and the Jingo spirit of Sutton Vane’s public. Maoriland
is “where the latest English mutton comes from,” also it
has acquired a gory notoriety through its copious
discharge of cohentingents for S’Africa.

this period. They would like to know something about
the taste of audiences in the different capitals ; which
dramas paid, and which drew for a brief, blighting week ;
anecdotes of the actors of these palmy days of comedy,
and the Brough views on management, stage and other-
wise, and the art dramatique. Think on’t, Robert.
Dear Bulletin.— As as old Londoner who knows what
is what in theatrical matters, I may be permitted to
laugh at “R.B.’s” "disclosure” (in last “ Pov. Point”)
of “ the secret of the trashy character of some plays
which go out to Australia as ‘ great London successes.’ ’*
“ R. 8.,” evidently a new-chum in the big city, tells you
that “ the gilt-edged melodrama comes (to Australia)
from some theatre in a filthy suburb —Islington or Ham-
mersmith. A London triumph is thus often equivalent
to a wonderful success at Geelong.” Now the filthy
portions of Islington are situated within two miles of
London G.P.O. The suburbs of London, in a cockney
way of speaking, are populated mainly by people with
some pretensions to education. Nonsensical lurid melo-
drama doesn’t live on suburban patronage. It is written
for the delectation of the utterly uneducated classes in
London and the large provincial manufacturing towns,
especially the latter, because it is chiefly in the northern
English centres of population that toilers who cannot
read or write are able to earn fair wages. The average
muck produced in Australia as a “ famous London suc-
cess” is probably unknown to any part of the metropolis.
It never gets a show in the prosperous suburbs. It is the
drama of lamentable ignorance, as ignorance goes in
Britain, and the fact that it can nowadays obtain steady
patronage in representative Australian theatres, where it
could not have flourished 20 years ago, is something for
politicians to reflect upon. The audiences here are better
educated than the Britishers for whom these awful
dramas are built, but they seem to take the stuff quite as
seriously.—E.
Of all the so-called “ problem ” plays there is but one
that presents a real problem to the mind of a thoughtful
audience. Jones’s “The Liars” is the exception. Did
Jones intend Jessica to be a forgiveable or a hateful
character ? She doesn’t even affect innocence of the harm
she is doing to her infatuated lover, and her only moment
of remorse on the infatuated one’s account is when she
admits to him that she is a coward. Having fooled him
all she can by encouraging him to fool himself, she finally
settles down to a life of contentment and oyster suppers
with the husband whose jealousy she had delighted to
aggravate. A philosopher will say that Lady Jessica is
only one of a natural type of attractive women. God
made lots more like her. But then God makes tempting
poisonous berries and instinctive murderesses, whose
figures are irreproachable, and will-o’-the-wisps and lots
of other essentially hateful things.
Cobb and Co. have abandoned as a hope-
less case their mail-contracts in the dry interior of
Queensland, The feed-account, usually about
£6OOO, promises this year to be £30,000, and the
mail-contract (which was entered into before
Providence cursed this country with an extra
special drought on account of its iniquitous war-
policy) won’t nearly cover the outlay. The co.
states frankly that it had to stop because its over-
draft could go no further, and its horses couldn’t
run on credit and eat promissory notes. On
4000 miles of Q. roads the familiar form of
“ Cobb’s coach ” will be seen no longer, unless
the Federal Government comes to the rescue,
which it must do. Mails have stopped. Passenger
traffic has stopped. The carriage of food (many
people got their victuals sent them by parcel
post) has largely stopped. Over an immense area
of country no man can write to his best girl now ;
nobody can be dunned through the post; bagmen
who went out can’t get back ; and civilisation has
practically fallen asunder. The link that kept it
together was Cobb’s coaoh, and without Cobb
there is Chaos.
Kaiser William has foolishly given Chris-
tian Science the advertisement of a “ persecution’
by excluding several fashionable patrons of the
new faith from the German Court.
Waxellency Madden was 58 on Friday
(May 15) of this week. He is exactly a year
older than Lord Carrington, who has just
been worsted in his fight for the privilege of help-
ing to lackey the King at the Coronation cere-
mony. Henniker Heaton will be 54 to-morrow
{Sunday). By the way, British high officialdom
doesn’t know what to do with Henniker H. He
is one of the best-advertised men in the Empire,
and everyone knows his name and the good work
he has done in connection with the post-office.
The public in general consider that he ought to be
made an Earl or something, but the Colonial
Office isn’t taking any Lord Henniker Heaton !
“ Longshoreman ” to The Bulletin :
Feat of the Boveric men, 'who came 1500 miles in an
open boat for aid for their shipmates, knocks such per-
formances as Slocum’s Spray voyage into a cocked hat.
Chief-officer Hay man, and second officer M’Oarthy had
■no incentive of money, theirs was a matter of plain duty.
Neither had they months to prepare ; possibly 24 hours
was the period that elapsed between the decision being
come to and departure taken. Such gear as the average
•coaster carries (for until recently the Boveric was engaged
in the coal traffic) had to be made to serve. Imagine
being cooped up in a 24ft. open ship’s lifeboat for 26 days,
nary a sight of land until the last day, and only one
steamer sighted (which passed on her way unheeding).
For practically a whole month those two officers must
have taken watch and watch about with every nerve
strained. These, be it noted, are the men whom it is the
•custom to refer to as common sailors, members of a
badly-paid and hardly-worked calling. Had that boat
belonged to one of King Ned’s cruisers, the whole
wide world would ring with news of the feat, and
•decorations and rewards galore would shower on the
heads of the gallant four. But because they are
merchantmen none, save perhaps the committee of Lloyds,
will be found to recognise the worth and pluck of the
men who carried the job through. We make much fuss
and many presentations of fancy nick-nacks to departing
and returning contingenters, but the odds are that this
•crew won't reap much reward. Posturers take precedence
of heroes nowadays.
The decision of Victorian Premier Pea-
oock not to accept the resignation of his ill-
regulated team puts the finishing touch on a very
long-winded joke. Six months ago all Peacock’s
subordinates except Trenwith were reported to
have signed a joint resignation, so that if Peacock
felt it his duty to reconstruct the Ministry on a
smaller and more economical basis he could do so.
It appears, however, that the resignations were
not to take effect till May I—reason not stated.
So on May 1 Solicitor-General Wynne handed in
the long-forgotten paper, to the great indignation
of the resigning Ministers, who thought it had
heen lost. Peacock didn’t accept the resignation
at the time, because he didn’t receive it, and he
refuses to accept it now that he has'received it,
because it is a six-months’-old document and quite
mouldy and out-of-date. The whole business is as
mysterious as a man lending himself 6d. and then
hiding the 1.0. U. under a tub.

done that previously for 12 months—always went in the
omnibus—as walking caused me such pain and distress in
the chest. I always walk now, and never feel it, and I
am stronger than I have been for years. I thank my son
for his great kindness in sending the medicine, and am,
dear Sir,
“Yours very truly,
“M. MORTIMER.’
