A Woman’s Ethics.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Vennaker sat gloomily pondqring over
the new phase of life which had come to him. He
sighed as he glanced at the empty chair opposite,
and irritably kicked the footstool on the hearth-
rug-on which he had so often seen her feet rest-
ing when she was there—as, turning to the table,
he helped himself from a decanter, and then re-lit
his pipe. He glanced round the comfortable
room —everything was just the same, and yet
with what an oppressive sense of difference ! He
had fancied that in time he would get used to it,
but he had come to learn the futility of this hope
—to find that the house was haunted by her—
for wherever aforetime she had been, there was
she now. Even at this moment he imagined he
could see her sitting facing him, as she used to
when he read or talked to her. He remembered
how he would glance at her in odd moments, and
wonder what were the thoughts behind that
intent, absorbed face. He had often marvelled,
too, at her wonderful instinct and fore-
knowledge, and as a child’s cry reached his
ears from an adjoining room he specu-
lated whether some mysterious agency would
carry that to her, for he recalled how she would
rise hurriedly from her chair at sounds which
reached her, but not him (although he was quick
of hearing), and, after lifting a warning finger,
noiselessly move away— knowing how the child
had how she would find it, whether it
ailed.
A knock at the door, and the housekeeper
entered.
“The little one is still restless, sir—maybe shes
sickening for something. I seen one of my own,
when my pore ’usband was ”
“ I’ll call at the doctor’s on my way to town in
the morning, and get him to come and see her,”
abruptly interposed Vennaker. . . “No —nothing
more, thanks. Good night, Mrs. Evly.”
“ May as well turn in, too, I suppose,’’ he
muttered, turning out the lamp. Walking quietly
to the bedroom, he bent down and examined
the face of the child as it lay sleeping, restlessly,
in its cot. He touched the small hand, and kissed
the half-opened mouth, and noticed how hot and
dry they were. The night was warm and oppres-
sive, and he opened wide the French window
leading to the verandah and garden, and smiled as
he remembered how, at one time, he had to give
up this habit because of the nervous terror of one
who would, perchance, suddenly clasp him in the
night and, tremblingly, bid him to listen !
And presently some choking sense of tender-
ness came to him, as he lay musing in bed, watch-
ing the shaft from the moon which now struck
across the room, and scenting the perfume from
the flower-heaped garden—with its single gum
shading the bench beneath. God! if these
thoughts would not come, then parting were
easier! Why was it that all remembrance of her
defects had faded—the numberless traits which
used to annoy him, because, to his man’s mind,
they seemed so small, and lacking in justice—
and that his memory was flooded only with the
hundred qualities he had loved in her —the very
weakness of some of which had made them the
more captivating, because of their essential femi-
ninity, and the need they imposed on her to seek
his strength and protection !
He reached an arm towards the pillow near his
own, and laughed quietly hG folly—but per-
mitted it to remain ther . # The action did not
strike him as peculiar, for he was only just con-
scious of having performed it, notwithstanding
the singular circumstance that some vague sense
of expectation lay half-revealed in his brain. He
was drowsy now, and mere speculation had
ceased, and it was because his senses were dulled
that he did not notice the moonray across the
room was now broken and shimmering, and that
some strange perfume was present. But a vague
consciousness came to him in his sleep that his
arm was becoming cramped, and, as he instinc-
tively moved it, that something had rested upon
it which was now removed.
Suddenly a sound startled him—the hour of one,
struck by the clock on the mantel-piece. He
opened his eyes, and in a moment was awake, with
every faculty clear, alert, tense. Prompted by
some obscure suggestion, he noiselessly raised his
head, and peered across the room—instinctively
towards the child’s cot: and there he saw her !
He knew it was she in a moment; the well-known
lavender odour from her dress reached him now,
and there was some unaccustomed fragrance on
his pyjama sleeve.
He looked with distended eyes, and a sense of
the overwhelming wonder of it! He heard her
kisses, and the unknowing, responsive murmurs of
the little sleeper, the slight clink of the spoon
against the glass as she moistened its lips, and a
suppressed, choking sob, as, holding the child’s
hands in her own—so white as the moon caught
them, and made the rings on her fingers to glisten
—she bent over it, and peered hungrily into the
flushed face. Then she lifted its head, reversed
the pillow, adjusted afresh the clothes of the cot,
and commenced to move quietly away.
As Vennaker lay back and closed his eyes, he
felt her presence approaching him, and his heart
beat. A longing to caress her once more took
hold of him, and made his blood course turbu-
lently through his veins. For a moment he was
curbed by the reflection of the folly of undoing all
that had been done—with such bitter heartache
for both of them—but he realised the weakness
that was upon him. He fancied that she would
steal silently over to him, that he would feel her
bending over his face, the slight current of her
breath, perhaps the touch of her lips—and then
there rushed suddenly across his mind the memory
of moments of passion they had spent together.
Well, let it come ! Let everything else go ! Gould
he not forget the bad past, and live only in that
which now haunted him, and in the new future
they would make together ?
. . . And a sense of keen disappointment and
defeat came upon him when he realised that she
had gone ! He rose hastily, and looked—sprang
to the ground, and gained the verandah—but all
was still. A sickening foreboding, too, struck
him chill—she didn’t wish a renewal of his love ;
had grown indifferent to him through the means
he had taken —had, perhaps, formed another and
more absorbing passion. He did not deny to him-
self the reasonableness of this, but it only served
now to awaken a strange sense of rivalry and
opposition, in which her value as an object to be
acquired became suddenly enhanced. He reflected
a few moments on this new phase of the subject,
and then smiled at the plan which occurred to
him ; and with a pleasing sense that it would be
successful, he at length found slumber.
Mrs. Evly received with a few sniffs and tosses
»f the head the orders which Vennaker gave her
in the morning.
“This is Mrs. Vennaker’s day of calling to see
the child,” he said. “ When she comes, tell her I
shall be absent till ten o’clock to-morrow evening,
It is just possible she may wish to remain and
nurse Minnie; if she does, allow her to do so, and
show her all the attention you can. If Minnie
should become worse, send to me at once at this
address ” —handing to her a slip of paper, with the
name of an hotel written on it.
* * * • .
It was with a new sense of trepidation that
Vennaker looked at his watch, as he reached his
house on the following afternoon. Five o’clock !
He noted with a little thrill, when he let himself
in, that a sunshade stood in the hall—and then lie
heard her voice in the bedroom. Hanging up his
hat, and placing his walking-stick in the stand, he
strode quietly to the kitchen, and questioned Mrs.
Evly with a look.
“ She’s come round wonderful, sir, so I didn’t
send for you. Mrs. Vennaker’s not gone yet,”
she said, half-apologetically; “we didn’t expect
you till ten.”
“ Quite right,” he answered. And then, with
assumed indifference, “Kindly give Mrs.. Ven-
naker my compliments, and ask her if she
will step into the drawing-room. I will go there
and await her.”
Vennaker was nervous, and only partially ob-
served the expression of surprise and apprehension
that came over Mrs. Evly’s face as she departed.
But he could not help noticing, as he entered the
room he so rarely visited now, that it had received
some new touches —flowers in the vases, some
ferns freshly watered, the music strewn about,
and the lid of the piano raised. He examined tli9
volume standing upon it—it was open at Bee-
thoven’s “Moonlight” sonata, a favorite of his.
He moved hastily away, and his heart beat
afresh as he heard a footstep in the hall, and in a
moment she stood before him. He saw the flush
on her cheek ; watched it fade into pallor ; and
noticed her manifest agitation as they faced and
looked at each other—constrained, uneasy, more
than strangers. She rested one hand on a chair-
back, and with the other crushed her gloves. A
sudden sense of the grief between them smote his
heart. He noticed too, that she wore her hat.
“You wish to see me, I understand,” she said,
faintly.
“Won’t you sit down?” he inquired, politely
placing a chair for her, and closing the door.
“Thank you,” she said, coldly, seating herself.
“I didn’t expect—l thought I should have left
before you ”
Vennaker slowly drew a chair, and sat facing
her. The formality of the thing was chilling
him.
“I returned—unexpectedly—earlier than I had
anticipated. . . I thought you would like to stay
with Minnie; I went away on purpose, so that
you could.”
“I am grateful,” she remarked, distantly. “ I
should not, of course, have remained otherwise.
Will you tell me why you sent for me ? I was
about to leave, and ”
Vennaker was fighting for time. He could not
marshal his thoughts, and he noticed that she was
becoming less perturbed than he.
“I saw you when you were here the night
before last,” he observed, with forced cheerful-
ness, “when you naturally expected to find me
asleep. It seemed a little like old times again ! ”
She flushed to her temples. “I—l felt uneasy
about the child ! I wouldn’t else have encroached
on my—time allowance —for seeing her,’ she
added, bitterly. “As for old times, pray don’t
refer to them —it is so unnecessary,” commencing
to draw on her gloves.
Vennaker colored slightly, as he said : “It
had not occurred to me that you were entirely
indifferent to them. It may perhaps, gratify you
if I admit that lam not. Amy ! I haven’t ceased
to love you, and I don’t like to see the child with-
out her mother. I know I divorced you—well,
you gave me sufficient cause, didn’t you ? Still,
I'm willing to forget that, and to re-marry you,
if you’ll return to me. That will establish you,
and .”
She rose, calmly, from her chair. “To receive
your forgiveness and pity ? ” she inquired, with a
little smile.
“Forgiveness—yes !” said Vennaker, earnestly,
standing before her—“ we’ll put pity on one side —
you wouldn’t have that, I know. But will it not
assuage any bitterness you may feel when I tell
you that no other woman, even now, could be the
same to me? I can’t forget the past —the old,
dear past —and I want to live it again ! I—l
didn’t think you could altogether cease to care for
me. Amy ! ” —and he attempted to take her
hand.
She drew back, quickly, and looked at him
wonderingly, and with pity. “Perhaps I
haven’t,” she said, sadly, “but do you not see
chat it is because of the happy past that there can
be no future for ns now ? ”
“Do you think I would ever upbraid you, if I
took you back?” exclaimed Vennaker, eagerly,
looking at her with the old admiration.
She shook her head, wistfully. “ No,” she said,
quietly. “ But you could not forget; and, even if
you did, I should fancy you thought of nothing
else. But the difficulty is my own —l could never
forget, or forgive myself! Don’t you see,” she
added, with strange pathos, “ that I’ve ruined my
own self-respect ? ”
Vennaker stood looking at her, and gloomily
reflecting on an aspect of the matter that had not
before occurred to him.
“Look here, Amy,” he said, slowly, “it was all
my own fault. I ought to have taken you to
England with me. I had no right to leave you
for six months, and think you were stronger than
I was myself. And supposing I had sinned
against you, as well as you against me, that would
make your mind easier about yourself, wouldn’t
it ? ”
“No ! ” she said, wearily, moving to the door.
“Wait!” he said, quickly; “it would! It
surely would ! —if I confess to you, Amy, I was—
although I loved you—unfaithful to you, in inten-
tion, before I left you, and that’s why I didn’t
take you ; and when I was in London. . . . Yes !
. . . Amy ! I ask you to forgive me ! ”
She drew herself up proudly, her hand on the
door. “ That makes it quite impossible we could
ever come together again,” she exclaimed, her
eyes flashing. “Even if I could forget my own
stain, I should despise you now. Why, you must
be a brute ! —and I—l was only weak, and yield-
ing. Did you not, even at parting, tell me of
your strength of character, and repeat your vows
of constancy ? ” And, with a contemptuous
glance, she opened the door and passed into the
hall.
“ But we are even now, aren’t we?” cried Ven-
naker, anxiously striding after her.
She broke into a peal of rippling laughter. . .
“Even? Yes, I suppose so, from your point o<.
view. But have you hated and despised yourself
for what you have done ? ” she exclaimed, turning
on him scornfully.
“No! ” he gasped—“ that is, not so far !’’
“I suppose not,” she said, scornfully, taking up
her sunshade; “and therein lies the difference
between us. But you revenged your—honor—
on me, you know,” she added, with satirical
soothing in her tone.
And as Vennaker stood, .pondering, the house
door closed smartly, and she was gone.

The Great City.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Not Whitman—but the reality.)
The place where the Great City stands is not,
camarados, a place of admirable municipal
conveniences, or a place of peerless, primal
beauty ;
It is not a place of lofty dwellings, cleanly
appliances, and a passionate devotion to the
morning bath-tub ;
It is not, amigos, by any means, a centre of super-
nal culture ;
Neither is it a place where a freeborn populace is
deeply addicted to the use of soap and water.
The place where a great city stands is mostly a
conglomeration of cursed, unspeakable odors ;
It is a curious compound of fat aldermen, oily
politicians, casual coppers, and fragrant
Italian fish-hawkers ;
It is a place where the microbes wrestle for a living
in the fragrant, all-congenial gutter,
And where the Bacillus begets its many-million
brood on the trailing skirts of passing beauty.
The place where the Great City stands is not pre-
cisely a cradle for every shining virtue ;
The name of alderman is not synonymous—(not
always ) with honest administration ;
The fame of city-contracts is foul as the fame of
dismal Phlegethon,
And the odor of the city jobs rises thick, palpable,
perilous and pestful—a blight on the face of
common decency.
The place where the Great City stands is redolent
of stale beer, bad tobacco and the putrid
breath that pervades the dens of the slum
landlord ;
It is the nest of Phthisis ; the haunt of Typhoid ;
the resort of things unclean, of creeping pests
and Plague Bubonic;
It i 3 beloved of guttersnipes, sewer-rats, human
degenerates, and grinding rack-renters ;
It spits a cloud of smoke and grime to foul the
fair face of the kindly Heaven ;
It is mostly a modern Sodom—full ripe for a brim-
stone Deluge.
Five minutes in a century its fat Rulers stir up
various stinks and prate awhile of sanitation ;
They bray the poor in a mortar—and bray them-
selves in the Senate—
A race of queer, insensate, carnivorous Asses !
The Place where the Great City stands !—there is
no Great City, camarados !
This hideous jumble of garish beer-shops, gaping
gutters, busy bagnios, crowded gaols, dwarfish
men, and pallid women,
This melange of blaring brick and staring stucco
—one stately hall and a thousand squalid
hovels,
This steaming street-where scowling Greed and
prowling Need are ever present side by side,
Where the physical Impotent rubs, cheek by jowl,
with the mental Imbecile—
’Tis naught, I say, but a gaping asylum for
cripples, an hospital fraught with a myriad
diseases,
A home for the Seven Deadly Sins and the Seven
Cast-out Devils,—a Hell-broth brewed of
seventy deadly stenches—
But a Great City—never—never— never ! !
P. LUFTIG.
Why We Went.
A very great minority in Australia (writes Professor
Wood, of Sydney University, in Manchester Guardian),
consisting chiefly of Irish, the Labor Party, shearers and
rouseahouts, are opposed to the war. Racial unity and a
profundity of ignorance of the real causes of the war are,
the professor declares, the chief reasons of Australia’s
support. He also states that Che colonies consider their
help a good investment, and a good advertisement. A
few volunteers, he continues, are inspired by self-sacrifice,
but most have a sheer love of adventure and a desire to
escape from the monotony of bush life.— Cable.
Mr. Wood’s utterances have been most unguarded .
a very serious libel on a community who have proved
their loyalty and devotion to the Empire. . . . The very
small minority should subordinate their opinion to that
of the majority.— N.S. W. State Premier See.
The majority has exercised its power in such a
tyrannical way that many of the minority dare not ex-
press its views.— Prof. Wood.
It is time for notice to be taken of the Professor’s
action.—S.M. Herald.
It was a burst of truest loyalty, a mighty impulse that
stirred us, a love of country, Queen and Empire.— A
volunteer.
* * * *
There’s been a lot of blather about Aus-
tralians who went to the war, and I think it’s
about “up to me” to chip in. I express the
sentiments of mates, acquaintances, and others
who left the North-west; and I know of cookies
and selectors’ sons in other parts who were actuated
by the same impulses. While admitting that
many went from ideas of “racial unity,” well-
founded or mistaken ; through disinterested
patriotism for the sake of the “Imperialism”
ingrained into the very fibre of their beings during
childhood in public schools, conducted by Britain-
born teachers; while others went, more or
less, through compulsion—sacrificed by “loyal”
parents, employers, and so on, clingingto the silken
bonds that are myths to them ; from ignorance
—from a hundred causes ; let me say that we had
very interested personal reasons for going,
* • * *
We weren’t stirred by any bursts of “loyalty,”
or the love of Queen or Empire. We were in-
different enough about those things before ; now
we’re utterly sick of the names. We may be loyal
enough : If Australia wanted Independence to-
morrow we’d fall over one another in our eagerness
to fight for her. That’s where our Patriotism
comes in. Yet we fought in a war that didn’t
personally concern us ; we helped to crush a game
little people that dared to fight for what we our-
selves would fight for ! The Boer was a stranger;
we had only a vague idea what he was being
kicked for ; but we loyally volunteered to go and
jump on him all the same. The good old Empire
called, we showed our loyalty . . . Love
Queen . . . Silken Bonds—Bah ! This is why we
yent.
> . lc J' ure a lonel y station in the drought-stricken
back-blocks; the same monotonous round of work
from year’s end to year’s end ; burning suns,
thirst, dust, flies; the never-gratified craving for
the sight and voice of a woman ; the only fresh
face that of a passing swagman ; seeing our fellows
commit suicide in despair (two from a station I
worked on near Tibooburra hanged themselves in
one year); or become eccentric, to be known as
Hatty Mick, Barmy Bill and Looney Jim. What
would we not do to escape such a hell ?
* * * *
As station-hands we get at the best a pound a
week, the roughest of tucker, and the meanest of
hovels to sleep in ; but plenty of work—seven
days a week of it. Out boundary-riding we are
lucky to get 15 bob a week and rations—Blb. of
cheap flour, 10 of stale mutton, 2 of black sugar,
and a £lb. of post-and-rail tea. To satisfy our
craving for vegetables we search the creek banks
and billabongs for pig-weed. The tent is pitched
m a clump of mulga, where a new face is seldom,
if ever, seen; where the only sounds come from
crows and dying sheep ; where we sit at night
staring into the fire, or talking to Fancies in the
smoke-wreaths ; imagining adventures, longing
for love and life, dreaming of women ; or we lie
on a blanket spread on the ground, or in a bag-
bunk, reading “Deadwood Dicks” and stale
weekly papers by a slush lamp, till we are worked
into a frenzy, sharing the hero’s wondrous fights
and adventures, yearning to be—well, anything to
kill the loneliness that damns existence.
* * * ft
Day by day we ride round the same old fences,
dodder about after perishing sheep, trying to keep
old ewes from dying, and seeing them die in spite
of everything. What more hopeless and heart-
breaking job could a man tackle? The stubborn
old things hang on the fences, poke into the
furthest corners, miles away from the tank, and
you start in the morning to drive them to water,
and by pushing, whipping, shoving and kicking
through heat, flies, everlasting dust, and periodic
dust-storms, you get half of them to the tank by
sundown—to see them bog, and spend half the
night pulling them out.
* » * *
You repeat this every day for months, leaving a
few behind each trip, and strewing your track
with lambs that you have knocked on the head.
You shout and yell till you are hoarse, and feel as
if you had strangled your windpipe and strained
your lungs; you curse all things woolly; the
sweat pours from you in streams, and your
head feels as though a little hell had got inside of
it. Elies buzz round you in millions, and when
the wind blows you are blinded and choked with
dust. Your dog is done-in with the heat long
before noon, and merely dodges "from shade to
shade, a long way behind ; your water-bag runs
dry, and by-and-bye you scrape the mucous coat-
ing off your lips to prevent the dust banking up,
and damming up the entrance to your innards for
ever. To cap all, when you start for “home,”
your horse is exhausted, and you have either to
drag him to the station, or let him go, and carry
your saddle on your back.
* • • *
In the midst of this hell-life came the call for
volunteers. From boundary-fence to boundary-
fence the news went, and we cheered for the
mother country, the good old Empire. Sitting
before our lonely campfires, with the memory of
first contingents, what glories that trip promised,
what temptations it held ! A smart uniform,
nothing to do but carry a gun, a trip to Sydney,
crowds hurrahing, bands, flags, girls to hug and
kiss—sweet, dainty girls ! —a long, delightful sea-
trip, new sights, new countries, more crowds,
bands, more lovely women, life-stirring adven-
tures on the veldt, promotion, a thousand-to-one
show of being shot, the chance of snavelling a good
section of Boer land, and Jive bob a day all the
time! Do you ask us why we went? "Do you
wonder that we did not consider the pros and cons,
but whooped with the Empire and the daily
papers, and rushed for places ?
• * * *
We went —and were sickened with the sights of
suffering women and dying children, of hardships
and wide-spread misery. We came back, a lot of
us, and applied for a promised Government billet;
looked for any kind of billet. Nothing for it but
to return to boundary-riding, or respond to the
Empire’s call again. The roads were waterless
and barren ; so we couldn’t ride back; we must
shoulder bluey, and chance perishing on the way;
also chance getting a job. On the other hand,
the Empire offered a sure five bob a day (riches !),
clothes, tucker, horses, arms, a pleasant trip.
* * * *
Well, for my personal part, the offers went beg-
ging ; there’s no place like Australia. But many
whom I knew, after battling around till their
money was done, and dreading more than ever the
monotony of bush life, after they had once tasted
of cities and glory and girls, and the spirit of
adventure was fired, showed “their loyalty to
the dear old Empire ” once more. Had America,
or any other country, perhaps, called for volun-
teers under the same conditions, they would have
answered just as readily. As before stated, I
speak for myself, and for those with whom I am
personally intimate.
* * * *
Many crossed the water from pure love of
adventure ; some were really stirred by a burst of
loyalty and love of the Empire. But take away the
change from the dreariness of out-back, leaving
naked loyalty on her merits, and Australia’s con-
tribution would have been small. It would not
have dragged us from our back-block runs. But
the change, the relief from the dead monotony of
bush life, was there —and that’s why most of us
went.
A BUSHMAN.
Writes “Neptune ” :
Australian dairy-farmers will have to get a “ two-bit ”
move on if they want to keep their claws on London
butter market. The latest competitor for this trade is
Siberia, containing vast areas of dairying and arable land,
which the Russian Govt., with an eye to future trade-
prospects, is practically giving away to intending emi-
grants. Loans are also made on easy terms to settlers, to
enable them to get machinery, Stock, and farming
requisites. John Foster Fraser, who recently described
his trans-Siberian railway tour, states that every Eastern-
bound train from European Russia is crammed with
emigrants, and asserts that Siberia, with its ever-certain
rainfall, is destined to become the srreat wheat-producing
country of the world. The Yankees have already
recognised this fact, for their agricultural machinery
drummers are to be met with in every town in Siberia.
With an improved train-service, journey from Vladivo-
stok, on the East, to Riga, the port of shipment in the
West, will be made in about 17 days ; thence to London,
another four days, giving the Siberian farmer a transit
period of 21 days as compared with our 50-day passages.
The Russian Govt, also, it is stated, contemplates the
establishment of dairy schools and laboratories in various
districts, provision of cool-storage at principal railway
centres, quick train-service from Siberia to the Baltic sea-
board, and quick steamer-servica from Riga to London.
These items would form a good working programme for
our own agricultural dept*., whose time seems mostly
spent in persuading each other’s experts to swap States.
